The First Three Views Lead to Compulsion
Argues that the first three views all entail jabr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first three views on human agency all entail compulsion (jabr).

Core Idea
The first three views on human agency all ultimately lead to jabr.

In this passage, the author critiques three earlier views on how human actions occur, showing that each view denies the slave true independence. The central claim is that all three reduce to compulsion (jabr). This argument exposes a key inconsistency in the theological positions discussed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: the author states the conclusion first, then provides the reasoning by enumerating the three views.
The passage begins with 'والثالث' indicating this is a third point in a larger discussion.
2
Identify the three views
The passage lists three ways the slave's act might be dependent: (1) on God's assistance, (2) on power and motive together, (3) as a necessary consequence when power and motive occur. Identify each one in the text.
Each view is introduced with 'كان' or 'إذا كان'.
3
Understand why they entail jabr
In each case, the slave is not fully independent (mustaqillan). The act is conditioned on external factors, so it cannot be truly voluntary. The author concludes that compulsion (jabr) necessarily follows.
This shows that even views that seem moderate (like Ash'ari kasb) reduce to jabr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : إن الأقوال الثلاثة الأول مشتركة في القول بالجبر . لأن بتقدير صحة كل واحد من [ تلك ] الأقوال الثلاثة ، لا يكون العبد مستقلاً بالإيجاد . فإن صدور الفعل عن العبد إذا كان موقوفاً على إعانة الله ، أو كان موقوفاً على حصول القدرة مع الداعي ، أو كان بحال متى حصلت القدرة مع الداعية وجب الفعل . ومتى لم يحصلا ، أو لم يحصل واحد منهما امتنع وقوع الفعل فكان الجبر لازماً
And the third: that the first three views share in saying compulsion. Because, given the correctness of each one of those three views, the slave is not independent in origination. For the occurrence of the act from the slave, if it is dependent on God's assistance, or dependent on the occurrence of power along with the motive, or is in a state such that when power and motive occur the act becomes necessary, and when they do not occur or one of them does not occur, the occurrence of the act is prevented, then compulsion is entailed.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a third point related to the Mu'tazila's internal discussions, but it functions as a critique of the three preceding views (presumably those of the philosophers, Ash'aris, and another). The author argues that all three views share the characteristic of leading to jabr (compulsion). The reasoning is that under any of those views, the slave is not fully independent in originating his actions. The three views are: (1) the act depends on God's assistance (i'ana), (2) the act depends on the simultaneous presence of power and motive, and (3) when power and motive are present, the act becomes necessary, and when either is absent, the act is prevented. In each case, the slave's role is not fully voluntary, and thus the result is jabr. The author concludes that compulsion necessarily follows from these positions.

استقلال
إعانة
قدرة
داعي
وجوب
امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first three views on human agency are entirely distinct from jabr and preserve human freedom.
Correction: The passage shows that all three views ultimately reduce to compulsion because the slave's action is dependent on external conditions, denying true independence.

Checkpoint
What do the first three views on human agency all entail according to this passage?
A) Human freedom
B) Compulsion (jabr)
C) Moral responsibility
D) Divine will

Key Takeaway
The first three views on human agency all lead to jabr because the slave is not independent in origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own actions, do you ever feel that they are not truly yours—that circumstances or divine decree forced them? How does the author's claim that such thinking leads to jabr challenge you to take full responsibility before Allah? What concrete step can you take today to own your choices more fully?
?
If you were to examine your own beliefs about human agency, which of the three views (dependence on God's help, power+motive necessary, or power+motive contingent) do you lean toward? Are you willing to accept that this view implies jabr, and if so, how does that affect your understanding of your accountability for sins and rewards for good deeds? What would you need to change in your worldview to avoid this implication?

The First Profound Research: The Mu'tazili Dilemma on Necessity of Action
Introduces the first profound research on the Mu'tazili stance on necessity of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma that al-Razi presents for the Mu'tazili position: if the act is necessary given power and motive, it leads to jabr; if not necessary, another difficulty arises.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili is caught between jabr and an alternative problematic consequence.

This passage introduces a series of profound investigations into the exact point of dispute between the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila. The first investigation presents a dilemma that challenges the coherence of the Mu'tazili claim of human independence. By examining this dilemma, we see how theological arguments often proceed by reducing the opponent's position to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the two contrasting options presented.
2
Understand the context
This passage follows a detailed enumeration of possible schools on the issue of human action. The author now delves into deeper investigations to pinpoint the exact point of dispute.
3
Identify the dilemma's two horns
The Mu'tazili must choose: either the act is necessary given power and motive, or it is not. Each option leads to a problematic conclusion.
4
Analyze the first horn
If the act is necessary, then motives must ultimately come from God (due to impossibility of infinite regress or circularity), leading to compulsion (jabr). The slave loses independence.
This undermines the Mu'tazili stance entirely.
5
Consider the incomplete second horn
The passage cuts off as the author begins to argue the second possibility. Reflect on what might follow: if the act is not necessary, perhaps it becomes contingent or relies on something else.
The argument is not fully presented in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا هو القول في تفصيل المذاهب الممكنة في هذه المسألة . واعلم : أن ههنا أبحاثاً غامضة في تعيين محل النزاع، لابد من التنبيه عليها . وهي من وجوه : فالبحث الأول : إن [ المعتزلي ] إما أن [ يقول ] : إن حصول الفعل عقيب مجموع القدرة والداعي : واجب . أو يقول : إنه غير واجب . فإن قال : إنه واجب . فقد بطل الاعتزال بالكلية . لأن تلك الدواعي لا يمكن أن يستند كل واحد منها إلى داعية أخرى ، لامتناع التسلسل والدور . بل لا بد من انتهائها إلى داعية تحصل بفعل الله تعالى . وإذا كان كذلك ، فنقول : عند حصول القدرة والداعية ، لما كان الفعل واجباً ، وعند فقدانها أحدهما لما كان الفعل ممتنعاً : فحينئذ يلزم الجير ، ولم يحصل للعبد استقلال ، لا بالفعل ولا بالترك في شيء . وأما إن قال المعتزلي : إن حصول الفعل عند حصول القدرة مع الداعي : غير واجب . فنقول : فعلى هذا
So this is the statement concerning the detailing of the possible schools on this issue. And know that here there are profound investigations for determining the point of dispute, which must be pointed out. And they are from aspects: the first research: that the Mu'tazili either says that the occurrence of the act consequent upon the combination of power and motive is necessary, or he says that it is not necessary. If he says it is necessary, then Mu'tazilism is invalidated entirely. Because those motives cannot each be based on another motive, due to the impossibility of infinite regress and circularity. Rather they must terminate at a motive that occurs by God's act. And if it is so, we say: when power and motive occur, since the act is necessary, and when one of them is absent, since the act is impossible, then compulsion necessarily follows, and the slave does not obtain independence, neither in act nor in abstention in anything. But if the Mu'tazili says that the occurrence of the act upon the occurrence of power with the motive is not necessary, we say: then upon this...
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage serves as a conclusion to the previous enumeration of doctrines and then introduces a series of 'profound investigations' (abhath ghamidah) aimed at precisely locating the point of dispute. The first investigation explores a dilemma for the Mu'tazili position. The Mu'tazili is given two options: either he affirms that the occurrence of the act following the conjunction of power and motive is necessary, or he denies that it is necessary. If he affirms necessity, then Mu'tazilism collapses, because the motives must ultimately trace back to a motive placed by God (due to the impossibility of infinite regress or circularity). This implies that when power and motive are present, the act is compelled, and the slave has no independence in either action or omission, which is jabr. If the Mu'tazili denies necessity, then the author begins a counter-argument that is cut off at the end of the page: 'we say: then upon this...' The passage ends without completing the second horn, but the argument structure is clear: the Mu'tazili is forced into a dilemma between jabr and another problematic consequence.

أبحاث غامضة
محل النزاع
داعي
تسلسل
دور
جبر
استقلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazili position on human free will is logically consistent and avoids jabr.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that if the Mu'tazili affirms necessity of act given power and motive, it leads to jabr because motives must ultimately be from God. If he denies necessity, another problem arises, as the passage begins to show.

Checkpoint
According to the first research, what happens if the Mu'tazili says the act is necessary given power and motive?
A) Mu'tazilism is strengthened
B) Mu'tazilism is invalidated and jabr follows
C) The slave gains full independence
D) The motives become infinite

Key Takeaway
The first research shows that claiming necessity of action given power and motive leads to compulsion, undermining the Mu'tazili doctrine of free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to do good, do you catch yourself thinking 'I just couldn't help it'? How does this lesson expose that excuse as a form of jabr that denies your responsibility? What is one specific sin you have justified this way, and how will you take full ownership of it today?
?
The author argues that your deepest motives ultimately come from Allah. When you feel a strong desire to disobey, do you ever blame God for giving you that desire? How does this lesson demand that you instead examine your own heart and repent?
?
The Mu'tazili dilemma forces an honest choice. Do you truly believe that you are independent of Allah in any of your actions? Name one area of your life where you act as if you are self-sufficient, and commit to acknowledging His control over it right now.

The Dilemma of Possibility: Compulsion or Denial of the Creator
Razi argues that the Mu'tazila view on possibility is logically inconsistent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that Razi poses against the Mu'tazila position on possibility and causality.

Core Idea
Possibility either demands a cause or it doesn't — there is no coherent middle ground.

Razi presents a powerful logical argument against the Mu'tazilite attempt to steer a middle path between compulsion and absolute free will. He shows that their view leads to contradiction, forcing us to choose between two stark alternatives. This lesson unpacks the dilemma and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand key terms
Familiarize yourself with the terms: possibility (imkan), agent cause (mu'aththir), and preponderator (murajjih). These are central to the argument.
Possibility here means contingency: a thing whose existence and non-existence are equally possible.
2
Follow the two horns of the dilemma
Razi lays out two conditionals: if possibility requires a cause, then every possible needs a preponderator leading to jabr; if it does not require cause, then one cannot infer a Creator from possibility.
Notice that both horns lead to extreme positions.
3
See why the Mu'tazila view is contradictory
The Mu'tazila claim that possibility necessitates a cause in some cases but not others. Razi calls this inconsistent and false.
The contradiction lies in applying the same rule differently without justification.
4
Examine the objection and response
An opponent might object that jabr leads to a necessitated Creator, but Razi argues that denying a Creator altogether is far worse.
Razi promises a future distinction between the witnessed and unseen to clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان الإمكان محوجاً إلى المؤثر، لزم احتياج كل ممكن إلى المرجح. وعلى هذا، يكون رجحان الفاعلية على التاركية، لا بد وأن يكون معللاً بعلة موجبة. وحينئذ يلزم الجبر. وأما إن كان الإمكان غير محوج إلى المرجح، فعند ذلك لا خفاء أن يستدل بالإمكان على المؤثر. وحينئذ [يكرم] نفي الصانع بالكلية. سواء كان موجباً أو مختاراً. فثبت أن [القول] الحق: إما القول باحتياج جميع الممكنات إلى المؤثر، وحينئذ يلزم الجبر. أو القول باستغناء جميع الممكنات عن المؤثر، وحينئذ يلزم نفي المؤثرات أصلاً. فأما القول بأن الإمكان محوج إلى المؤثر في موضع دون موضع، كما هو قول المعتزلة: فهو قول متناقض باطل. فثبت: أنه إن لزم على القول بالجبر: إثبات أن مؤثر العالم موجب بالذات، لا فاعل بالاختيار، لزم على القول بالقدر أن يكون رجحاناً وجود العالم على عدمه لا لمؤثر أصلاً - والأول - وإن كان قبيحاً ـ فلا شك أن هذا الأخير أقبح منه، وأفحش بكثير على أنا سنذكر الفرق اليقيني بين الشاهد والغائب إن شاء الله تعالى.
For if possibility (al-imkan) necessitates the agent cause (al-mu'aththir), then every possible thing requires the preponderant (al-murajjih). And accordingly, the preponderance of agency over abandonment must be caused by a necessitating cause. Then compulsion (al-jabr) follows. But if possibility does not necessitate the preponderant, then it is not hidden that one would use possibility to infer the agent cause. And then [يكرم] denial of the Maker entirely, whether He is necessitating or choosing. So it is established that the correct [view] is either the statement that all possible things need the agent cause, then compulsion follows; or the statement that all possible things are independent of the agent cause, then the denial of all agents follows. As for the statement that possibility necessitates the agent in some cases and not in others, as is the view of the Mu'tazila, it is a contradictory false statement. So it is established that if the claim that the agent of the world is necessitating in essence, not a chooser, follows from the view of compulsion, then from the view of power (al-qadr) it follows that the preponderance of the world's existence over its non-existence is from no agent at all — the first, though ugly, yet this last is uglier and far more indecent. Moreover, we will mention the certain distinction between the witnessed and the unseen, God willing.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument against the Mu'tazilite position on free will. The author begins by stating a conditional: if possibility (al-imkan) necessarily requires an agent cause (mu'aththir), then every contingent thing needs a preponderant (murajjih) to bring it into existence. This leads to compulsion (jabr) because the preponderance of action over inaction must be caused by a necessitating cause. Alternatively, if possibility does not require a preponderant, then one cannot infer the existence of a Creator from possibility, leading to outright denial of the Maker. The author then presents the two horns of the dilemma: either all possible things need a cause (jabr) or all are independent (denial of the Creator). The Mu'tazilite middle ground — that possibility requires a cause in some cases but not others — is dismissed as contradictory. Finally, the author addresses an objection: the compulsion view leads to the Creator being necessitated (not a free agent), but the alternative (denial of a Creator) is far worse. He promises to later explain the certain distinction between the witnessed (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib).

Key Terms
إمكان (imkan)
مؤثر (mu'aththir)
مرجح (murajjih)
جبر (jabr)
قدر (qadr)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There is a coherent middle ground between jabr and denial of Creator.
Correction: The Mu'tazila attempt is logically contradictory because it treats identical cases differently.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows from the Mu'tazila claim that possibility necessitates a cause in some cases but not in others?
A) It leads to compulsion (jabr) in all cases
B) It is a contradictory and false statement
C) It correctly balances compulsion and free will
D) It proves the necessity of a Creator

Key Takeaway
Possibility must uniformly either require a cause or not; any inconsistent application leads to logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult choice, do you ever feel that your decision was predetermined? How does Razi's argument challenge you to think consistently about your own agency?
?
Razi says the Mu'tazila position is contradictory. In your own life, where do you hold contradictory beliefs about your control over events? What would it cost you to clarify your position?
?
The passage threatens both pure free will and pure determinism as ugly. Which horn are you more tempted toward, and why? What is the Qur'anic path between them?

The Core Thesis: Free Will Problem as Proof of the Creator
Razi presents the argument that the jabr/qadr issue is identical to proving the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the free will issue is identical to proving the existence of the Creator, and how the premise of possibility needing a cause leads to either jabr or atheism.

Core Idea
The free will debate is inseparable from proving God's existence.

Razi opens this investigation by citing many verifiers who hold that the problem of jabr (compulsion) and qadr (free will) is not a standalone issue. Instead, it directly parallels the proof of the Creator. The core argument hinges on the principle that a contingent thing (mumkin) requires an external cause (mu'aththir) and a preponderant (murajjih). Let's trace how this leads to a stark choice: either jabr or denial of the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read the first sentence where Razi cites the verifiers' thesis. Note that the issue of jabr and qadr is said to be identical to proving the Maker.
This is a strong claim that frames the entire argument.
2
Understand the main premise
Identify the premise that possibility (imkan) necessitates a cause (mu'aththir) and a preponderant (murajjih). This is the key to the argument.
This premise is borrowed from kalam cosmology.
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
Trace the 'if-then' structure: if the premise is valid, then all possible things need a cause. Then examine ability (qudra): if not fit for leaving, jabr follows; if fit for leaving, a preponderant is needed, also leading to jabr.
Razi shows that either way, jabr is necessary.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is a disjunction: either jabr or denial of the Creator. Understand that rejecting the premise makes proof of God impossible.
This is a powerful dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثالث: إن كثيراً من المحققين قالوا: إن مسألة الجبر والقدر، ليست [مسألة] مستقلة بنفسها، بل هي بعينها مسألة إثبات الصانع. وذلك لأن العمدة في إثبات الصانع تعالى، هو أن الإمكان محوج إلى المؤثر والمرجح. فنقول: إن صحت هذه القضية، وجب الحكم بافتقار كل الممكنات إلى المؤثر والمرجح. وحينئذ نقول: القدرة على الفعل، وإن لم تكن صالحة للترك، فالجبر لازم. وإن كانت صالحة للترك، وجب أن لا يحصل رجحان الفاعلية على التاركية، إلا لمرجح. وذلك أيضاً يوجب الجبر. فيثبت: أنه لو صلح قولنا: الممكن لا بد له من مرجح، فالقول بالجبر لازم. وإن فسدت هذه المقدمة، فحينئذ يتعذر علينا الاستدلال بإمكان الممكنات على إثبات الصانع. فثبت: إما القول بالجبر، وإما القول بنفي الصانع. فأما الحكم بأن الإمكان محوج في بعض الصور إلى المرجح، وفي صور أخرى غير محوج إلى المؤثر - كما هو قول المعتزلة - فهو قول فاسد. ومثال هذه المسألة: سلسلة مركبة من خلق متشابهة في الخلقة، والصورة، والقوة، والضعف. فإذا كان التقدير ما ذكرناه. وامتنع مع هذا التقدير أن يحكم على بعض تلك الخلق بالقوة، وعلى بعضها بالضعف، فكذا ههنا ماسوی الواحد الأحد، الحق، الواجب لذاته: لا بد وأن يكون ممكناً لذاته. وكل الممكنات مشاركة في طبيعة الإمكان. فإن كان الإمكان محوجاً إلى المؤثر، فليكن كذلك في كل الممكنات، وإن لم يكن محوجاً إلى المؤثر، فليكن كذلك في كل الممكنات. فأما الحكم على بعضها بالاستغناء مع اشتراك الكل في ماهية الإمكان، فإنه محض التحكم الباطل. وبالله التوفيق. فهذا تمام الكلام في هذه المقدمة.
The third investigation: Many of the verifiers have said that the issue of compulsion (jabr) and free will (qadr) is not an independent issue in itself, but rather it is the very issue of proving the Maker. That is because the mainstay in proving the Maker, exalted, is that possibility (al-imkan) necessitates the agent cause (al-mu'aththir) and the preponderant (al-murajjih). So we say: If this proposition is sound, then it is necessary to judge that all possible things depend on the agent cause and the preponderant. Then we say: The ability (al-qudra) to act, if it is not fit for leaving, then compulsion is necessary. And if it is fit for leaving, then the preponderance of agency over abandonment must not occur except by a preponderant. That also necessitates compulsion. So it is established that if our statement 'the possible must have a preponderant' is sound, then the view of compulsion is necessary. And if this premise is corrupt, then it becomes impossible for us to infer the Maker from the possibility of possible things. So it is established: either the view of compulsion, or the view of denying the Maker. As for judging that possibility necessitates the preponderant in some cases and does not necessitate the agent in other cases — as is the view of the Mu'tazila — it is a corrupt view. An example of this issue: a chain composed of creatures similar in constitution, form, strength, and weakness. If the estimation is what we mentioned, and it is impossible with this estimation to judge some of those creatures as strong and some as weak, then likewise here, everything other than the One, the Unique, the Truth, the Necessary by essence must be possible by essence. And all possible things share in the nature of possibility. If possibility necessitates the agent cause, then let it be so in all possible things; and if it does not necessitate the agent cause, then let it be so in all possible things. As for judging some of them as independent despite the sharing of all in the quiddity of possibility, that is pure false arbitrariness. And with God is success. This is the end of the discussion on this introduction.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a third investigation (al-bahth al-thalith) that identifies the problem of jabr and qadr with the problem of proving the existence of the Creator. The author begins by citing many verifiers (muhaqqiqun) who hold that the free will issue is not separate but identical to the proof of the Maker. The reason is that the main argument for God is that possibility (imkan) necessitates a cause (mu'aththir) and a preponderant (murajjih). If this proposition is accepted, then all contingent things need both. Then the author examines ability (qudra) to act: if it is not fit for leaving (i.e., incapable of refraining), then compulsion (jabr) follows. If it is fit for leaving, then the preponderance of acting over leaving must come from a preponderant, which also leads to compulsion. Thus, if the premise 'the possible needs a preponderant' is valid, jabr follows; if it is invalid, then the proof of God from possibility collapses. Therefore, either jabr or denial of God. The Mu'tazila middle ground—that possibility necessitates in some cases but not others—is rejected as corrupt. The author then gives an analogy: a chain of creatures identical in constitution, form, strength, and weakness. If they are identical, one cannot judge some as strong and others as weak. Similarly, all contingent things share the nature of possibility; if possibility necessitates a cause, it must do so for all; if not, for none. Judging some as independent despite shared possibility is arbitrary and false. The passage concludes with 'And with God is success', and marks the end of this introduction.

Key Terms
مسألة الجبر والقدر
إثبات الصانع
إمكان
مؤثر
مرجح
قدرة
ممكن
واجب لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The free will debate is purely about human agency and has no direct link to proving God's existence.
Correction: Razi shows that the issue is identical: the same premise (imkan needs a cause) that proves God also leads to jabr, making it impossible to separate the two.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what conclusion follows if the premise 'the possible must have a preponderant' (al-mumkin la budda lahu min murajjih) is valid?
A) Free will (qadr) is established
B) Compulsion (jabr) is necessary
C) The Mu'tazila view is correct
D) The Creator's existence is disproven

Key Takeaway
The jabr/qadr issue is not separate from proving the Creator; the same logical premise leads either to jabr or to the denial of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's argument forces you to confront a stark choice: either accept that every possible, including your own choices, is determined by an external preponderant (jabr), or abandon the rational proof of God's existence from contingency. Have you ever truly faced the logical implications of your belief in free will? Are you willing to follow reason to its conclusion, even if it challenges your comfortable assumptions?
?
Your sense of moral responsibility depends on the belief that you could have done otherwise. But if the same principle that proves God also proves that every action is compelled, then on what basis do you hold yourself accountable? Can you honestly claim that your choices are your own while also affirming that nothing occurs without God's preponderance? What is the real foundation of your moral conscience?
?
Imagine you had to defend your belief in God to someone who rejects the contingency argument because of its deterministic implications. Razi shows that if you abandon that argument, you lose the primary rational proof for God. Would you be willing to forfeit that proof rather than accept compulsion? What does your answer reveal about the strength or weakness of your faith? Are you relying on reason or mere sentiment?

Refuting the Mu'tazila: The Chain Analogy
Razi rejects the Mu'tazila middle ground and illustrates with a chain analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi rejects the Mu'tazila middle ground and how the chain analogy illustrates the inconsistency of treating possibility differently in different beings.

Core Idea
If all possible things share the same nature, they cannot be treated differently.

Having established the core dilemma, Razi now addresses the Mu'tazila who try to take a middle position: they accept the premise of imkan needing a cause for some things (like the world) but not for human actions. Razi argues this is inconsistent. He illustrates with a powerful analogy of a chain of identical beings: if they are all alike, you cannot judge some as strong and others as weak. Similarly, all possible things share the same nature of possibility, so you cannot make exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the rejection of the Mu'tazila
Read the sentence where Razi criticizes the view that imkan necessitates a cause in some cases but not others. Note the strong language: 'it is a corrupt view'.
The Mu'tazila claim is that the world needs a cause but human actions do not.
2
Understand the chain analogy
Read the analogy of a chain of identical creatures. Notice that since they are identical in all respects, it is impossible to judge some as strong and some as weak.
The analogy is meant to show the arbitrariness of making distinctions within a single nature.
3
Apply the analogy to imkan
Razi applies the same logic: all things other than God share the nature of possibility (imkan). If imkan necessitates a cause, it must do so for all; if not, for none.
The conclusion is that the Mu'tazila position is pure arbitrariness (mahd al-tahakkum).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The final sentence declares the end of this introduction. Reflect on how this argument seals the case that jabr/qadr is identical to proving the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثالث: إن كثيراً من المحققين قالوا: إن مسألة الجبر والقدر، ليست [مسألة] مستقلة بنفسها، بل هي بعينها مسألة إثبات الصانع. وذلك لأن العمدة في إثبات الصانع تعالى، هو أن الإمكان محوج إلى المؤثر والمرجح. فنقول: إن صحت هذه القضية، وجب الحكم بافتقار كل الممكنات إلى المؤثر والمرجح. وحينئذ نقول: القدرة على الفعل، وإن لم تكن صالحة للترك، فالجبر لازم. وإن كانت صالحة للترك، وجب أن لا يحصل رجحان الفاعلية على التاركية، إلا لمرجح. وذلك أيضاً يوجب الجبر. فيثبت: أنه لو صلح قولنا: الممكن لا بد له من مرجح، فالقول بالجبر لازم. وإن فسدت هذه المقدمة، فحينئذ يتعذر علينا الاستدلال بإمكان الممكنات على إثبات الصانع. فثبت: إما القول بالجبر، وإما القول بنفي الصانع. فأما الحكم بأن الإمكان محوج في بعض الصور إلى المرجح، وفي صور أخرى غير محوج إلى المؤثر - كما هو قول المعتزلة - فهو قول فاسد. ومثال هذه المسألة: سلسلة مركبة من خلق متشابهة في الخلقة، والصورة، والقوة، والضعف. فإذا كان التقدير ما ذكرناه. وامتنع مع هذا التقدير أن يحكم على بعض تلك الخلق بالقوة، وعلى بعضها بالضعف، فكذا ههنا ماسوی الواحد الأحد، الحق، الواجب لذاته: لا بد وأن يكون ممكناً لذاته. وكل الممكنات مشاركة في طبيعة الإمكان. فإن كان الإمكان محوجاً إلى المؤثر، فليكن كذلك في كل الممكنات، وإن لم يكن محوجاً إلى المؤثر، فليكن كذلك في كل الممكنات. فأما الحكم على بعضها بالاستغناء مع اشتراك الكل في ماهية الإمكان، فإنه محض التحكم الباطل. وبالله التوفيق. فهذا تمام الكلام في هذه المقدمة.
The third investigation: Many of the verifiers have said that the issue of compulsion (jabr) and free will (qadr) is not an independent issue in itself, but rather it is the very issue of proving the Maker. That is because the mainstay in proving the Maker, exalted, is that possibility (al-imkan) necessitates the agent cause (al-mu'aththir) and the preponderant (al-murajjih). So we say: If this proposition is sound, then it is necessary to judge that all possible things depend on the agent cause and the preponderant. Then we say: The ability (al-qudra) to act, if it is not fit for leaving, then compulsion is necessary. And if it is fit for leaving, then the preponderance of agency over abandonment must not occur except by a preponderant. That also necessitates compulsion. So it is established that if our statement 'the possible must have a preponderant' is sound, then the view of compulsion is necessary. And if this premise is corrupt, then it becomes impossible for us to infer the Maker from the possibility of possible things. So it is established: either the view of compulsion, or the view of denying the Maker. As for judging that possibility necessitates the preponderant in some cases and does not necessitate the agent in other cases — as is the view of the Mu'tazila — it is a corrupt view. An example of this issue: a chain composed of creatures similar in constitution, form, strength, and weakness. If the estimation is what we mentioned, and it is impossible with this estimation to judge some of those creatures as strong and some as weak, then likewise here, everything other than the One, the Unique, the Truth, the Necessary by essence must be possible by essence. And all possible things share in the nature of possibility. If possibility necessitates the agent cause, then let it be so in all possible things; and if it does not necessitate the agent cause, then let it be so in all possible things. As for judging some of them as independent despite the sharing of all in the quiddity of possibility, that is pure false arbitrariness. And with God is success. This is the end of the discussion on this introduction.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a third investigation (al-bahth al-thalith) that identifies the problem of jabr and qadr with the problem of proving the existence of the Creator. The author begins by citing many verifiers (muhaqqiqun) who hold that the free will issue is not separate but identical to the proof of the Maker. The reason is that the main argument for God is that possibility (imkan) necessitates a cause (mu'aththir) and a preponderant (murajjih). If this proposition is accepted, then all contingent things need both. Then the author examines ability (qudra) to act: if it is not fit for leaving (i.e., incapable of refraining), then compulsion (jabr) follows. If it is fit for leaving, then the preponderance of acting over leaving must come from a preponderant, which also leads to compulsion. Thus, if the premise 'the possible needs a preponderant' is valid, jabr follows; if it is invalid, then the proof of God from possibility collapses. Therefore, either jabr or denial of God. The Mu'tazila middle ground—that possibility necessitates in some cases but not others—is rejected as corrupt. The author then gives an analogy: a chain of creatures identical in constitution, form, strength, and weakness. If they are identical, one cannot judge some as strong and others as weak. Similarly, all contingent things share the nature of possibility; if possibility necessitates a cause, it must do so for all; if not, for none. Judging some as independent despite shared possibility is arbitrary and false. The passage concludes with 'And with God is success', and marks the end of this introduction.

ممكن
إمكان
الواحد الأحد
واجب لذاته
تحكم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila position that human actions do not need a cause while the world does is a valid compromise.
Correction: Razi shows that if possibility is a shared nature, then the causal requirement must be uniform. Singling out some possible things as independent is arbitrary and logically unsound.

Key Takeaway

Why a Voluntary Agent Can Choose Without a Preponderator
The author argues that a voluntary agent can prefer one action without a preponderator, citing everyday examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that a capable agent can prefer one action over another without any external preponderator, based on everyday examples.

Core Idea
A voluntary agent can choose between options without needing an external factor to tip the scale.

This lesson presents a key argument from daily life to refute the claim that every preference requires a preponderator. The author uses three relatable examples to show that humans can and do choose between equally attractive options. Understanding this helps clarify the nature of voluntary action.

See how the author concludes that the capable agent can preponderate without a preponderator. Consider how this challenges the necessity of a preponderator in voluntary actions.
Think about what this implies for the concept of free will.

In each case, the agent makes a choice without any apparent preponderator. The conclusion is that a voluntary agent can indeed preponderate one side over the other without a preponderator.

The Fourth Objection: First Proof – Preponderance Without a Preponderator
The author states the objection and gives the first proof showing preponderance without a preponderator is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against the claim that a capable agent can preponderate one of two equals without a preponderator, and why it leads to impossibility.

Core Idea
Preponderance without a preponderator would mean an effect without a cause, which is impossible.

The fourth objection suggests that a capable agent can simply favor one action over another with no external preponderator. The author responds that this is impossible and offers multiple proofs. In this lesson, we focus on the first proof, which examines the capable agent's relation to the two opposites. By analyzing whether that relation is equal or not, the argument shows that any claim of preponderance without a preponderator collapses into absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author states the objection and immediately declares it impossible, then begins the proof.
The proof uses a dichotomous argument about the relation of the agent to the two opposites.
2
Understand the two cases
The proof considers two possibilities: either the agent's relation to the two opposites is equal or not equal. If equal, preponderance cannot occur; if it does occur, it would be an effect without a cause, which is impossible. If not equal, that means the agent already has a preference, which admits the need for a preponderator.
3
See the conclusion
Both cases lead to the same conclusion: preponderance without a preponderator is impossible. The first case leads to an effect without a cause; the second case implicitly concedes a preponderator.
4
Note the incomplete second proof
The passage begins a second proof but is cut off. Be aware that the full response includes additional arguments that are not available here due to missing pages.
This lesson focuses only on the first, complete proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الرابع: وهو قوله: (القادر يمكنه أن يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا المرجح، فنقول: هذا محال. ويدل عليه وجوه:
الأول: إن نسبة القادر إلى الضدين. إما أن تكون على السوية، أولا على السوية. فإن كان على السوية، امتنع حصول الرجحان. وإلا فحينئذ يكون ذلك الرجحان حاصلا، لا لمؤثر. لأن الاستواء لا يوجب الرجحان البتة. وحصول الرجحان قول بحدوث الأثر، لا لمؤثر. وذلك يوجب نفي الصانع وهو محال. وإن كانت نسبة القادر إلى أحد الضدين راجحة، فهذا اعتراف بأن الرجحان لا لمرجح محال. وهو المطلوب.
الثاني: إنه لو حصل الرجحان لالمرجح، لكان حصول ذلك [؟]
As for the fourth question: which is his saying: 'The capable agent can preponderate one of his two objects of power over the other without a preponderator (murjīḥ).' We say: This is impossible. And it is proved by several proofs:
The first: That the relation of the capable agent to the two opposites is either equal (ʿalā s-sawiyya) or not equal. If it is equal, the occurrence of preponderance (rujḥān) is impossible. Otherwise, then that preponderance would occur without an effect (lā li-muʾaththir). Because equality does not necessitate preponderance at all. And the occurrence of preponderance is a statement of the occurrence of an effect without an effector. That necessitates the negation of the Maker, which is impossible. And if the relation of the capable agent to one of the two opposites is preponderant (rājiḥa), then this is an admission that preponderance without a preponderator is impossible. And that is the desired conclusion.
The second: That if preponderance occurred without a preponderator, then the occurrence of that [would be...]
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth objection: that a capable agent (al-qādir) can preponderate one of his two equal objects of power without any preponderator (murjīḥ). The author immediately declares this impossible (muḥāl) and sets forth several proofs. Due to the page break, only the first proof and the beginning of the second proof are present.

The first proof considers the relation of the capable agent to the two opposites (aḍdād), such as action and its omission. This relation is either equal (ʿalā s-sawiyya) or preponderant (rājiḥa). If equal, then preponderance cannot occur because equality does not necessitate one side over the other; if preponderance did occur, it would be an effect without an effector (athar lā li-muʾaththir), which leads to denying the existence of the Maker (nāfī aṣ-Ṣāniʿ). That is impossible. If the relation is preponderant, then that means the agent already has a preference, which implicitly admits that a preponderator is needed—contradicting the original claim.

The second proof begins but is cut off. It starts: 'If preponderance occurred without a preponderator, then the occurrence of that...' The rest is missing from the available pages.

The Logic of Agent and Opposites
First proof using the relation of the agent to opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first proof demonstrates that preponderance without a preponderator is impossible by analyzing the relation of the capable agent to two opposites.

Core Idea
Equal relation cannot yield preference; that would be an effect without a cause.

The author begins his answer to the fourth objection by asserting the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator. The first proof uses a logical dichotomy: either the agent's relation to the two opposites is equal or it is not. We will examine each branch and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the first proof carefully. Notice the dichotomy presented.
The passage starts with the opponent's claim and then the author's response.
2
Break down the dichotomy
Consider two cases: the relation is equal, or it is not equal. What follows in each case?
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why both branches lead to the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator.
The proof forces the opponent into a dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الرابع : وهو قوله : ( القادر يمكنه أن يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا المرجح ، فنقول : هذا محال . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن نسبة القادر إلى الضدين . إما أن تكون على السوية ، أولا على السوية . فإن كان على السوية ، امتنع حصول الرجحان . وإلا فحينئذ يكون ذلك الرجحان حاصلا ، لا لمؤثر. لأن الاستواء لا يوجب الرجحان البتة . وحصول الرجحان قول بحدوث الأثر ، لا لمؤثر . وذلك يوجب نفي الصانع وهو محال . وإن كانت نسبة القادر إلى أحد الضدين راجحة ، فهذا اعتراف بإن الرجحان لا لمرجح محال . وهو المطلوب الثاني : إنه لو حصل الرجحان لالمرجح ، لكان حصول ذلك الرجحان على سبيل الاتفاق المحض. وقد دللنا على أن ذلك يوجب الجبر الثالث : إنا لما اختبرنا أنفسنا ، علمنا : أن عند حصول تعارض الدواعي يتعذر الفعل . وذلك يدل على أن عند عدم الدواعي يمتنع صدور الفعل .
As for the fourth question: namely his statement: 'The capable agent (al-qadir) can prefer one of his two possibilities over the other without a preponderator (murajjih).' We say: This is impossible. And the evidence for it is by proofs: First: The relation of the capable agent to the two opposites (al-diddayn) is either equal or not equal. If it is equal, then preponderance (rujhan) cannot occur. Otherwise, then that preponderance would occur without an influence (mu'aththir). Because equality never necessitates preponderance. And the occurrence of preponderance entails the occurrence of the effect without an influence. That leads to the denial of the Creator, and that is impossible. If the relation of the capable agent to one of the two opposites is preponderant, then this is an admission that preponderance without a preponderator is impossible. And that is what is sought. Second: If preponderance occurred without a preponderator, then that preponderance would occur by sheer chance (ittifaq mahd). And we have already shown that that necessitates compulsion (jabr). Third: When we examine ourselves, we know that when conflicting incentives occur, action becomes impossible. And that indicates that when there are no incentives, the performance of an action is impossible.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the fourth objection, where the opponent claims that a capable agent can prefer one of his two possible actions without a preponderator. The author asserts this is impossible and provides three proofs. The first proof examines the relation of the agent to the two opposites (e.g., doing or not doing). If the relation is equal, preponderance cannot occur; if it occurs, it would be without an influence, leading to atheism. If the relation is already preponderant, then the opponent admits that preponderance without a preponderator is impossible, which is the author's claim. The second proof states that such preponderance would be by pure chance, which leads to compulsion (jabr) — a consequence the opponent likely rejects. The third proof is empirical: when we experience conflicting incentives, action is hindered; conversely, without incentives, action cannot occur. This shows that a preponderator (incentive) is necessary for action. All three proofs reinforce the Ash'ari principle that no preponderance occurs without a preponderator, defending against objections to divine free will.

قادر
مقدور
ضدان
رجحان
مرجح
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It would be without an influence and leads to denial of the Creator.
C) It is necessary because of the agent's nature.
D) It shows that the agent is not capable.

Key Takeaway
The first proof uses a dichotomy to show that preponderance cannot occur without a preponderator, whether the relation is equal or preponderant.

Think of a time you made a choice between two equally appealing options. Did you truly choose without any reason, or was there a subtle preponderating factor? How does this lesson change your understanding of that moment?
?
If preponderance without a cause leads to atheism, then every moment you act as if your choices are uncaused, you are undermining the foundation of your faith. When in your daily life do you tend to ignore Allah's causation and attribute your decisions solely to your own whim?
?
This proof shows that any effect must have a cause. Consider your own existence: are you living as if you are self-caused, or do you constantly acknowledge Allah as the cause of your every action? What practical step can you take today to align your awareness with this truth?

Chance and Incentives: Two More Proofs
Second and third proofs: chance leads to compulsion, and empirical evidence of incentives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why preponderance without a preponderator leads to chance and compulsion, and why empirical evidence of incentives confirms the need for a preponderator.

Core Idea
Without a cause, choice becomes chance or compulsion; our own experience shows action depends on incentives.

After the logical first proof, the author presents two additional arguments. The second proof demonstrates that preponderance without a preponderator would be pure chance, which leads to compulsion (jabr). The third proof is empirical: we observe that when conflicting incentives arise, action is hindered; without incentives, action cannot occur. Together, these proofs strengthen the case against preponderance without a preponderator.

Guided Path
1
Read the second and third proofs carefully
Read both proofs and understand their core arguments.
The second proof is about chance leading to compulsion; the third is about incentives.
2
Understand the consequence of chance
Why does the author argue that preponderance by chance leads to compulsion? Consider the implications.
3
Reflect on the empirical observation
Think about your own experience: does action become impossible when motives conflict? What does that imply?
The third proof is based on self-examination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الرابع : وهو قوله : ( القادر يمكنه أن يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا المرجح ، فنقول : هذا محال . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن نسبة القادر إلى الضدين . إما أن تكون على السوية ، أولا على السوية . فإن كان على السوية ، امتنع حصول الرجحان . وإلا فحينئذ يكون ذلك الرجحان حاصلا ، لا لمؤثر. لأن الاستواء لا يوجب الرجحان البتة . وحصول الرجحان قول بحدوث الأثر ، لا لمؤثر . وذلك يوجب نفي الصانع وهو محال . وإن كانت نسبة القادر إلى أحد الضدين راجحة ، فهذا اعتراف بإن الرجحان لا لمرجح محال . وهو المطلوب الثاني : إنه لو حصل الرجحان لالمرجح ، لكان حصول ذلك الرجحان على سبيل الاتفاق المحض. وقد دللنا على أن ذلك يوجب الجبر الثالث : إنا لما اختبرنا أنفسنا ، علمنا : أن عند حصول تعارض الدواعي يتعذر الفعل . وذلك يدل على أن عند عدم الدواعي يمتنع صدور الفعل .
As for the fourth question: namely his statement: 'The capable agent (al-qadir) can prefer one of his two possibilities over the other without a preponderator (murajjih).' We say: This is impossible. And the evidence for it is by proofs: First: The relation of the capable agent to the two opposites (al-diddayn) is either equal or not equal. If it is equal, then preponderance (rujhan) cannot occur. Otherwise, then that preponderance would occur without an influence (mu'aththir). Because equality never necessitates preponderance. And the occurrence of preponderance entails the occurrence of the effect without an influence. That leads to the denial of the Creator, and that is impossible. If the relation of the capable agent to one of the two opposites is preponderant, then this is an admission that preponderance without a preponderator is impossible. And that is what is sought. Second: If preponderance occurred without a preponderator, then that preponderance would occur by sheer chance (ittifaq mahd). And we have already shown that that necessitates compulsion (jabr). Third: When we examine ourselves, we know that when conflicting incentives occur, action becomes impossible. And that indicates that when there are no incentives, the performance of an action is impossible.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the fourth objection, where the opponent claims that a capable agent can prefer one of his two possible actions without a preponderator. The author asserts this is impossible and provides three proofs. The first proof examines the relation of the agent to the two opposites (e.g., doing or not doing). If the relation is equal, preponderance cannot occur; if it occurs, it would be without an influence, leading to atheism. If the relation is already preponderant, then the opponent admits that preponderance without a preponderator is impossible, which is the author's claim. The second proof states that such preponderance would be by pure chance, which leads to compulsion (jabr) — a consequence the opponent likely rejects. The third proof is empirical: when we experience conflicting incentives, action is hindered; conversely, without incentives, action cannot occur. This shows that a preponderator (incentive) is necessary for action. All three proofs reinforce the Ash'ari principle that no preponderance occurs without a preponderator, defending against objections to divine free will.

اتفاق
جبر
دواعي

Checkpoint

A) Action becomes easier because incentives clarify choice.
B) Action becomes impossible when incentives conflict.
C) Action is independent of incentives.
D) Action occurs randomly.

Key Takeaway
The second proof links chance to compulsion, and the third proof shows through self-examination that incentives are necessary for action, reinforcing the need for a preponderator.

The Fleeing Person and the Necessity of Intention
Refuting the claim that action can occur without intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the examples of the fleeing person and the thirsty person demonstrate that every specific action requires a specific intention, and that intention itself requires an incentive (da'iyah), supporting the necessity of a preponderator.

Core Idea
Without intention, no action occurs; even intention needs a cause.

This passage addresses a common objection: if a person can choose between two options without any cause, then action without intention is possible. The author uses two vivid examples—a person fleeing from a lion and a thirsty person choosing between two cups—to show that without a specific intention, no movement occurs. He then turns the examples into a proof for the necessity of an incentive (da'iyah) for any action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow from example to conclusion.
The passage is a single argument, so read it as a whole.
2
Analyze the examples
Identify what happens to the fleeing person and the thirsty person when they lack intention. The author claims they remain stationary.
This is an empirical observation used as a premise.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the capable agent does not act without an incentive (da'iyah). Note how the examples support this.
The conclusion is: 'al-qadir la yasdir 'anh al-fi'l al-mu'ayyan illa idha da'ahu al-da'i ilayh.'
4
Examine the counter-question and answer
The author anticipates an objection: what caused the intention itself? His answer: that is precisely the same proof (al-dalil) we have used.
This shows that the opponent's own example leads to infinite regress or acceptance of the principle.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument matters for the broader discussion of causation and divine attributes.
This connects to the larger theological debate about human action and divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الخامس : وهو الهارب من السبع ، إذا عن له طريقان فجوابه : من وجهين : الأول : وهو أن الهارب من السبع، ما لم يقصد الذهاب في أحد الطريقين ، فإنه لا يترجح ذهابه في أحد الطريقين، على ذهابه في الآخر . وكذلك العطشان إذا خير بين شرب قدحين فإنه ما لم يقصد أخذ أحد القدحين فإنه لا تمتد يده إلى أحد القدحين دون الثاني حتى إنه لو لم يحصل في قلب الهارب من السبع ، قصد إلى سلوك أحد الطريقين بعينه ، فإنه يبقى هناك ولا يتحرك . وكذلك العطشان إذا لم يخص أحد القدحين بمد اليد وأخذه ، فإنه لا يتحرك ذلك القدح ، ولا يرتفع إلى فمه ، ولا ينصب بنفسه إلى حلقه . فثبت : أن القادر ما لم يخص أحد الطريقين بالقصد إليه ، وما لم يخص أحد القدحين بمد اليد إليه، فإنه لا يترجح وهو على غيره. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فحينئذ يصير هذا الكلام حجة لنا في قولنا : ( إن القادر لا يصدر عنه الفعل المعين ، إلا إذا دعاه الداعي إليه ) بقي أن يقال : فلم حصل في قلب هذا الهارب : القصد إلى هذا الطريق ، دون ذلك الطريق ؟ ولم حصل في قلب هذا العطشان : إرادة أخذ القدح، دون ذلك القدح، إلا أن نقول هذا عين الدليل الذي تمسكنا به.
As for the fifth question: namely the person fleeing from a lion, when two roads appear to him, its answer is from two aspects: First: The one fleeing from a lion, as long as he does not intend to go by one of the two roads, his going by one of the two roads is not preponderant over his going by the other. Similarly, the thirsty person when given a choice between drinking two cups, as long as he does not intend to take one of the two cups, his hand does not reach out to one of them rather than the other. Indeed, if there does not occur in the heart of the one fleeing from the lion an intention to take a particular road, he remains there and does not move. Likewise, the thirsty person, if he does not single out one of the two cups by extending his hand and taking it, that cup does not move, nor does it rise to his mouth, nor does it pour itself into his throat. So it is established that the capable agent, as long as he does not single out one of the two roads by intending it, and does not single out one of the two cups by extending his hand to it, then he has no preponderance over another. And if the matter is so, then this speech becomes an argument for us in our statement: 'The capable agent does not perform a specific action unless the incentive (da'iyah) calls him to it.' It remains to be said: Why did the intention to this particular road occur in the heart of the one fleeing, rather than that road? And why did the will to take that cup occur in the heart of the thirsty one, rather than that cup? Except that we say: This is precisely the proof we have adhered to.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage answers the fifth question, which likely posed the example of a fleeing person choosing between two roads to argue that a capable agent can prefer one without a preponderator. The author gives two aspects (though only the first is fully developed). He begins by analyzing the example: the fleeing person, without an intention (qasd) to take a specific road, does not move; similarly, the thirsty person, without a specific intention, does not reach for one cup. This empirical observation shows that preponderance (rujhan) in action occurs only when there is a specific intention. Therefore, the example actually supports the author's claim that the capable agent cannot act without an incentive (da'iyah). Then the author anticipates a counter-question: what caused the intention for this road rather than that? The answer is that this is precisely the same proof (al-dalil) the author had earlier used to show the need for a preponderator. In other words, the opponent's own example ultimately reinforces the necessity of a preponderator for the intention itself, leading to an infinite regress or acceptance of the principle.

The Need for Motives in Action
Sanusi presents the third proof that action requires motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every voluntary action requires a preponderant motive, as proven by introspection.

Core Idea
Action cannot occur without a motivating reason.

This lesson presents a brief but powerful proof from introspection. By examining our own experience, we see that when motives conflict, action becomes impossible. From this, the author deduces that in the absence of any motive, action is impossible. This establishes that a capable agent requires a specific motive to act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

2
Understand the introspective method
The author appeals to self-testing (ikhtibār anfusinā). Notice that this is an empirical observation about our own psychology.
This method is common in kalam for establishing foundational principles.
3
Identify the two logical steps
First, conflicting motives → impossibility of action. Second, no motives → impossibility. Recognize how the second follows from the first.
The passage uses a concise deductive structure.
4
Reflect on the implication
If action requires a motive, then every voluntary act must have a preponderant motive. This is key to understanding causality and divine action in kalam.

Third: When we tested ourselves, we knew that when conflicting motives occur, the action becomes impossible. And that indicates that when there are no motives, the occurrence of the action is impossible.

This shows that in the absence of any motive, action cannot occur. Thus, a capable agent requires a specific preponderant motive to act.

The Escapee and the Thirsty: Why Intention is Necessary for Action
The author replies to the objection by demonstrating the necessity of specific intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari argument about the necessity of motive (dā'ī) is strengthened by the objection about the fleeing person and the thirsty.

Core Idea
Specific intention is necessary for any action; otherwise, nothing happens.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection that seems to challenge the need for a motive by using everyday examples. He shows that without a specific intention, even the simplest actions cannot occur. This leads to a deeper point: the motive itself must come from God, not the servant, confirming the Ash'ari doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the full English translation. Focus on the flow of the argument.
The passage uses concrete examples to illustrate a theological point.
2
Identify the examples and their conclusion
Notice the two examples: the fleeing person with two paths and the thirsty person with two cups. What common conclusion does the author draw from them?
The conclusion is that without specific intention, no action occurs.
3
Follow the follow-up question and answer
The author anticipates a question: why does one specific intention occur rather than another? How does he answer?
He argues that the specific motive must come from God, not the servant, to avoid infinite regress.
4
Understand how this supports the Ash'ari position
Reflect on how the objection, instead of refuting the need for a motive, actually confirms it and leads to the conclusion that the servant is not independent.
The author calls the objection a 'confirmation, strengthening, and establishment' of his own argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الخامس : وهو الهارب من السبع ، إذا عن له طريقان فجوابه : من وجهين : الأول : وهو أن الهارب من السبع، ما لم يقصد الذهاب في أحد الطريقين ، فإنه لا يترجح ذهابه في أحد الطريقين، على ذهابه في الآخر . وكذلك العطشان إذا خير بين شرب قدحين فإنه ما لم يقصد أخذ أحد القدحين فإنه لا تمتد يده إلى أحد القدحين دون الثاني حتى إنه لو لم يحصل في قلب الهارب من السبع ، قصد إلى سلوك أحد الطريقين بعينه ، فإنه يبقى هناك ولا يتحرك . وكذلك العطشان إذا لم يخص أحد القدحين بمد اليد وأخذه ، فإنه لا يتحرك ذلك القدح ، ولا يرتفع إلى فمه ، ولا ينصب بنفسه إلى حلقه . فثبت : أن القادر ما لم يخص أحد الطريقين بالقصد إليه ، وما لم يخص أحد القدحين بمد اليد إليه، فإنه لا يترجح وهو على غيره. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فحينئذ يصير هذا الكلام حجة لنا في قولنا : ( إن القادر لا يصدر عنه الفعل المعين ، إلا إذا دعاه الداعي إليه ) بقي أن يقال : فلم حصل في قلب هذا الهارب : القصد إلى هذا الطريق ، دون ذلك الطريق ؟ ولم حصل في قلب هذا العطشان : إرادة أخذ القدح، دون ذلك القدح، إلا أن نقول هذا عين الدليل الذي تمسكنا به. وذلك لأنا بينا : أن صدور الفعل عن القادر يتوقف على حصول الداعية المخصوصة في قلبه . وحصول تلك الداعية بعينها في قلبه ، ليس بداعية أخرى من قبل العبد وإلا لزم التسلل . بل لا بد وأن يكون من الله ، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العبد غير مستقل لا بالفعل ولا بالترك . فهذا الذي أوردتموه علينا ليس اعتراضا على كلامنا بل هو تأكيد ، وتقوية ، وتقرير له فظهر أن هذا السؤال الذي ذكروه : سؤال ساقط ، لا يصدر إلا عن قلة الفهم
As for the fifth question: the one fleeing from a lion, when two ways present themselves, its answer is from two aspects: The first: that the one fleeing from the lion, as long as he does not intend to go in one of the two ways, his going in one of the two ways is not preponderant over his going in the other. Likewise, the thirsty one when he is given a choice between drinking two cups, as long as he does not intend to take one of the two cups, his hand does not extend to one of the two cups to the exclusion of the second. Indeed, if the intention to take one specific way does not occur in the heart of the fleeing one, he remains there and does not move. Likewise, the thirsty one if he does not specify one of the two cups by extending his hand and taking it, that cup does not move, nor does it rise to his mouth, nor does it pour itself into his throat. So it is established that the capable agent, as long as he does not specify one of the two ways by intending it, and as long as he does not specify one of the two cups by extending his hand to it, does not preponderate [one action] over another. And when this is the case, then this speech becomes an argument for us in our statement: 'The capable agent does not produce the specific action except when the motive (dā'ī) calls him to it.' It remains to be said: Why did the intention for this specific way occur in the heart of the fleeing one, and not that way? And why did the desire to take this cup occur in the heart of the thirsty one, and not that cup? [The answer is] this is exactly the proof we have used. And that is because we have clarified that the occurrence of the action from the capable agent depends on the occurrence of the specific motive (al-dā'iyya al-makhṣūṣa) in his heart. And the occurrence of that specific motive itself in his heart is not from another motive on the part of the servant, otherwise regression (tasalsul) would follow. Rather, it must be from God. Then it follows that the servant is not independent, neither in action nor in inaction. So this that you have raised against us is not an objection to our speech, but rather it is a confirmation, strengthening, and establishment of it. So it becomes clear that this question that they mentioned is a fallen question, which only arises from lack of understanding.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is the first answer to the fifth question, which objects that a fleeing person can choose one path without a motive. The author argues by example: the fleeing person, unless he specifically intends one path, does not move. Similarly, a thirsty person does not reach for a cup without specific intention. Therefore, the agent requires a specific motive (dā'ī) to act. Then the author anticipates a follow-up question: why did the intention for this specific path occur and not another? The answer is that this is exactly the point: the specific motive must come from God, not from the servant, otherwise infinite regress (tasalsul). Thus, the objection actually confirms the Ash'ari position that the servant is not independent in action.

Correction: The author argues that without a specific intention, no movement occurs; therefore, a motive is necessary, and its source must be divine.

Refuting Action Without a Preponderator: The First Option
Sanusi refutes the first option of action without a preponderator using two thought experiments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reason necessarily requires a preponderating factor for any action, and how the author refutes the claim that an agent can act without one.

Core Idea
Action without a preponderator is impossible by rational necessity.

Sanusi continues his defense of the necessity of a preponderating factor (murajjiḥ) in all actions. In this passage, he presents the second answer to the objection that an agent can act without a preponderator. He first refutes the notion that an act can occur without any preponderating factor, using two concrete examples that appeal to our innate rational intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two options
The author presents two possible positions for the objector. Identify them and see which one he refutes here.
2
Analyze the first example (the market walker)
Consider the scenario of a person with two equal motives. Notice the conclusion: he cannot move until one side becomes preponderant.
This shows that action requires a preponderating factor.
3
Analyze the second example (the thirsty person)
The absurdity of a cup rising by itself illustrates the impossibility of action without a preponderator.
4
Understand the role of badīhat al-ʿaql
The author appeals to self-evident rational knowledge as the ultimate arbiter. These examples are meant to be immediately convincing to any rational person.
Badīhat al-ʿaql is the innate, intuitive intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في الجواب : إنكم إما أن تقولوا : إن القادر على الضدين ، يترجح صدور أحد الضدين عنه من غير مرجح البتة . أو تقولوا : القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الثاني من غير مرجح . والأول باطل . لأنا نجد أنفسنا بالضرورة : أن الذي يمشي في السوق إلى موضع ، إذا وقع في قلبه : أن الرجوع عن ذلك الجانب إلى جانب آخر : مصلحة من وجه . فإنه متى تعادلت الداعيتان في قلبه ، ولم يترجح أحدهما على الآخر البتة ، فإن ذلك الإنسان يبقى في ذلك الموضع ، ولا يمكنه أن يتقدم أو يتأخر ويبقى على تلك الحالة إلى أن يقع في قلبه : أن أحد الجانبين أكثر مصلحة . وعند ظهور هذا الرجحان يترجح الإذهاب إلى ذلك الجانب . فثبت : أن القول بأنه يصدر عنه أحد الضدين من غير أن يكون هو مرجحا لذلك الضد كلام تدفعه بديهة العقل . وأيضا : فهل يقبل العقل أن يقال : العطشان إذا خير بين شرب قدحين ، ثم أنه لا يمد يده إلى أحدهما ، ولا يخص أحدهما بالأخذ والرفع . بل المجرد كونه قادرا يرتفع أحد القدحين إلى فمه، وينصب ذلك الماء في حلقه ؟ فهذا مما يعلم بطلانه ببديهة العقل . وأما إن قيل : إن القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا
The second aspect of the answer: You [objectors] either say: The capable agent of two opposites, the occurrence of one of the two opposites from him is preponderant without any preponderator at all. Or you say: The capable agent preponderates one of his two possibles over the other without a preponderator. The first is false. For we find in ourselves necessarily: that the one who walks in the market to a place, when it occurs in his heart that returning from that side to another side is a benefit in some respect, then when the two motives are equal in his heart and neither is preponderant over the other at all, that person remains in that place, and cannot go forward or backward, and remains in that state until it occurs in his heart that one of the two sides is more beneficial. And upon the appearance of this preponderance, the going to that side becomes preponderant. So it is established that the statement that one of the two opposites occurs from him without him being a preponderator for that opposite is a statement repudiated by the innate intellect (badīhat al-ʿaql). Also, does the intellect accept that it is said: The thirsty one, when given a choice between drinking two cups, then he does not extend his hand to either, nor does he specify one for taking and raising, but rather, by virtue of being capable, one of the two cups rises to his mouth and that water pours into his throat? This is known to be false by the innate intellect (badīhat al-ʿaql). As for if it is said that the capable agent preponderates one of his two possibles over the other without...
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the second answer to the fifth question. The author poses two options for the objector: either the act occurs without any preponderator (murajjiḥ), or the agent himself preponderates without a preponderator. The first is refuted by necessary knowledge (ḍarūra) and introspection. The example: a person walking in a market, when two motives are equally balanced, cannot move until one side becomes more beneficial. Thus, action requires a preponderating factor. The author then gives the example of the thirsty person who does not specify a cup; the idea that the cup rises by itself is absurd. This shows that the first option is false by rational intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql). The text then begins to discuss the second option but cuts off.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that free will means acting without any cause or preponderating factor.
Correction: The author shows that reason itself requires a preponderator for any action; without it, no action occurs, as illustrated by the two examples.

Checkpoint

A) The agent acts with a preponderator
B) The agent acts without any preponderator
C) The agent is compelled
D) The agent chooses randomly

Key Takeaway
Action without a preponderating factor is rationally impossible, as shown by two intuitive examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The thirsty person in the example does not drink unless he specifies. In your spiritual life, are you waiting for the cup to rise to your mouth without making a choice? ?
If action without a preponderator is impossible, then every delay in obedience is evidence of a missing or weak preponderator. What is the one act of obedience you have been postponing, and what would it take for that motive to become preponderant in your heart starting today?

Preponderance Necessitates Obligation
Conclusion of the first proof: rajahan necessitates wujub.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author concludes that preponderance (rajahan) necessitates obligation (wujub) in divine acts, and how this refutes the objection of mere probability.

Core Idea
Preponderance always necessitates obligation — probability is ruled out.

The first proof has built the case that whenever there is preponderance for an act, it must be obligatory. In this concluding passage, the author drives the point home: the alternative — cessation without obstacle — is rationally impossible. We examine this final step and its refutation of the opponent's appeal to probability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: impossibility of cessation → necessity of obligation → refutation of probability.
The final phrase 'wa billah al-tawfiq' is a pious invocation, not part of the argument.
2
Understand the impossibility of cessation without obstacle
The author states that if cessation (tark) occurs when intending an act with no obstacle, it is rationally impossible. Ask yourself: why would a rational agent fail to do what they intend when nothing prevents them? This sets the foundation.
3
Connect preponderance to obligation
From the impossibility above, the author concludes that whenever preponderance (rajahan) is present, obligation (wujub) necessarily follows. Trace how this connection is made: preponderance means the act is outweighingly preferable, so not doing it would be cessation without reason.
4
See how this refutes probability
The opponent claimed that preponderance only makes an act probable, not obligatory. The author argues that this probability claim is invalidated by the logical necessity of obligation when preponderance occurs.
The refutation is direct: 'that invalidates what they mentioned of probability.'
5
Reflect on the invocation
The passage ends with 'And with Allah is guidance.' Consider why the author includes this. It acknowledges that ultimate success in understanding comes from Allah, not from human reasoning alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage concludes the first proof. The author states that if cessation (tark) occurs when intending an act, with no obstacle, this is utterly impossible for a rational person. Therefore, it is established that whenever preponderance (rajahan) is present, obligation (wujub) necessarily follows in the divine acts. This refutes the opponent's claim of mere probability. The final phrase 'wa billah al-tawfiq' is a pious invocation.

Preponderance in divine acts necessitates obligation, not mere probability.
