The Third and Fourth Ranks: Necessity of Inclination and Perception
Al-Razi establishes the third and fourth ranks as necessary prerequisites for action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi considers inclination and perception as necessary and involuntary prerequisites for any action, and how this supports the determinist position.

Core Idea
Inclination and perception are forced upon us, not chosen.

Al-Razi continues his analysis of the causal chain leading to action by identifying two more ranks that must occur before any voluntary movement. The third rank is the spontaneous inclination towards what seems beneficial and aversion from what seems harmful. The fourth rank is the mental perception of the object itself. Both are known by necessity and are not subject to choice. This passage strengthens the determinist argument by showing that even the psychological basis of action is compelled.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that Al-Razi presents the third and fourth ranks in sequence, each supported by a justification.
Pay attention to the phrase 'known by necessity' (معلوم بالضرورة) which indicates these are self-evident.
2
Identify the two ranks
Find the descriptions of the third rank (inclination and aversion) and the fourth rank (perception). Note how they are connected: perception must come before knowing benefit or harm.
The third rank is about the emotional response; the fourth is about the cognitive grasp.
3
Analyze the necessity claim
Al-Razi says these are 'necessary premises' (مقدمات لازمة). Ask yourself: can a person avoid having inclinations? Can they avoid forming perceptions of objects? According to Al-Razi, no—these are automatic.
This challenges the idea that the initial step of action is freely chosen.
4
Reflect on the determinist implication
Consider how these two ranks fit into the overall argument. If even our basic inclinations and perceptions are compelled, then the entire chain leading to action is determined.
This lesson is part of a larger discussion on qadar (divine predetermination).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المرتبة الثالثة : وهي أنه لابد من حصول الميل إلى ما يعتقد فيه كونه نفعا ، وحصول النفرة عما يعتقد فيه كونه ضررا . فهذا أمر معلوم بالضرورة . فإنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن اللذة والسرور مطلوبان بالذات ، والألم والغم مهروب عنهما بالذات . وأما المرتبة الرابعة : وهي التصور. وهذا أيضا أمر لا بد منه . فإنه إذا لم يصر ذلك الشيء متصورا ، امتنع العلم بكونه نافعا وكونه ضارا . وما لم يحصل هذا الإدراك ، لم يحصل الطلب والهرب . فثبت : أن هذه المقدمات لازمة.
As for the third rank: it is that there must occur inclination to what is believed to be beneficial, and aversion to what is believed to be harmful. This is a matter known by necessity. For we know by necessity that pleasure and joy are desired in themselves, and pain and grief are fled from in themselves. As for the fourth rank: it is perception (tashawwur). This too is indispensable. For if that thing is not perceived, knowledge of it being beneficial or harmful is impossible. And if this perception does not occur, seeking and fleeing do not occur. So it is established that these premises are necessary.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of two necessary ranks (marātib) that precede voluntary action. The third rank states that inclination (mayl) toward what is believed beneficial and aversion (nufra) from what is believed harmful are necessary; this is known intuitively (bi-ḍarūra) because pleasure and joy are sought intrinsically and pain and grief are avoided intrinsically. The fourth rank is perception (tashawwur): without forming a mental image of the object, one cannot know it as beneficial or harmful, and consequently no seeking or fleeing occurs. Al-Razi concludes that these premises (muqaddimāt) are necessary (lāzima)—they cannot be chosen or avoided. This supports the determinist argument by showing that even the beginning of voluntary action is compelled.

Key Terms
الميل / inclination
النفرة / aversion
التصور / perception
الضرورة / necessity
المقدمات اللازمة / necessary premises

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inclination and perception are voluntary acts that we freely choose.
Correction: Al-Razi argues they are necessary (ḍarūrī) and indispensable; we do not choose to have inclinations or to perceive objects—they occur automatically.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth rank that must occur before seeking or fleeing?
A) Inclination toward benefit.
B) Aversion from harm.
C) Perception (tashawwur) of the thing.
D) Knowledge of God.

Key Takeaway
The third and fourth ranks show that even the psychological prerequisites of action—inclination and perception—are necessary and not freely chosen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your inclinations toward good and aversion from evil are not ultimately your choice, then what is your genuine excuse for repeatedly sinning? How will you hold yourself accountable for something you claim you cannot control?
?
You perceive the harm of your habits daily—yet you still flee from correction. What does this reveal about the state of your heart, and what are you willing to sacrifice to change it?
?
Al-Razi says pleasure is sought by nature—but do you seek Allah's pleasure as naturally as you seek physical comfort? If not, what must be rebuilt in your soul so that spiritual delight overpowers worldly craving?

Four Rational Proofs That Knowledge Does Not Necessitate
Objection and four rational proofs that divine knowledge does not necessitate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first four rational proofs that divine knowledge does not turn possible things into necessary or impossible, and how each proof defends human responsibility and divine wisdom.

Core Idea
God's knowledge does not change the nature of possible things; it conforms to them.

This passage presents an objection claiming that God's knowledge prevents or necessitates actions, and then gives four rational proofs to refute it. The proofs argue from divine power, the nature of knowledge, human experience, and the implications for divine commands. By studying these proofs, you will see how classical Islamic theology preserves both divine omniscience and human accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the claim being refuted: that divine knowledge turns possible things into necessary or impossible.
The objection is stated at the beginning of the passage.
2
Work through the first proof
This proof shows that if knowledge necessitated, God would have no power over what He knows, negating divine power.
Focus on the logical conditional: if knowledge changes possibility, then God is not capable.
3
Examine the second proof
Knowledge relates to the known as it is; possible things remain possible in knowledge.
This proof is based on the principle that knowledge conforms to its object.
4
Consider the third proof from human experience
Intuitive moral distinctions between voluntary and involuntary actions show that knowledge does not prevent action.
Notice the example of the stone thrower and the intuitive blame.
5
Reflect on the fourth proof regarding divine commands
If knowledge of non-existence prevented existence, God's command to the disbeliever to believe would be absurd.
This proof highlights the wisdom of God and the impossibility of commanding to annihilate His knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : علم الله لا يقلب الجائز ممتنعاً . ويدل عليه وجوه عقلية ووجوه سمعية أما الوجوه العقلية فعشرة : الأول : لو كان كذلك ، لوجب أن لا يكون الله قادراً على شيء أصلاً . لأنه تعالى إن علم في الشيء أنه سيقع ، كان واجب الوقوع . وما كان واجب الوقوع لم يكن له في وقوعه حاجة إلى المؤثر ، فكان ينبغي أن يستغني وقوعه عن قدرة الله تعالى . فإن علم أنه لا يقع كان ممتنع الوقوع ، والممتنع لا قدرة عليه . فثبت : أن العلم لو اقتضى انقلاب الجائز واجباً أو ممتنعاً ، لزم نفي قدرة الله تعالى . وذلك محال . الثاني : إن العلم يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو عليه . فإن كان ممكناً ، علمه ممكناً وإن كان واجباً ، علمه واجباً . ولا شك أن الإيمان والكفر ، بالنظر إلى ذاته من باب الممكنات . ولو صار واجب الوجود ، لصار العلم مؤثراً في المعلوم . وقد بينا أنه محال . الثالث : لو كان العلم والخبر مانعاً ، لما كان العبد قادراً على شيء أصلا . لأن الذي علم الله وقوعه كان واجب الوقوع . والواجب لا قدرة عليه ، والذي علم علمه كان ممتنع الوقوع ، والممتنع لا قدرة عليه . فوجب أن لا يكون العبد قادرا على شيء أصلا ، فكانت حركاته وسكناته ، جارية مجرى حركات الجمادات ، ومجرى الحركات الاضطرارية للحيوانات . لكنا بالبديهية نعلم فساد ذلك . فإن من رمى إنساناً بالأجرة حتى شجة ، فإنه يذم الرامي ، ولا تذم الأجرة ، ويدرك بالبديهة تفرقة بين ما إذا سقطت الأجرة عليه ، وبين ما إذا لكمه إنسان بالاختيار. ولذلك فإن العقلاء ببدائه عقولهم . يدركون الفرق بين مدح المحسن وذم المسيء ، ويلتمسون ويأمرون ويعاتبون ، ويقولون : لم فعلت ؟ ولم تركت ؟ فدل ذلك على أن العلم والخبر غير مانعين من الفعل. الرابع : لو كان العلم بالعدم، مانعاً من الوجود . لكان أمر الله الكافر بالإيمان : أمراً له بإعدام علمه وبتجهيله وبتكذيبه . وكما أنه لا يليق بحكمته أن يأمر عباده بأن يعدموه ، فكذلك لا يليق بحكمته أن يأمرهم بأن يعدموا علمه . لان إعدام ذات الله تعالى وصفاته ، ليس في طاقته . فكان الأمر سفها وعبثاً . وهو على الحكيم محال . فدل هذا على أن العلم بالعدم لا يكون مانعا من الوجود .
If it is said: God's knowledge does not turn the possible into impossible. And evidence for it is rational proofs and scriptural proofs. As for the rational proofs, they are ten: First: If it were so, then it would necessarily follow that God is not capable of anything at all. Because if He knows that a thing will occur, it becomes necessary of occurrence. And what is necessary of occurrence has no need in its occurrence for an efficient cause, so its occurrence should be independent of God's power. And if He knows that it will not occur, it becomes impossible of occurrence, and the impossible cannot be subject to power. So it is established: if knowledge necessitated the change of the possible to necessary or impossible, it would necessitate the denial of God's power. And that is impossible. Second: Knowledge relates to the known as it is. If it is possible, He knows it as possible; and if it is necessary, He knows it as necessary. There is no doubt that faith and disbelief, in themselves, are from the category of possible things. If they became necessary of existence, then knowledge would become an effect in the known. And we have shown that is impossible. Third: If knowledge and report (khabar) were preventive, then the servant would not be capable of anything at all. Because what God knew would occur is necessary of occurrence, and the necessary cannot be subject to power; and what He knew [would not occur] is impossible of occurrence, and the impossible cannot be subject to power. So it would be necessary that the servant is not capable of anything at all, and his movements and rests would be like the movements of inanimate objects and like the involuntary movements of animals. However, we know by intuition the corruption of that. For if a person throws a stone at another until it wounds him, he blames the thrower, not the stone; and by intuition one perceives a difference between when the stone falls on him and when a person strikes him intentionally. Therefore, rational people by their intuitive minds perceive the difference between praising the good-doer and blaming the evildoer, and they seek, command, rebuke, and say: Why did you do? And why did you leave? So that indicates that knowledge and report are not preventive of action. Fourth: If knowledge of non-existence were preventive of existence, then God's command to the disbeliever to believe would be a command to annihilate His knowledge, to make Him ignorant, and to deny Him. And just as it is not befitting of His wisdom to command His servants to annihilate Him, so likewise it is not befitting of His wisdom to command them to annihilate His knowledge. Because annihilating God's essence and attributes is not within their power. So the command would be foolishness and frivolity. And that is impossible for the Wise. So this indicates that knowledge of non-existence is not preventive of existence.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and four rational proofs in response. The objector claims that God's knowledge does not turn the possible into impossible. The author counters with ten rational proofs, of which four are given here. The first proof argues that if knowledge made things necessary or impossible, then God would not be capable of anything: if He knows something will happen, it becomes necessary and independent of His power; if He knows it won't, it becomes impossible and cannot be effected. Therefore, divine power would be negated, which is absurd. The second proof states that knowledge conforms to its object: possible things are known as possible, not made necessary. Faith and disbelief are possible in themselves; if knowledge made them necessary, it would change their nature, which is impossible. The third proof uses human experience: if knowledge prevented action, then humans would have no power, and their actions would be involuntary like stones. But intuitively we distinguish between voluntary and involuntary actions, praising and blaming accordingly. This shows that knowledge does not prevent action. The fourth proof argues that if knowledge of non-existence prevented existence, then God's command to the disbeliever to believe would be a command to annihilate God's own knowledge (since He knows they won't believe), which is impossible and unworthy of divine wisdom. Hence, knowledge does not prevent existence.

Key Terms
جائز
واجب
ممتنع
علم الله
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These proofs deny divine foreknowledge.
Correction: They affirm foreknowledge but argue that it does not necessitate or prevent acts in a way that negates human responsibility or divine power.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first rational proof against the objection?
A) If knowledge made things necessary, God would be powerless.
B) Knowledge is irrelevant to possibility.
C) God's knowledge is eternal.
D) Possible things remain possible.

Key Takeaway
The first four rational proofs demonstrate that divine knowledge does not change the nature of possible things, thereby preserving both divine power and human moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God knows everything you will do, does it make you feel trapped or liberated? How can you hold onto hope that your choices are real even though God's knowledge is certain?
?
If you truly believed that divine knowledge does not force your actions, would you take more responsibility for your faith? What is one concrete step you will take today to act in a way that reflects that belief?
?
Think of a time you blamed someone else or circumstance for your sin. If knowledge does not prevent action, then your sin is your own. Will you repent truly, knowing that you could have chosen otherwise? What will change tomorrow?

Reconciling Possibility and Necessity in Divine Knowledge
The author shows that God's knowledge of a possible thing as possible does not conflict with its being necessary by another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a possible event can become necessary through divine decree without contradicting its essential possibility, and how God's knowledge corresponds to both aspects.

Core Idea
A thing can be possible in itself and necessary by another — no contradiction.

The second objection claims that if knowledge corresponds to a thing as it is, and the thing is possible, then it cannot become necessary. In this passage, the author shows that the objection rests on a misunderstanding of the relationship between essential possibility and external necessity. He affirms that God's knowledge encompasses both the thing's intrinsic possibility and its becoming necessary through God's will and power.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection statement carefully. Notice its premise: knowledge must match the thing's own nature, and the thing is possible in itself.
The objector assumes that if something is possible, it cannot be necessary.
2
Follow the author's acceptance
The author accepts the premise that knowledge pertains to the thing as it is. But he then draws a distinction between two aspects of the thing.
Accepting a premise does not mean accepting the conclusion.
3
Grasp the key distinction
Focus on the difference between 'possible in itself' and 'necessary by another'. The thing is possible by its own essence, but necessary due to an external cause (God's power and will).
This distinction is crucial in kalam for understanding divine decree.
4
See how knowledge covers both
The conclusion shows that God's knowledge includes both the essential possibility and the acquired necessity. There is no conflict because they refer to different aspects.
Knowledge is comprehensive, not one-dimensional.

Thus knowledge encompasses both aspects: possibility in its essence and necessity by external cause. There is no contradiction.

Possible in Itself, Necessary by Another
Author answers objection about knowledge of possible acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a thing can be possible in itself yet necessary by another, and how God's knowledge encompasses both aspects without contradiction.

Core Idea
A thing's possibility in itself does not exclude necessity by another.

This passage addresses a key objection in theodicy: if God knows an act as possible, how can it be certain? The author shows that knowledge has two dimensions—one capturing the act's intrinsic possibility, another its conditional necessity. This distinction resolves the apparent tension.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer

The objector assumes that if knowledge relates to the act as possible, it cannot be necessary.
2
Examine the twofold knowledge

The author says knowledge relates to it 'as it is itself among possible things' and 'as it became necessary of occurrence'.
3
Grasp the principle
Understand the logical principle: 'A thing's being possible in itself does not contradict its being necessary by another.' Reflect on why this is important for understanding divine foreknowledge and human action.

Resolving the Contradiction: A Thing Can Be Possible and Impossible by Different Considerations
The author resolves a contradiction by distinguishing intrinsic and extrinsic modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a thing can be both possible in itself and impossible due to a separate cause, and why this resolves the apparent contradiction regarding faith.

Core Idea
A thing can be possible in itself yet impossible due to a separate cause.

This short passage addresses a subtle objection: if faith is possible in itself, how can it be impossible in a particular instance? The author responds by distinguishing between intrinsic and conditional modalities. As we read, notice how the same act can have different modal statuses depending on the consideration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation slowly. Pay attention to the objection and the author's response.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the author distinguishes between 'possible in itself' and 'impossible due to a separate cause'. This is the core of the argument.
3
Analyze the example
The author provides an example of the possible (mumkin) and its three states. List them and relate them to the original objection.
The three states are: possible in itself, necessary when cause present, impossible when cause absent.
4
Apply to faith
Understand how the same reasoning applies to faith: it is possible in itself but may be impossible due to lack of conditions (like knowledge of its necessity).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الخامس: وهو قوله: (الإيمان في نفسه من الجائزات) فلو بسبب العلم: يكون الشيء الواحد: ممكناً محالاً، فجوابه: إنه لا امتناع في كون الشيء الواحد: جائزاً لذاته، ممتنعاً لسبب منفصل. ألا ترى أن الممكن: ممكن لذاته، واجب عند حضور علة وجوده، ممتنع عند عدم سبب وجوده. فكذا ههنا.

Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector argues that if knowledge makes an act necessary, then it is both possible (as a type) and impossible (in this instance) — a contradiction. The author responds using standard kalam modal categories: a thing can be jāʾiz (possible) in itself, but mumtaniʿ (impossible) due to a separate cause (sabab munfaṣil). He illustrates with the modal triad: the possible (mumkin) is possible in itself, necessary when its cause is present, and impossible when its cause is absent. So no contradiction arises.

Intrinsic Possibility vs. Extrinsic Impossibility
Sanusi answers an objection by distinguishing intrinsic and extrinsic possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single thing can be possible in itself yet impossible due to a separate cause, and why this does not entail a contradiction.

Core Idea
Possibility and impossibility can coexist in one thing when considered from different perspectives.

Sanusi now tackles a subtle objection: if God's knowledge makes faith impossible, then the same thing would be both possible and impossible. To answer this, he draws a crucial distinction between what a thing is in itself and what it becomes due to an external cause. This distinction is not just a logical trick—it mirrors how contingent things behave in the world: they are possible by nature but become necessary or impossible depending on causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. The objector claims that if faith is possible in itself but becomes impossible due to God's knowledge, then the same single thing would have contradictory attributes.
Notice the phrase 'الشيء الواحد : ممكناً محالاً' — the objector sees a contradiction.
2
Identify Sanusi's answer
Find where Sanusi states his answer: 'إنه لا امتناع في كون الشيء الواحد : جائزاً لذاته ، ممتنعاً لسبب منفصل'. This is the core thesis.
He does not deny the possibility; he reinterprets the meaning of 'impossible'.
3
Analyze the analogy
Sanusi provides an analogy: a contingent thing (الممكن) is possible in itself, but when its cause is present it becomes necessary, and when its cause is absent it becomes impossible. Reflect on how this illustrates his point.
The analogy shows that the same entity can have different modal statuses depending on external factors.
4
Apply the principle
Now apply Sanusi's distinction to the original objection: faith is possible in itself, but due to God's eternal knowledge that it will not occur, it becomes impossible from that external perspective. There is no contradiction because the two judgments address different aspects.
This resolution preserves both God's foreknowledge and the contingency of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الخامس : وهو قوله : ( الإيمان في نفسه من الجائزات (۱) فلو بسبب العلم : يكون (۲) الشيء الواحد : ممكناً محالاً ، فجوابه : إنه لا امتناع في كون الشيء الواحد : جائزاً لذاته ، ممتنعاً لسبب منفصل . ألا ترى أن الممكن : ممكن لذاته، واجب عند حضور علة وجوده ، ممتنع عند عدم سبب وجوده . فكذا ههنا
As for the fifth question — which is his statement: 'Faith in itself is among the possible things, so if due to knowledge, the single thing becomes possible and impossible' — its answer is: There is no impossibility in a single thing being possible in itself and impossible due to a separate cause. Do you not see that the possible is possible in itself, necessary when the cause of its existence is present, and impossible when the cause of its existence is absent? So likewise here.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that if God's knowledge makes a single thing both possible and impossible, that is contradictory. Sanusi responds by distinguishing between intrinsic possibility and extrinsic impossibility. He gives an analogy: a contingent thing is possible in itself, but becomes necessary when its cause exists and impossible when its cause is absent. Similarly, faith may be possible in itself but become impossible due to God's knowledge that it will not occur. This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.

Key Terms

علة الوجود

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility and impossibility are always intrinsic properties of a thing; if a thing is impossible, it cannot be possible in any sense.
Correction: Sanusi shows that possibility and impossibility can be relative: a thing can be possible in itself but impossible due to an external cause, just as a contingent thing becomes necessary or impossible depending on its cause.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A thing can be possible in itself yet impossible due to an external cause, so there is no contradiction in faith being possible by nature but impossible relative to God's foreknowledge.

Truth and Falsehood in Modal Propositions
Definition of truth and falsehood across three modal categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how truth and falsehood are defined for necessary, impossible, and possible propositions concerning the past and present.

Core Idea
Truth and falsehood depend on the modal status of the proposition.

This passage establishes the basic framework for evaluating statements in kalam by defining truth and falsehood relative to the modal categories of necessity, impossibility, and possibility. It shows that the same subject-predicate pair can yield different truth values depending on its modal nature. Understanding this classification is essential for theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical setup with subject, predicate, and time.
The passage connects truth and falsehood to the rational judgment of contradiction.
2
Identify the three modal categories
Identify the three categories: necessary, impossible, and possible (past/present). For each, note how truth and falsehood are defined.
Compare the definitions: necessary and impossible are opposites.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why does the author distinguish between these categories? How does this affect theological statements?
This classification is foundational for evaluating claims about God and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقريره : إنا إذا عينا الموضوع أمراً واحداً في نفسه ، وعينا المحمول أمراً واحداً في نفسه ، وعينا الوقت وقتاً واحداً في نفسه . فإذا قلنا : ذلك المحمول ، ثبت لذلك الموضوع ، في ذلك الوقت . ثم قلنا : ذلك المحمول ، ما ثبت لذلك الموضوع، في ذلك الوقت . والعقل قاطع بأنه لا بد في هذا الايجاب ، وهذا السلب ، أن يكون أحدهما صادقاً ، والآخر كاذباً . ثم قالوا : الصادق والكاذب معنيان في الواجب وفي الممتنع وفي الممكن الماضي والحاضر. أما في الواجب فكل ما هو في جانب الثبوت ، فهو الصادق . وكل ما هو في جانب السلب ، فهو الكاذب . وأما في الممتنع ، فبالضد . وأما في الممكن الماضي والحاضر، فالذي لم يقع هو الكذب في نفسه, والذي وقع في نفسه ، فهو الصادق.
And its explanation: When we specify the subject as one thing in itself, and we specify the predicate as one thing in itself, and we specify the time as one time in itself. Then we say: that predicate is affirmed for that subject at that time. Then we say: that predicate is not affirmed for that subject at that time. And the intellect decisively judges that in this affirmation and this negation, one of them must be true and the other false. Then they said: The true and the false are two meanings in the necessary, in the impossible, and in the possible past and present. As for the necessary, everything on the side of existence is the true, and everything on the side of non-existence is the false. As for the impossible, it is the opposite. As for the possible past and present, that which did not occur is the false in itself, and that which did occur in itself is the true.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage defines truth and falsehood (al-sidq wa al-kidhb) in propositions. It begins with a preliminary explanation: When we fix a specific subject, a specific predicate, and a specific time, then to say that predicate is affirmed for that subject at that time and then to say it is not affirmed results in a contradiction. The intellect necessarily judges that one of these two statements must be true and the other false. The author then classifies truth and falsehood according to the modal status of the proposition's content. For necessary propositions, whatever is on the side of existence (thubūt) is true, and whatever is on the side of negation (salb) is false. For impossible propositions, the opposite holds. For possible propositions concerning the past and present, what did not occur is false in itself, and what did occur is true. This classification establishes the basic framework for discussing truth conditions in modal contexts.

Key Terms
صدق (sidq)
كذب (kidhb)
واجب (wājib)
ممتنع (mumtani‘)
ممكن (mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, for an impossible proposition, which of the following is true?
A) What is on the side of existence is true, and what is on the side of non-existence is false.
B) The opposite of the necessary: what is on the side of existence is false, and what is on the side of non-existence is true.
C) What actually occurred is true, and what did not occur is false.
D) There is no truth or falsehood for impossible propositions.

Key Takeaway
Truth and falsehood are defined differently depending on whether a proposition is necessary, impossible, or possible concerning past/present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm something as necessary in your creed—such as the existence of Allah—what evidence anchors it? Is it based on logical necessity (thubūt) or on personal comfort? The passage demands that truth for necessary matters is on the side of existence itself. Have you truly reasoned that existence is necessary, or do you merely assume it?
?
The passage says that for possible past events, truth is determined by actual occurrence. Think of a past sin you have not fully repented from: do you sometimes behave as if it did not happen, calling it 'false' in your mind? Will you let the truth of what actually occurred drive you to sincere repentance today?
?
In the impossible category, truth is the opposite of what one might expect. Is there a belief you hold—perhaps about yourself or about God—that is actually impossible, yet you cling to it because it feels true? Are you willing to examine that belief and accept that if it is impossible, it is false, even if it changes how you live?

Truth and Falsehood in Future Possibilities: The Author's Proof
Author argues for determinate truth of future possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author insists that truth and falsehood are determinate in future possible propositions, and how he uses the concept of real qualities to prove it.

Core Idea
Truth and falsehood are real qualities that require a determinate subject.

This passage presents a key argument about the nature of future contingent propositions. Some scholars think they are indeterminate—we cannot say which is true. The author argues that truthness and falseness are real qualities existing in reality, and thus must have specific subjects. This lays the groundwork for understanding how divine knowledge relates to future events.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the contrasting phrases: 'قال بعضهم' vs 'وعندي'.
2
Identify the two positions
Locate the opposing view (indeterminate truth) and the author's view (determinate truth). Understand what each claims.
The author first states the opposing view before giving his own.
3
Analyze the proof structure
Follow the logical steps: (1) truthness/falseness are real qualities, (2) they need an existing locus, (3) every existing thing is determinate, (4) therefore one proposition is specifically true and the other false in reality.
The phrase 'ولما ثبت' marks the conclusion of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الممكن المستقبل . فقال بعضهم : الصادق والكاذب غير متعينين ، بل أحدهما صادق ، والآخر كاذب على التعيين . وعندي : أنه يجب أن يكون الصادق متعيناً ، والكاذب متعيناً في نفس الأمر ، في الممكن المستقبل . والدليل عليه : أن الصدقية والكذبية صفتان حقيقيتان حاصلتان في نفس الأمر . فلا بد لكل واحد منهما من محل موجود في الخارج. وكل موجود في الخارج فهو في نفسه معين لأن كل ما كان موجوداً في نفسه ، فهو معين في نفسه ، والإيهام لا يحصل إلا في الأذهان . بمعنى أنه لا يعلم أن الموصوف بالصدقية . أهو هذه القضية ، أم تلك الأخرى ؟ فأما أن يكون الذي هو الموجود في الأعيان بينهما في نفسه غير معين بحسب وجوده فهو محال . ولما ثبت أن الصدقية والكذبية صفتان موجودتان ، وثبت أن الصفة الموجودة تستدعي محلاً معيناً في نفس الأمر ، يثبت أن إحدى هاتين القضيتين هي الموصوفة بالصدقية في نفس الأمر ، والأخرى هي الموصوفة بالكذبية في نفس الأمر بل نحن لا نعرف أن الموصوف بالصدقية أي الطرفين ؟ والموصوف بالكذبية أيهما ؟ فأما في نفس الأمر ، فإنه يجب أن يكون أحدهما بعينه موصوفاً بالصدقية ، والأخرى بالكذبية .
As for the possible future. Some said: The true and the false are not determinate, rather one of them is true and the other false without specification. But in my view: It is necessary that the true be determinate and the false be determinate in reality, in the possible future. The proof for it: That truthness and falseness are real qualities existing in reality. So each of them must have an existing locus in the external world. And everything existing in the external world is in itself determinate, because everything existing in itself is determinate in itself, and confusion occurs only in minds. Meaning that it is not known whether the described by truthness is this proposition or that other one. But as for that which exists in external things, for it to be in itself indeterminate with respect to its existence is impossible. And since it is established that truthness and falseness are existing qualities, and it is established that an existing quality requires a determinate locus in reality, it is established that one of these two propositions is described by truthness in reality, and the other is described by falseness in reality. Rather we do not know which of the two sides is described by truthness and which is described by falseness. But in reality, it is necessary that one specifically be described by truthness and the other by falseness.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents the author's position on truth and falsehood for future possible propositions (al-mumkin al-mustaqbal). Some scholars hold that such propositions are indeterminate — one is true and the other false, but we cannot specify which. The author disagrees, asserting that truth and falsehood are real qualities (ṣifāt ḥaqīqiyya) that exist in reality (fī nafs al-amr). Every real quality must have an existing locus (maḥall mawjūd) in the external world. Anything existing externally is determinate (mu‘ayyan) in itself. Therefore, truthness and falseness must belong to determinate propositions in reality, even if we are ignorant of which is which. The author supports this by arguing that if the qualities exist, they require a determinate subject; otherwise, an existing thing would be indeterminate, which is impossible. Thus, in reality, one specific proposition is true and the other false, though we may not know which.

Key Terms
ممكن مستقبل (mumkin mustaqbal)
صدقية (ṣidqiyya)
كذبية (kidhbīyya)
صفة حقيقية (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya)
محل (maḥall)
متعين (mu‘ayyan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Future possible propositions have no determinate truth value in reality; they only become true or false when the event occurs.
Correction: The author argues that truthness and falseness are real qualities existing in reality from eternity, and thus they must have a determinate subject, even if we do not know which.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument for truth and falsehood being determinate in future possible propositions?
A) Because revelation informs us of future events.
B) Because truth and falsehood are real qualities requiring an existing determinate locus.
C) Because the intellect cannot conceive of indeterminate truth values.
D) Because all possible propositions are actually necessary.

Key Takeaway
In reality, future possible propositions have a determinate truth value, even if we do not know which is true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If truth and falsehood are already determinate in reality regarding your future—even your unknown decisions—how does that change the way you approach uncertainty? Will you now actively seek the truth of what is right, or remain passive? Write one specific area where you will commit to uncovering the truth rather than avoiding it.
?
This lesson says that indeterminacy is only in minds, not in reality. Is there a belief or doubt you have been ‘retaining’ as uncertain just because it is uncomfortable to resolve? Confront it now: what step will you take before today ends to settle that doubt in your heart?
?
The proof relies on the fact that real qualities demand a real locus. Does your life reflect a conviction that truth is real and binding—even when you cannot see it clearly? Where have you been treating truth as optional or adjustable? Repent and decide to align with the truth that is already determined, whether it is for you or against you.

Two Arguments for the Reality of Truth and Falsity
Objection and two refutations on truth and falsity as real attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments that establish truth and falsity as real attributes requiring a specific subject, refuting the claim that they are mere mental considerations.

Core Idea
Truth and falsity must be real attributes of a subject because they are contradictories that cannot both be absent.

The objection threatens the very foundation of theological discourse by reducing truth and falsity to arbitrary mental labels. The author responds with two sharp arguments: one based on the nature of opposition, the other on the impossibility of a proposition lacking both truth and falsity. This passage demonstrates how kalam defends objective reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Understand the opponent's claim that truth/falsity might not be real attributes but mere mental considerations.
The objection is not a straw man; it challenges a core assumption.
2
First Argument – Opposition
Examine how the mutual negation of truth and falsity forces one to be a real attribute with a specific subject.
Even if considered negative, a negative attribute still requires a subject.
3
Second Argument – Impossibility of Neutrality
See why a proposition cannot be free from both truth and falsity, even in future contingents.
The objection that it might be potentially true is dismissed as an illusion.
4
The Response to the Counter-Objection
Understand the distinction between mental illusion and external reality.
The author clarifies that the mind may imagine neutrality, but in reality one attribute is present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : مدار كلامكم على أن كون الخبر صدقاً وكذباً : صفة حقيقية . تستدعي موصوفاً موجوداً ، وكل موجود فهو في نفسه معين . فنقول : لا نسلم أن كون الخبر صدقاً أو كذباً : صفة وجودية . ولم لا يجوز أن يكونا أمرين اعتباريين ، لا حصول لهما في الأعيان ؟ . والجواب من وجوه : الأول : إن الصدقية والكذبية متقابلان بالسلب والإيجاب . لأن الصدق هو الخبر المطابق ، والكذب هو الخبر الذي لا يطابق . ولما كان هذان الوصفان متقابلين بالسلب والإيجاب ، كان أحدهما لا محالة صفة ثابتة موجودة . وإذا كان كذلك ، وجب أن يحصل له موصوف معين . وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب . ولا ينافي مذهبنا أن الصدقية والكذبية وصفان سلبيان ، لأن الوصف السلبي لا بد له أيضاً من محل معين . لأن هذا السلب عبارة عن عدم شيء [ عن شيء (۱) ] من شأنه أن يكون حاصلاً [ له (٢) ] فيما لم يحصل إمكان حصول ذلك الوصف لذلك الموصوف ، امتنع أن يحكم الذهن بزواله عنه . فثبت : أنه وإن كان سلبياً إلا أنه لا بد له من محل معين . [ الثاني (٣) ] : وهو إنه إذا كان هذا الطرف بعينه خالياً عن الصدقية والكذبية ، كان مجرد قولنا : « زيد غدا يمشي ) : خالياً عن كونه كاذباً ، وخالياً عن كونه صادقاً . فتكون هذه القضية بعينها خالية عن النقيضين معاً . وذلك محال ، وكذا القول في الطرف الآخر. لا يقال : إنه وإن خلا عن الصدقية بعينها ، والكذبية بعينها ، لكنه ما خلا عن كونه . إما صدقاً وإما كذبا . لأنا نقول : هذا الإيهام إنما يقع في الأذهان. أما في الأعيان . فالحاصل أما وصف الصدقية ، وإما وصف الكذبية. فإذا كانت القضية خالية عنهما معاً ، لزم خلوهما عن النقيضين ، وهو محال .
If it is said: The basis of your discussion is that being true or false is a real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya) requiring an existing subject, and everything existing is itself specific. So we say: We do not concede that being true or false is an existential attribute (ṣifa wujūdiyya). Why is it not permissible that they are both mental considerations (amrān iʿtibāriyān) that have no actual existence? The answer is in several respects: First: Truthfulness and falsefulness are opposites by negation and affirmation. Because truth is the report that corresponds, and falsehood is the report that does not correspond. Since these two attributes are opposites by negation and affirmation, one of them must necessarily be a fixed, existing attribute. If so, it must have a specific subject, and thus what is sought is obtained. This does not contradict our doctrine that truthfulness and falsefulness are negative attributes, because the negative attribute also necessarily has a specific subject. For this negation is the absence of something from something that should have been present. When it is not obtained, and there is possibility of obtaining that attribute for that subject, the mind cannot judge its removal from it. So it is established that even if it is negative, it necessarily has a specific subject. Second: If this specific side is free from both truthfulness and falsefulness, then our mere saying 'Zaid will walk tomorrow' would be free from being false and free from being true. So this specific proposition would be free from both contradictories together, which is impossible. The same holds for the other side. It cannot be said that even if it is free from specific truthfulness and specific falsefulness, it is not free from being either true or false. For we say: This illusion only occurs in minds. But in reality, either the attribute of truthfulness or the attribute of falsefulness is present. So if the proposition is free from both, it would be free from the two contradictories, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and the author's response. The objector argues that truth and falsity might not be real attributes but mental constructs. The author gives two refutations: (1) Truth and falsity are opposites by negation and affirmation (one is the privation of the other), so one must be a real, positive attribute. Even if they are considered negative, a negative attribute still requires a specific subject. (2) If a proposition about the future were neither true nor false, it would lack both contradictories, which is impossible because in reality it must be either one or the other. The objection that it might be potentially true or false is dismissed as a mental illusion; in external reality, the attribute is actually present.

Key Terms
صفة وجودية
أمر اعتباري
تقابل السلب والإيجاب
الصدقية
الكذبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Truth and falsity are mere mental constructs with no objective grounding in reality.
Correction: The passage argues that truth and falsity are real attributes requiring a specific subject, by showing that they are opposites by negation and that a proposition cannot lack both.

Checkpoint
What is the second argument the author gives to refute the claim that truth and falsity are mere mental considerations?
A) Truth and falsity are opposites by affirmation and negation
B) A proposition cannot lack both truth and falsity
C) Truth and falsity require a subject
D) God's knowledge determines truth

Key Takeaway
Truth and falsity are real attributes grounded in correspondence or lack thereof, and they necessarily require a specific subject in reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say something is 'just your opinion' or 'neither true nor false,' are you denying the reality of truth and falsehood as attributes of your statements? How does the author's second argument challenge your tendency to avoid committing to objective truth or falsehood in areas like faith or morality? This week, identify one claim you've been treating as 'undecided' and ask yourself: in reality, is it either true or false? What would it cost you to accept that?
?
The author insists that even a negative attribute like falsehood requires a real subject. Where in your life do you hide behind negations—'I am not a bad person,' 'I don't have a problem'—without acknowledging the positive attribute that is actually present? What might that positive attribute be, and why are you avoiding it? Commit today to naming one positive attribute you possess (good or bad) instead of just denying its opposite.
?
Truth and falsehood are not neutral categories—they demand a response. If every statement you make is either true or false in reality, which of your recent 'harmless' statements are actually false? How does that knowledge press you to change your speech or beliefs? Take one specific example from this week and commit to correcting it, whether in thought or action.

The First Case: Sufficiency of Conditions Leads to Necessity
First part of the eighth proof: reduction to absurdity and first case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves that if the totality of conditions for an action is sufficient, then the action must be necessary, implying jabr.

Core Idea
If the conditions are sufficient, the action cannot be merely possible—it must be necessary.

The eighth proof in Al-Matalib al-Aliyah aims to establish that human actions are necessitated (jabr) by God's creation of the conditions. The author begins by considering what happens when the totality of conditions sufficient for an action is present. The argument is concise but foundational for understanding the determinist position.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'البرهان الثامن' to 'وهو الجبر' (the first occurrence). Notice the structure: a reduction to absurdity if actions depend on previous actions, then a division into two cases.
The author first shows that infinite regress is impossible, so there must be a first action.
2
Focus on the first case
Identify the first case: the totality of conditions (essence + God-given attributes) is sufficient for the action. The author argues that if it is sufficient, then the action must be necessary.
3
Understand the logical reasoning
Follow the logic: if the conditions were sufficient but the action could still fail to occur, then the conditions would not actually be sufficient (contradiction). Therefore, the action is necessary.
This proves jabr: the action is compelled by the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثامن
حدث بقدرة العبد شيء، لامتنع أن يقال: إنه إنما صدر عنه هذا الفعل، لأنه صدر عنه فعل آخر. وإلا لزم التسلسل وهو محال. بل لا بد وأن تنتهي أفعال العبد إلى فعل أول صدر عنه ذلك الفعل، لا بواسطة فعل آخر منه، فنقول: صدور ذلك الفعل عنه، لا بد وأن يكون لأجل اتصاف ذاته بصفات حصلت بتخليق الله تعالى فنقول: ذاته مع مجموع تلك الأمور المعتبرة في كونه موجداً لذلك. إما أن يكون كافياً في صدور ذلك الأثر عنه، أو لا يكون. فإن كان كافياً، كان الفعل مع واجباً. إذ لو لم يجب، لجاز أن يتخلف. ولو جاز أن يتخلف، لكان صدور الأثر عن ذلك المجموع ممكناً، لا واجباً. فكان يفتقر إلى زائد آخر. ولو كان كذلك، لما كان الحاصل قبل ذلك كافياً في صدور الأثر عنه. وكنا قد فرضناه كذلك. هذا خلف. فثبت: أن حصول الأثر عند ذلك المجموع، يكون واجباً. وهو الجبر.

وأيضاً: إذا كان حصول الأثر عند حصول ذلك المجموع غير واجب، بل يجوز أن يحصل الفعل تارة، وأن لا يحصل أخرى، ولم يكن تميز حصول الفعل عن لا حصوله، بأمر آخر يضمه العبد إلى المجموع، الذي كان حاصلا. فحينئذ يكون حصول الفعل محض الاتفاق. وهذا أيضاً محض الجبر.

وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: ذات العبد مع مجموع تلك الأمور: غير كاف في كونها مصدراً لذلك الفعل. فحينئذ لا بد من ضم شيء آخر إليه، وذلك الأمر الآخر، إن كان فعلا له، فهو محال وإلا لزم أن يحصل فعل، قبل حصول أول الأفعال. وذلك محال. وإن لم يكن فعلاً له، بل كان فعلا الله تعالى، فعند حصوله قد تم كل ما لا بد منه في كونه مصدراً لأفعاله. فإما أن يكون صدور أفعاله عنه واجباً، أو لا يكون. وحينئذ يعود التقسيم المذكور بعينه. فثبت: أن حصول الفعل عند حصول كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية: واجب، وثبت: أن عند فقدان كل تلك الأمور، وعند فقدان بعضها، يكون صدور الفعل عن العبد ممتنعاً. وعلى هذا التقدير، فإنه يكون القول بالجبر لازماً.

وبالله التوفيق
The eighth proof: If something occurs by the power of the servant, it would be impossible to say: He only performed this action because he performed another action. Otherwise, infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. Rather, the actions of the servant must end with a first action that originated from him without mediation of another action from him. So we say: The origination of that action from him must necessarily be due to his essence being qualified by attributes that came into being through the creation of Allah, the Exalted. So we say: His essence together with the totality of those matters considered in his being an originator of that. Either it is sufficient for the occurrence of that effect from him, or it is not. If it is sufficient, then the action with it is necessary. For if it were not necessary, it would be possible for it to be absent. And if it were possible for it to be absent, then the occurrence of the effect from that totality would be possible, not necessary. Then it would need another additional factor. And if that were the case, that which existed before would not have been sufficient for the occurrence of the effect from him. And we had assumed it to be so. This is a contradiction. Thus it is established that the occurrence of the effect upon that totality is necessary. And that is jabr (compulsion). And also: If the occurrence of the effect upon the occurrence of that totality is not necessary, but rather it is possible for the action to occur sometimes and not to occur at other times, and there is no distinction between the occurrence of the action and its non-occurrence by another matter that the servant adds to the totality that existed. Then the occurrence of the action would be pure coincidence. And this too is pure jabr. As for the second division: That it is said: The essence of the servant together with the totality of those matters is not sufficient for it to be the source of that action. Then it is necessary to attach something else to it. And that other matter, if it is an act of his, it is impossible, for then an action would occur before the occurrence of the first action, and that is impossible. And if it is not an act of his, but rather an act of Allah the Exalted, then upon its occurrence everything necessary for him to be the source of his actions has been completed. Then either the occurrence of his actions from him is necessary, or it is not. And then the aforementioned division returns exactly as it was. Thus it is established that the occurrence of the action upon the occurrence of everything necessary for effectiveness (al-mu'aththiriyya) is necessary. And it is established that when all those matters are absent, or some of them are absent, the occurrence of the action from the servant is impossible. And on this assumption, the assertion of jabr becomes necessary. And with Allah is the success.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a proof (al-burhan al-thamin) demonstrating that human actions are necessarily determined (jabr). The author begins by considering a scenario where an action occurs by the servant's power. He argues that if an action occurs, it cannot be traced back to another action ad infinitum, so there must be a first act. That first act requires the servant's essence to be qualified with attributes created by Allah. Then the author presents a division: either the totality of conditions (the essence plus these attributes) is sufficient for the action or not. If sufficient, the action is necessary—if it were not necessary, it could fail to occur, meaning the conditions were not actually sufficient, contradicting the assumption. Therefore, necessity (jabr) is proven. Then the author considers the second possibility: the totality is not sufficient. In that case, an additional factor is needed. If that factor is the servant's own act, it leads to regress. If it is Allah's act, then the servant's action becomes necessary upon the completion of all conditions, again leading to jabr. The proof systematically eliminates alternatives and concludes that the servant's actions are necessitated by God's creation, which is the position of jabr.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
المجموع (al-majmu')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an action is necessary, there is no role for the human agent at all.
Correction: The proof does not deny the human's role as the locus of the action; it argues that the human's ability is created by God and the conditions are set, so the action is necessitated, but the human still performs it.

Checkpoint
According to the first case of the eighth proof, if the totality of conditions is sufficient for an action, what is the logical consequence?
A) The action is merely possible.
B) The action is impossible.
C) The action is necessary.
D) The action requires additional conditions.

Key Takeaway
When the totality of conditions for an action is sufficient, the action must be necessary, establishing the foundation of jabr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your actions are necessitated by God-created conditions, what does that mean for the times you blame yourself for failures? Are you truly responsible, or is the blame misplaced? How does this affect your repentance?
?
This proof argues that when conditions are sufficient, the action must occur. Consider a sin you have committed. Do you believe that at that moment the conditions were sufficient? How does the concept of jabr challenge your sense of guilt and your reliance on Allah's mercy?
?
How can you reconcile the feeling of personal effort with the teaching that your actions are necessary? Does this make you more humble or more passive? What specific change in your daily life does this truth demand?

5 min
The Second Case: Insufficiency and the Inevitability of Jabr
Second part of the eighth proof: alternative arguments for jabr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author shows that even if the totality of conditions is not sufficient, the conclusion of jabr still follows through another line of reasoning.

Core Idea
Whether conditions are sufficient or not, the logic forces us to accept jabr as inevitable.

After establishing that if conditions are sufficient the action is necessary, the author considers the alternative: what if the conditions are not sufficient? He explores two possibilities: that the additional factor is the servant's own act (leading to regress) or that it is God's act (leading to necessity). In both cases, the conclusion that the action is necessitated (jabr) re-emerges.

Guided Path
1
Read the remainder of the proof
Read the passage from 'وأيضاً: إذا كان حصول الأثر' to the end. Notice it contains two arguments: a second argument from coincidence, and the second division (القسم الثاني).
The author uses additional reasoning to reinforce the same conclusion.
2
Analyze the second argument
The author argues that if the action is not necessary despite sufficient conditions, then its occurrence is pure coincidence, which also implies jabr.
Coincidence means no human control, which is still compulsion.
3
Understand the second division
If the totality is not sufficient, an additional factor is needed. That factor cannot be the servant's own act (regress), so it must be God's act. Once that act is added, the conditions are complete, and the action becomes necessary again.
Thus, regardless of the case, necessity (jabr) follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثامن
حدث بقدرة العبد شيء، لامتنع أن يقال: إنه إنما صدر عنه هذا الفعل، لأنه صدر عنه فعل آخر. وإلا لزم التسلسل وهو محال. بل لا بد وأن تنتهي أفعال العبد إلى فعل أول صدر عنه ذلك الفعل، لا بواسطة فعل آخر منه، فنقول: صدور ذلك الفعل عنه، لا بد وأن يكون لأجل اتصاف ذاته بصفات حصلت بتخليق الله تعالى فنقول: ذاته مع مجموع تلك الأمور المعتبرة في كونه موجداً لذلك. إما أن يكون كافياً في صدور ذلك الأثر عنه، أو لا يكون. فإن كان كافياً، كان الفعل مع واجباً. إذ لو لم يجب، لجاز أن يتخلف. ولو جاز أن يتخلف، لكان صدور الأثر عن ذلك المجموع ممكناً، لا واجباً. فكان يفتقر إلى زائد آخر. ولو كان كذلك، لما كان الحاصل قبل ذلك كافياً في صدور الأثر عنه. وكنا قد فرضناه كذلك. هذا خلف. فثبت: أن حصول الأثر عند ذلك المجموع، يكون واجباً. وهو الجبر.

وأيضاً: إذا كان حصول الأثر عند حصول ذلك المجموع غير واجب، بل يجوز أن يحصل الفعل تارة، وأن لا يحصل أخرى، ولم يكن تميز حصول الفعل عن لا حصوله، بأمر آخر يضمه العبد إلى المجموع، الذي كان حاصلا. فحينئذ يكون حصول الفعل محض الاتفاق. وهذا أيضاً محض الجبر.

وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يقال: ذات العبد مع مجموع تلك الأمور: غير كاف في كونها مصدراً لذلك الفعل. فحينئذ لا بد من ضم شيء آخر إليه، وذلك الأمر الآخر، إن كان فعلا له، فهو محال وإلا لزم أن يحصل فعل، قبل حصول أول الأفعال. وذلك محال. وإن لم يكن فعلاً له، بل كان فعلا الله تعالى، فعند حصوله قد تم كل ما لا بد منه في كونه مصدراً لأفعاله. فإما أن يكون صدور أفعاله عنه واجباً، أو لا يكون. وحينئذ يعود التقسيم المذكور بعينه. فثبت: أن حصول الفعل عند حصول كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية: واجب، وثبت: أن عند فقدان كل تلك الأمور، وعند فقدان بعضها، يكون صدور الفعل عن العبد ممتنعاً. وعلى هذا التقدير، فإنه يكون القول بالجبر لازماً.

وبالله التوفيق
The eighth proof: If something occurs by the power of the servant, it would be impossible to say: He only performed this action because he performed another action. Otherwise, infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. Rather, the actions of the servant must end with a first action that originated from him without mediation of another action from him. So we say: The origination of that action from him must necessarily be due to his essence being qualified by attributes that came into being through the creation of Allah, the Exalted. So we say: His essence together with the totality of those matters considered in his being an originator of that. Either it is sufficient for the occurrence of that effect from him, or it is not. If it is sufficient, then the action with it is necessary. For if it were not necessary, it would be possible for it to be absent. And if it were possible for it to be absent, then the occurrence of the effect from that totality would be possible, not necessary. Then it would need another additional factor. And if that were the case, that which existed before would not have been sufficient for the occurrence of the effect from him. And we had assumed it to be so. This is a contradiction. Thus it is established that the occurrence of the effect upon that totality is necessary. And that is jabr (compulsion). And also: If the occurrence of the effect upon the occurrence of that totality is not necessary, but rather it is possible for the action to occur sometimes and not to occur at other times, and there is no distinction between the occurrence of the action and its non-occurrence by another matter that the servant adds to the totality that existed. Then the occurrence of the action would be pure coincidence. And this too is pure jabr. As for the second division: That it is said: The essence of the servant together with the totality of those matters is not sufficient for it to be the source of that action. Then it is necessary to attach something else to it. And that other matter, if it is an act of his, it is impossible, for then an action would occur before the occurrence of the first action, and that is impossible. And if it is not an act of his, but rather an act of Allah the Exalted, then upon its occurrence everything necessary for him to be the source of his actions has been completed. Then either the occurrence of his actions from him is necessary, or it is not. And then the aforementioned division returns exactly as it was. Thus it is established that the occurrence of the action upon the occurrence of everything necessary for effectiveness (al-mu'aththiriyya) is necessary. And it is established that when all those matters are absent, or some of them are absent, the occurrence of the action from the servant is impossible. And on this assumption, the assertion of jabr becomes necessary. And with Allah is the success.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a proof (al-burhan al-thamin) demonstrating that human actions are necessarily determined (jabr). The author begins by considering a scenario where an action occurs by the servant's power. He argues that if an action occurs, it cannot be traced back to another action ad infinitum, so there must be a first act. That first act requires the servant's essence to be qualified with attributes created by Allah. Then the author presents a division: either the totality of conditions (the essence plus these attributes) is sufficient for the action or not. If sufficient, the action is necessary—if it were not necessary, it could fail to occur, meaning the conditions were not actually sufficient, contradicting the assumption. Therefore, necessity (jabr) is proven. Then the author considers the second possibility: the totality is not sufficient. In that case, an additional factor is needed. If that factor is the servant's own act, it leads to regress. If it is Allah's act, then the servant's action becomes necessary upon the completion of all conditions, again leading to jabr. The proof systematically eliminates alternatives and concludes that the servant's actions are necessitated by God's creation, which is the position of jabr.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr)
الاتفاق (al-ittifaq)
المؤثرية (al-mu'aththiriyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the action is by coincidence, humans have free will because it is uncaused.
Correction: The author shows that coincidence means no human control, which is a form of compulsion, not freedom.

Checkpoint
In the second division of the eighth proof, if the totality is insufficient, what cannot be the additional factor?
A) An act of God
B) An act of the servant
C) A coincidence
D) A previous action

Key Takeaway
Even if conditions are not sufficient, the logic leads back to necessity (jabr) once the missing factor is supplied by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider a good deed you performed recently, can you honestly say you were the sole initiator? Or did it only occur after Allah provided every condition—your existence, ability, opportunity, and will? If everything is necessitated, how does that reshape your gratitude and humility before Him?
?
If all actions are ultimately compelled, what happens to your sense of personal responsibility? Do you find yourself tempted to abandon effort, or does it drive you to depend more deeply on Allah's mercy? Which response aligns with the truth, and what will you choose today?
?
The author argues that without necessity, actions become mere coincidence. Does your own experience of making choices feel like coincidence or necessity? If it feels like choice, how can you reconcile that feeling with the logical proof? Is your conviction based on evidence or emotion, and what would it cost you to submit to the truth?

The Kalām Argument for Exclusive Divine Creation
The author presents the opponents' two-part argument for exclusive divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the classical kalām argument that only God can create bodies and life, including its two main divisions and supporting proofs.

Core Idea
The kalām argument claims created power cannot produce bodies, but its reasoning is flawed.

Before critiquing the opponents' position, the author faithfully presents their argument. This argument uses a dichotomy: either the creator's power is essential or acquired, and both are deemed impossible. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the subsequent refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'They said' to 'this is the gist'. Focus on the logical flow: the agent's power must be either by essence or by power, and both are impossible.
Notice the two divisions (qismān) and the two supporting proofs.
2
Analyze the first division
The first division claims the agent cannot be powerful by essence because it would have to be a body, and all bodies are similar, so one body's essential power would necessitate all bodies have it – which is false.
The principle 'the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like' is key.
3
Analyze the second division
The second division argues the agent cannot be powerful by acquired power because observed human power cannot create bodies. This incapacity is a common attribute of all powers, so no power can create bodies.
Two sub-proofs are given: the common attribute argument and the similarity/difference argument.
4
Identify the conclusion
The opponents conclude that only God can create bodies and life, and this is their foundation for proving miracles from God.
The author will later dismiss this entire argument as extremely weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : الدليل دل على أن غير الله لا يقدر على خلق الجسم والحياة وتقرير ذلك الدليل ، أن نقول : لو قدر أحد من الخلق على ذلك ، لكان ذلك القدر . إما أن يكون قادراً لذاته، وإما أن يكون قادراً بالقدرة . والقسمان باطلان ، فبطل القول بكون أحد من الخلق قادراً على خلق الجسم ، وعلى خلق الحياة ، وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون قادراً لذاته ، لأن ذلك القادر لا بد وأن يكون جسماً - والأجسام متماثلة - فلو كان الجسم قادراً لذاته، لكان كل كذلك . ضرورة أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله . ولما بطل هذا ، علمنا : أنه لا يجوز أن يكون قادراً لذاته وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون قادراً بالقدرة . لأن القدرة لا تصلح بفعل الجسم . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إن القدرة التي لنا ، لا تصلح لفعل الأجسام ، فهذا الامتناع حكم مشترك فيه بين القدرة التي لنا. والحكم المشترك لا بد من تعليله بوصف مشترك فيه . وما ذاك إلا كونها قدرة . فثبت : أن كون القدرة قدرة : مانعة من صلاحية إيجاد الجسم. فوجب أن تكون كل قدرة كذلك الثاني : إن كل قدرة نفرضها . فهي إما أن تكون مثلاً لهذه القدرة الحاصلة لنا ، أو مخالفاً لها. فإن كانت مثلاً لهذه القدرة ، وجب أن لا تكون صالحة للإيجاد . كما أن هذه القدرة غير صالحة للإيجاد . ضرورة أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله . وإن كانت مخالفة لهذه القدرة ، لم تكن مخالفتها لهذه القدرة أشد من مخالفة بعض هذه القدر لبعض . فلما كانت هذه القدر مع كون بعضها مخالفاً للبعض ، لا يصلح للإيجاد . فكذلك تلك القدرة المفروضة ، وجب أن لا تصلح للإيجاد هذا حاصل ما لخصه القوم ، وعولوا عليه ، في أن فاعل المعجزات هو الله تعالى . وهو كلام في غاية الرخاوة والسقوط. فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الفاعل قادراً لذاته لكان كل جسم كذلك . ضرورة أن الأجسام متماثلة ، لكنا لا نسلم أن ذلك الفاعل يجب أن يكون جسماً . وذلك لأن الفلاسفة يثبتون موجودات ليست متحيزة ولا حالة في المتحيز كالعقول والنفوس الفلكية ، والنفوس الناطقة . فما لم تقيموا الدلالة على فساد هذا التقسيم ، لا ندرك الجزم بأن ذلك الفاعل ، يجب أن يكون جسماً . والمتكلمون ما ذكروا في ابطال هذا القسم كلاماً يصلح أن يلتفت إليه بـل نقول : لم لا يجوز أن تكون النفس الناطقة التي لهذا الشيء ، هي التي أحدثت هذا المعجز . وما كانت نفسه مخالفة لسائر النفوس في الماهية ، لم يلزم من قدرته على تلك الأفعال قدرة غيره عليها ؟ . وبالجملة : فهذا الاحتمال لا يندفع إلا باقامة الدلالة على أن كل ما سوى الله تعالى ، فهو إما متحيز ، أو حال فيه سلمنا : أن ذلك الفاعل يجب أن يكون جسماً . فلم قلتم : إن الاجسام كلها متماثلة ؟ وما الدلالة على ذلك ؟ وتقريره : إن المعلوم : هو أن الأجسام متساوية في وجوب الحصول في الحيز ، وفي كونها بحيث يمنع غيرها عن أن يحصل بحيث هو ، وفي كونها قابلة للأعراض. إلا أن ذلك إشارة إلى الاحكام واللوازم . وقد ثبت : أن الأشياء المختلفة في الماهيات ، لا يمتنع اشتراكها في لازم واحد . فلم قلتم : إن جميع الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في ماهياتها حتى يتم لكم ما ذكرتم ؟ سلمنا : أن الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في تمام الماهية . لكن لم قلتم : إن الأفراد المتساوية في تمام الماهية ، يجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها ما يصح على الآخر ؟ : بيانه : وهو أن تلك الأفراد ، وإن كانت متساوية في الماهية ، لكنها مختلفة في التشخص والتعين ، وإلا لكان كل واحد عين الآخر . وحينئذ لا يبقى التعدد . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون
They said: The proof indicates that other than God is not capable of creating the body and life. And the elaboration of that proof: we say: If one of the creatures were capable of that, then that capability would either be powerful by essence, or powerful by power (qudra). And both divisions are invalid, so the statement that any of the creatures is capable of creating the body and creating life is invalid. And we said: It is not permissible for it to be powerful by essence, because that powerful being must be a body — and bodies are similar — so if the body were powerful by essence, then every [body] would be [powerful] likewise, necessarily because the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like. And when this became invalid, we knew that it is not permissible for it to be powerful by essence. And we said: It is not permissible for it to be powerful by power, because power is not suitable for the act of the body. And two aspects indicate this: The first: The power that we have is not suitable for the act of bodies, so this impossibility is a common ruling between the power that we have. And a common ruling must be caused by a common attribute, and that is nothing other than its being power. So it is established that being a power prevents suitability for bringing the body into existence. So it is necessary that every power be like that. The second: Every power we suppose is either similar to this power we possess or different from it. If it is similar to this power, then it must not be suitable for bringing into existence, just as this power is not suitable for bringing into existence, necessarily because the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like. And if it is different from this power, its difference from this power is not more severe than the difference of some of these powers from others. So since these powers, despite some being different from others, are not suitable for bringing into existence, then likewise that supposed power must not be suitable for bringing into existence. This is the gist of what the folk summarized and relied upon regarding that the agent of miracles is God Almighty. And it is speech at the utmost degree of weakness and fallaciousness. So we say: Why is it not permissible for that agent to be powerful by essence, then every body would be [powerful] likewise, necessarily because bodies are similar? But we do not concede that that agent must be a body. That is because the philosophers affirm existents that are not occupying space (mutahayyiz) and not inhering in what occupies space, such as the intellects, the celestial souls, and the rational souls. So as long as you have not established the proof of the corruption of this division, we do not attain certainty that that agent must be a body. And the mutakallimun did not mention in refuting this division speech worthy of attention. Rather we say: Why is it not permissible that the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) that belongs to this thing is what brought about this miracle? And since its soul is different from other souls in essence, it does not follow from its power over those actions the power of another over them? In summary, this possibility is not repelled except by establishing the proof that everything other than God Almighty is either occupying space or inhering in it. We concede that that agent must be a body. Then why do you say that all bodies are similar? And what is the proof of that? And its elaboration: The known is that bodies are equal in the necessity of being in place, and in being such that they prevent others from being where they are, and in being receptive of accidents. But that refers to judgments and concomitants. And it has been established that things different in essences are not prevented from sharing a single concomitant. So why do you say that all bodies entirely are equal in their essences so that what you mentioned is established for you? We concede that all bodies entirely are equal in complete essence. But why do you say that individuals equal in complete essence must have what is valid for one valid for the other? Its explanation: Those individuals, even if they are equal in essence, are different in individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) and determination (ta‘ayyun), otherwise each one would be the same as the other, and then multiplicity would not remain. And if it is so, then why is it not permissible that it be...
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a classical kalām proof intended to demonstrate that only God can create bodies and life. The author first presents the opponents' argument in detail, then critiques it step by step. The opponents' argument runs as follows: If a creature could create a body or life, its power to do so must either be essential (by its own essence) or acquired (by a power inhering in it). Both options are impossible. The essential option fails because the agent would have to be a body, and all bodies are similar, so if one body were powerful by essence, all would be, which is false. The acquired option fails because the power we observe in creatures cannot produce bodies; this is a shared attribute of all powers, so no power can produce a body. The author then dismisses this argument as extremely weak. The refutation begins by questioning the premise that the agent must be a body. The author cites the philosophers' view that there are non-material existents like intellects and souls that are not bodies. Unless the opponent can prove that all existents other than God are bodies or accidents, the argument fails. Furthermore, the author suggests that the rational soul itself could be the agent of miracles, and its uniqueness prevents generalization from other souls. The author then concedes (sallamnā) for argument's sake that the agent is a body, but challenges the claim that all bodies are identical in essence. Bodies share certain properties (e.g., being in place, preventing occupancy, receiving accidents) but these are merely concomitants, not essences. Things can differ in essence while sharing a concomitant. Even if bodies share the same essence, individuals differ in individuation, so what is possible for one may not be possible for another. Thus the original proof is thoroughly dismantled.

Key Terms
قدرة
جسم
متماثلة
قدرة لذاتها
قدرة بالقدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first division in the opponents' argument about the possible source of creative power?
A) Power by knowledge or power by will
B) Power by essence or power by acquired power
C) Power by nature or power by choice
D) Power by God or power by creatures

Key Takeaway
The kalām argument uses a dichotomy of essential vs. acquired power and the principle of similitude to conclude that no creature can create bodies or life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument says no created power can bring a body into existence. When you face a situation that feels impossible, do you truly believe that only God can create change, or do you still rely on your own efforts as the ultimate cause? What would it mean for you to live as if every genuine transformation is from God alone?
?
The argument uses the principle that similar things have similar rulings. Consider your own heart: if you are similar to others in your humanness, what does that say about your innate inability to change your own nature? Have you been trying to purify yourself by your own power, or have you recognized that only God can create true spiritual life?
?
The opponents in this passage argued that if one body were powerful by essence, all would be. Think about your own attitude toward God's power: do you sometimes act as if you have a piece of that power—as if you can independently create your own success or virtue? What would it practically mean to confess that you have no power to create even a single atom?

Refutation: The Agent Need Not Be a Body
The author begins his refutation by rejecting the necessity of a bodily agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the kalām argument by challenging the premise that the agent of creation must be a body, using the philosophers' concept of immaterial existents.

Core Idea
The agent of creation could be a non-bodily entity like a rational soul, undermining the argument's first step.

The author begins his refutation by dismissing the opponents' argument as extremely weak. He questions the necessity that the agent be a body, pointing to the philosophers' affirmation of immaterial beings such as intellects and souls. This directly attacks the foundation of the first division of the opponents' argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the beginning of the refutation
Start at 'He is speech...' and read until the point where the author concedes that the agent must be a body ('we concede that...'). Note the author's dismissive tone and the alternative he proposes.
The author calls the opponents' argument 'extremely weak and fallacious'.
2
Identify the first objection
The author argues that the agent need not be a body because philosophers affirm existents that are neither spatial nor inhering in space, such as intellects and celestial souls.
This challenges the premise that only bodies can be agents.
3
Consider the alternative: rational soul
The author suggests that the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) could be the agent of miracles, and its uniqueness prevents generalization from other souls.
The soul's essence differs from other souls, so its power does not imply others have it.
4
Summarize the refutation's strategy
The author concludes that this possibility can only be refuted by proving that all things other than God are either bodies or accidents.
The opponents have not provided such proof.

Source Text
