إشارة إلى الاحكام واللوازم . وقد ثبت : أن الأشياء المختلفة في الماهيات ، لا يمتنع اشتراكها في لازم واحد . فلم قلتم : إن جميع الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في ماهياتها حتى يتم لكم ما ذكرتم ؟ سلمنا : أن الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في تمام الماهية . لكن لم قلتم : إن الأفراد المتساوية في تمام الماهية ، يجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها ما يصح على الآخر ؟ : بيانه : وهو أن تلك الأفراد ، وإن كانت متساوية في الماهية ، لكنها مختلفة في التشخص والتعين ، وإلا لكان كل واحد عين الآخر . وحينئذ لا يبقى التعدد . وإذا كان كذلك ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون
They said: The proof indicates that other than God is not capable of creating the body and life. And the elaboration of that proof: we say: If one of the creatures were capable of that, then that capability would either be powerful by essence, or powerful by power (qudra). And both divisions are invalid, so the statement that any of the creatures is capable of creating the body and creating life is invalid. And we said: It is not permissible for it to be powerful by essence, because that powerful being must be a body — and bodies are similar — so if the body were powerful by essence, then every [body] would be [powerful] likewise, necessarily because the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like. And when this became invalid, we knew that it is not permissible for it to be powerful by essence. And we said: It is not permissible for it to be powerful by power, because power is not suitable for the act of the body. And two aspects indicate this: The first: The power that we have is not suitable for the act of bodies, so this impossibility is a common ruling between the power that we have. And a common ruling must be caused by a common attribute, and that is nothing other than its being power. So it is established that being a power prevents suitability for bringing the body into existence. So it is necessary that every power be like that. The second: Every power we suppose is either similar to this power we possess or different from it. If it is similar to this power, then it must not be suitable for bringing into existence, just as this power is not suitable for bringing into existence, necessarily because the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like. And if it is different from this power, its difference from this power is not more severe than the difference of some of these powers from others. So since these powers, despite some being different from others, are not suitable for bringing into existence, then likewise that supposed power must not be suitable for bringing into existence. This is the gist of what the folk summarized and relied upon regarding that the agent of miracles is God Almighty. And it is speech at the utmost degree of weakness and fallaciousness. So we say: Why is it not permissible for that agent to be powerful by essence, then every body would be [powerful] likewise, necessarily because bodies are similar? But we do not concede that that agent must be a body. That is because the philosophers affirm existents that are not occupying space (mutahayyiz) and not inhering in what occupies space, such as the intellects, the celestial souls, and the rational souls. So as long as you have not established the proof of the corruption of this division, we do not attain certainty that that agent must be a body. And the mutakallimun did not mention in refuting this division speech worthy of attention. Rather we say: Why is it not permissible that the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) that belongs to this thing is what brought about this miracle? And since its soul is different from other souls in essence, it does not follow from its power over those actions the power of another over them? In summary, this possibility is not repelled except by establishing the proof that everything other than God Almighty is either occupying space or inhering in it. We concede that that agent must be a body. Then why do you say that all bodies are similar? And what is the proof of that? And its elaboration: The known is that bodies are equal in the necessity of being in place, and in being such that they prevent others from being where they are, and in being receptive of accidents. But that refers to judgments and concomitants. And it has been established that things different in essences are not prevented from sharing a single concomitant. So why do you say that all bodies entirely are equal in their essences so that what you mentioned is established for you? We concede that all bodies entirely are equal in complete essence. But why do you say that individuals equal in complete essence must have what is valid for one valid for the other? Its explanation: Those individuals, even if they are equal in essence, are different in individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) and determination (ta‘ayyun), otherwise each one would be the same as the other, and then multiplicity would not remain. And if it is so, then why is it not permissible that it be...
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a classical kalām proof intended to demonstrate that only God can create bodies and life. The author first presents the opponents' argument in detail, then critiques it step by step. The opponents' argument runs as follows: If a creature could create a body or life, its power to do so must either be essential (by its own essence) or acquired (by a power inhering in it). Both options are impossible. The essential option fails because the agent would have to be a body, and all bodies are similar, so if one body were powerful by essence, all would be, which is false. The acquired option fails because the power we observe in creatures cannot produce bodies; this is a shared attribute of all powers, so no power can produce a body. The author then dismisses this argument as extremely weak. The refutation begins by questioning the premise that the agent must be a body. The author cites the philosophers' view that there are non-material existents like intellects and souls that are not bodies. Unless the opponent can prove that all existents other than God are bodies or accidents, the argument fails. Furthermore, the author suggests that the rational soul itself could be the agent of miracles, and its uniqueness prevents generalization from other souls. The author then concedes (sallamnā) for argument's sake that the agent is a body, but challenges the claim that all bodies are identical in essence. Bodies share certain properties (e.g., being in place, preventing occupancy, receiving accidents) but these are merely concomitants, not essences. Things can differ in essence while sharing a concomitant. Even if bodies share the same essence, individuals differ in individuation, so what is possible for one may not be possible for another. Thus the original proof is thoroughly dismantled.

?
The author argues that a rational soul can perform miracles. If your soul can be a conduit for God's power, what does that demand of you today? Are you content with a purely materialistic mindset, or will you train your soul to be worthy of such agency?
?
You often assume that power lies in bodies—in strength, wealth, or physical means. But this lesson says the true agent could be non-bodily. Where in your life are you relying on outward appearances, ignoring the immaterial reality of your soul? Will you shift your focus to the unseen?

Rational Judgments

Refutation: Challenging the Similarity of Bodies
The author challenges the premises of body similarity and identical capacities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author challenges the claims that all bodies are identical in essence and that identical essence implies identical capacities, using examples of shared concomitants and individuation.

Core Idea
Bodies share some properties but not essences; even with identical essence, individuation prevents identical capacities.

Having conceded for argument that the agent is a body, the author now attacks two further premises: that all bodies are similar in essence, and that if they were, they would have the same capacities. He uses logical distinctions between essence and concomitant, and between essence and individuation, to undermine the opponents' reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the concession and new questions
Start at 'We concede that the agent must be a body. Then why do you say that all bodies are similar?' Read through the rest to see the author's two main challenges.
The author uses the phrase 'We concede' (sallamnā) to move the argument forward.
2
First challenge: similarity of bodies
The author argues that bodies share certain properties (being in place, preventing occupancy, receiving accidents) but these are concomitant properties, not the essence. Things with different essences can share a concomitant.
This undermines the claim that bodies are identical in essence.
3
Second challenge: essence vs. individuation
Even if bodies share the same essence, individuals differ in individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) and determination (ta‘ayyun). Without difference, there would be no multiplicity. So what is possible for one may not be possible for another.
This blocks the inference from one body's capacity to all bodies.
4
Recognize the incomplete ending
The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: the opponents have not proven that all bodies have identical capacities.
The author likely continues with further objections.

قالوا : الدليل دل على أن غير الله لا يقدر على خلق الجسم والحياة وتقرير ذلك الدليل ، أن نقول : لو قدر أحد من الخلق على ذلك ، لكان ذلك القدر . إما أن يكون قادراً لذاته، وإما أن يكون قادراً بالقدرة . غاية الرخاوة والسقوط. فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الفاعل قادراً لذاته لكان كل جسم كذلك . ضرورة أن الأجسام متماثلة ، لكنا لا نسلم أن ذلك الفاعل يجب أن يكون جسماً . وذلك لأن الفلاسفة يثبتون موجودات ليست متحيزة ولا حالة في المتحيز كالعقول والنفوس الفلكية ، والنفوس الناطقة . فما لم تقيموا الدلالة على فساد هذا التقسيم ، لا ندرك الجزم بأن ذلك الفاعل ، يجب أن يكون جسماً . والمتكلمون ما ذكروا في ابطال Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a classical kalām proof intended to demonstrate that only God can create bodies and life. The author first presents the opponents' argument in detail, then critiques it step by step. The opponents' argument runs as follows: If a creature could create a body or life, its power to do so must either be essential (by its own essence) or acquired (by a power inhering in it). The acquired option fails because the power we observe in creatures cannot produce bodies; this is a shared attribute of all powers, so no power can produce a body. The author then dismisses this argument as extremely weak. The refutation begins by questioning the premise that the agent must be a body. The author cites the philosophers' view that there are non-material existents like intellects and souls that are not bodies. Unless the opponent can prove that all existents other than God are bodies or accidents, the argument fails. Furthermore, the author suggests that the rational soul itself could be the agent of miracles, and its uniqueness prevents generalization from other souls. The author then concedes (sallamnā) for argument's sake that the agent is a body, but challenges the claim that all bodies are identical in essence. Bodies share certain properties (e.g., being in place, preventing occupancy, receiving accidents) but these are merely concomitants, not essences. Thus the original proof is thoroughly dismantled.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All bodies are identical in essence and therefore must have identical capacities.
Correction: Bodies share only concomitant properties, not essences; even if essences were identical, individuation allows for different capacities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

God Must Know His Actions: The First Premise
Razi presents the first premise: God must know His own actions, with Quranic and rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the creator of an action must have knowledge of its details, as proven by the Quran and rational argument.

Core Idea
Creation implies knowledge: the One who originates actions must know them intimately.

Razi begins his argument by establishing a foundational premise: the creator of an action must know its details. He supports this with both a Quranic verse and a rational proof from the nature of choice and intention. This lesson unpacks that premise and its two lines of evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a conditional premise followed by two proofs.
Pay attention to the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
2
Identify the two types of evidence
Locate the Quranic proof (القرآن) and the rational proof (البرهان). Understand that both serve to support the same conclusion.
The Quranic proof is a direct verse; the rational proof is a logical argument.
3
Understand the rational proof step by step
Follow the rational proof: human capacity for variation, specification by the chooser, necessity of intention for specification, and intention requiring knowledge. Connect each step to the conclusion.
This proof shows that creation necessarily involves intention and thus knowledge.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how the conclusion 'the creator must know what it creates' applies to God. Reflect on why this premise is important for the broader argument.
This premise is foundational for discussions on divine attributes and action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقدمة الأولى : وهي قولنا : لو كان موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لكان عالماً بتفاصيل نفسه وهو غير عالم بتفاصيل نفسه ، فوجب أن لا يكون موجداً لأفعال نفسه فيدل عليه القرآن والبرهان. أما القرآن : فهو قوله : ( ألا يعلم من خلق ) ؟ استكبر أن يكون الخالق للشيء ، غير عالم به. وأما البرهان : فهو إن العبد يمكنه ان يأتي بالأزيد مما أتى به ، وبالأنقص عما أتى به ، وبفعل آخر مغاير لما أتى به. فلما كان قادراً على الكل ، كان رجحان بعض هذه الممكنات على البعض، لا بد أن يكون لأجل أن القادر المختار : خصص ذلك النوع ، وذلك المقدار بالوقوع . دون المغاير ، ودون الأزيد والأنقص . لكن القصد إلى إيقاع الشيء بقدر خاص ، وكيفية خاص مشروط بالعلم بذلك القدر. لأن القصد إلى الشيء ، بدون الشعور بماهيته : محال . فثبت : أن خالق الشيء لا بد وأن يكون قاصداً إليه ، وثبت : أن القاصد إلى الشيء عالم بماهية ذلك الشيء ، الذي قصد إليه . وذلك يدل : على أن خالق الشيء ، لا بد وأن يكون عالماً به.
As for the first premise: it is our statement: If He were the one who originates His own actions, then He would be knowledgeable of the details of His own actions. But He is not knowledgeable of the details of His own actions, so it must be that He is not the originator of His own actions. The Qur'an and the proof indicate this. As for the Qur'an: it is His statement: 'Does He who creates not know?' It is too great that the creator of a thing be not knowledgeable of it. As for the proof: it is that the servant can produce more than what he produced, or less than what he produced, or a different act from what he produced. Since he is capable of all, the preponderance of some of these possibilities over others must be because the powerful chooser specified that type and that amount to occur, to the exclusion of the different, the more, and the less. But intending to bring about a thing in a specific measure and specific modality is conditional upon knowledge of that measure. Because intending a thing without awareness of its quiddity is impossible. Therefore it is established that the creator of a thing must be intending it, and it is established that the one who intends a thing is knowledgeable of the quiddity of that thing which he intended. And that indicates that the creator of a thing must be knowledgeable of it.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents the first premise of an argument concerning divine knowledge and action. It is a conditional proof. The premise is stated hypothetically: if God were the originator of His own actions, then He would know their details; but He does not know them, therefore He is not the originator. This is then supported by two types of evidence: Quranic and rational. The Quranic evidence is the verse 'Does He who creates not know?' implying that the Creator must know His creation. The rational proof proceeds as follows: a human agent can choose to perform an act that is more, less, or different from what he actually did. Since he is capable of all, the actual occurrence of one particular possibility must be due to the agent's choice specifying that specific type and amount. For choice to be directed to a specific detail, the agent must have knowledge of that detail. Thus, any creator must have knowledge of what it creates. This establishes the necessary connection between creation and knowledge.

Key Terms
مقدمة
موجد
قادر مختار
قصد
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Quranic verse alone suffices without needing the rational proof.
Correction: Razi includes a rational proof to show the logical necessity, demonstrating that the conclusion follows from reason as well as revelation.

Checkpoint
What does the rational proof in the first premise establish about the creator?
A) The creator must be powerful.
B) The creator must be knowledgeable of what it creates.
C) The creator must be eternal.
D) The creator must be unique.

Key Takeaway
The creator of any action must have knowledge of its details, because intention requires knowledge, and creation involves intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every action you perform is known in detail by God because He created it, how would it change what you do today?
?
The proof shows that intention requires knowledge. Reflect on an action you performed recently without really 'intending' it with full awareness. How does that relate to the connection between creation and knowledge?
?
The Quranic verse asks 'Does He who creates not know?' Is there any area of your life where you act as if God does not know the details? How would acknowledging His knowledge change that behavior?

Second Premise: Human Ignorance of Their Own Actions
Razi presents the second premise with two proofs from human experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's second premise that humans lack detailed knowledge of their own voluntary actions, and his two proofs from the cases of the sleeping/inattentive and the analysis of bodily movement.

Core Idea
You do not know the details of your own actions — a key premise in the kalām proof of God's absolute power.

Al-Razi now moves to the second of his premises: that human beings are not fully cognizant of the details of their own actions. This might seem counterintuitive — we think we know what we are doing. But Razi offers two careful arguments to show that our awareness is always incomplete. Understanding this premise is essential for the later conclusion that the real agent of every action is not the human but God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the translation to get the full meaning. Let the author's own structure come through before you interpret.
Notice how Razi uses a very analytical, almost mathematical style.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage contains two distinct arguments: the sleeping/inattentive example, and the detailed analysis of voluntary movement. Find each one and see how they support the premise.
3
Analyze the second proof in detail
The movement proof has three sub-arguments: ignorance of body parts, ignorance of spatial positions, and ignorance of time moments. Trace each step in the passage.
Razi counts arguments from atoms or simple organs, to places, to moments.
4
Connect to the overall argument
Understand how this premise fits into the larger kalām argument: if humans lack detailed knowledge, they cannot be the ultimate agents.
This premise is a stepping stone to proving God's exclusive agency.
5
Avoid a common misunderstanding
Note that the argument does not deny human free will or voluntary action; it only denies comprehensive knowledge of the details of those actions.
Razi is not saying humans do not act; he is saying they do not know everything about their act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي قولنا : إن العبد غير عالم بتفاصيل أفعال نفسه . فيدل عليه وجوه : الأول: إن النائم والساهي قد يصدر عنهم كثير من الأفعال الاختيارية، مع أنه لا شعور لهم بتفاصيل تلك الأفعال لا بكميتها ولا بكيفيتها الثاني : إن الإنسان إذا حرك بدنه وجثته ، فلا شك أن بدنه مؤلف من أجزاء كثيرة موجودة بالفعل . أما عند من يثبت الجوهر الفرد ، فلا شك فيه . وأما عند من ينكره ، فلا شك أنه معترف بأن مجموع البدن ، مؤلف من الأعضاء البسيطة - أعني العظام والغضاريف والأعصاب والعضلات والرباطات ، إلى غير ذلك من الأعضاء البسيطة - فإذا حرك الانسان بدنه ، فلا معنى لهذا التحريك إلا أنه حرك مجموع تلك الأجزاء ، لكنا نعلم بالضرورة أنه البتة غير عالم بأعداد تلك الأعضاء. وأيضاً : فلا شك أنه لما حرك بدنه ، فقد نقل تلك الجثة من حيز إلى حيز ، ومر بما بين الطرفين ، مع أنه غير عالم بأعداد تلك الأحياز فلا يمكنه أن يعلم ما بين مبدأ تلك الحركة إلى منتهاها . وأيضاً : فلا شك أن تلك الحركة وقعت في مقدار معين من الزمان، وذلك القدر المعين من الزمان ، مركب من آنات متتالية متعاقبة. وهو البتة لا يعلم مقدار الزمان ، ولا عدد الآنات ، التي منها تركب ذلك الزمان . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن من انتقل من مكان إلى مكان ، فهو لم يعلم أن الأجزاء التي حركها . كم هي ؟ والأحياز التي منها تألفت تلك المسافة التي فيها وقعت الحركة . كم هي ؟ والآنات التي منها تألف الزمان ، الذي هو طرف لتلك الحركة. كم هي فثبت : أن العبد غير عالم بتفاصيل أفعاله البتة
As for the second premise: it is our statement: The servant is not knowledgeable of the details of his own actions. This is indicated by several aspects: First: The sleeping and the inattentive often perform many voluntary actions while they have no awareness of the details of those actions, neither their quantity nor their quality. Second: When a man moves his body and his frame, there is no doubt that his body is composed of many parts actually existing. As for one who affirms the indivisible atom, there is no doubt about it. As for one who denies it, there is no doubt that he acknowledges that the totality of the body is composed of simple limbs—namely bones, cartilages, nerves, muscles, ligaments, and other such simple limbs. So when a man moves his body, this movement means nothing other than that he moved all those parts. Yet we know necessarily that he is absolutely not knowledgeable of the numbers of those limbs. Also, there is no doubt that when he moved his body, he transferred that frame from one place to another and passed through the intermediate spaces, while he is not knowledgeable of the numbers of those places. So he cannot know what lies between the beginning of that movement and its end. Also, there is no doubt that that movement occurred in a specific amount of time, and that specific amount of time is composed of successive consecutive moments. He is absolutely not knowledgeable of the amount of time nor the number of moments from which that time is composed. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that whoever moves from one place to another does not know how many are the parts he moved, nor how many are the places from which the distance in which the movement occurred was composed, nor how many are the moments from which the time, which is the measure of that movement, was composed. So it is established that the servant is absolutely not knowledgeable of the details of his actions.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents the second premise: humans are ignorant of the details of their own actions. The author provides two proofs. The first proof is the example of a sleeping or inattentive person who performs voluntary acts without awareness of their quantity or quality. The second proof is a detailed analysis of voluntary bodily movement. It argues that the body is composed of many parts (whether atoms or simple organs). When a person moves, they move all those parts simultaneously, yet they do not know the exact number of parts. Moreover, the movement involves traversing a spatial path consisting of numerous positions and occurring over time composed of countless moments. The agent is ignorant of all these numerical details. Therefore, it is impossible for the agent to have exhaustive knowledge of the details of his own action. This establishes the second premise.

Key Terms
نائم (sleeping)
ساهي (inattentive)
جوهر فرد (indivisible atom)
أجزاء (parts)
آنات (moments)
بتة (absolutely)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies human free will or the reality of voluntary action.
Correction: The argument does not deny that humans perform voluntary actions; it only denies that they have comprehensive knowledge of the quantitative and qualitative details of those actions.

Checkpoint
What do the two proofs in the second premise aim to show?
A) That humans have complete knowledge of their actions.
B) That humans lack detailed knowledge of their actions.
C) That humans are not the agents of their actions.
D) That humans cannot perform voluntary actions.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second premise establishes that humans are ignorant of the detailed quantitative and qualitative aspects of their own actions, preparing the ground for the conclusion that God is the true agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform any action, like moving your hand to pick up this screen, you are ignorant of countless details. How does that knowledge change the way you view your own ability and control over your life?
?
If even your most intentional actions contain details you are completely unaware of, what does that say about your claim to be the 'doer' of those actions? Are you willing to admit that your actions are not fully yours?
?
Razi's argument humbles human agency. Where in your daily life do you need to remind yourself that your ignorance of details points to a greater Agent? Will you let this lesson shift your reliance from yourself to God?

Proof that Ignorance Occurs by Compulsion
Al-Maturidi proves that all ignorances are necessary by regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that all ignorances occur by compulsion (idhtirār) and not by choice (ikhtiyār), as argued through a regress of premises.

Core Idea
Ignorance is not chosen; it is compelled by a chain of premises beyond our control.

This passage presents a subtle proof that ignorance (jahl) is not something we choose; rather, it is a necessary state brought about by antecedent premises. Understanding this helps clarify human responsibility in matters of knowledge. The author argues that if ignorance occurs, it must be caused, leading back to a first ignorance that arises without choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Focus on the logical flow: condition, regress, and conclusion.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Notice the opening conditional: unless premises necessitating ignorance are arranged, ignorance does not occur. This establishes that ignorance depends on its causes.
3
Follow the regress
The same reasoning applies to the premises themselves, leading to an infinite regress. This forces a first ignorance that is not caused by choice.
The regress is the key to proving necessity.
4
Understand the conclusion
The first ignorance and all subsequent ones occur by compulsion, not by the person's effort or choice. This establishes that all ignorances are necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجهل له وذلك لأنه ما لم يترتب في ذهنه مقدمات موجبة لهذا الجهل، لم يحصل في قلبه هذا الجهل. ثم الكلام في تلك المقدمات كما في هذه النتيجة. فتلك الجهالات لا بد أن تترقى إلى جهل أول وقع في قلبه. لا منه، ولا بسعي بطلب الحصول ذلك الجهل الأول في قلبه، وتأدى ذلك الجهل إلى سائر الجهالات اللازمة منه: ليس أيضاً بسعيه ولا باختياره. فثبت: أن جميع الجهالات إنما تحصل في العبد على سبيل الاضطرار، ولا على سبيل الاختيار.
Ignorance is for him, and that is because unless premises necessitating this ignorance are arranged in his mind, this ignorance does not occur in his heart. Then the discussion about those premises is like the discussion about this result. Those ignorances inevitably ascend to a first ignorance that occurred in his heart. Not from himself, nor by his seeking to obtain that first ignorance in his heart, and that ignorance led to the rest of the necessary ignorances: neither is it by his effort nor his choice. So it is established that all ignorances only occur in the slave by way of compulsion (idhtirār), and not by way of choice (ikhtiyār).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a proof that ignorance (jahl) occurs in the human being necessarily (idhtirāran) rather than voluntarily (ikhtiyāran). The author begins by stating that ignorance is 'for him' (likely meaning the servant/slave, i.e., the human). The argument proceeds: unless premises necessitating this ignorance are present in one's mind, the ignorance does not occur. Then, when we examine those premises, the same reasoning applies—they too must have been necessitated. This leads to an infinite regress of ignorances, which must be traced back to a first ignorance that arose in the heart. This first ignorance did not come from the person himself, nor from his seeking to acquire it. Since the first ignorance was not chosen, and subsequent ignorances follow necessarily from it, all ignorances are necessary. Therefore, all ignorances in the servant occur by compulsion (idhtirār), not by choice (ikhtiyār). The proof uses an argument of regress (tasalsul) to establish the necessity of the first ignorance.

Key Terms
الجهل (jahl)
الاضطرار (idhtirār)
الاختيار (ikhtiyār)
المقدمات (muqaddimāt)
النتيجة (natīja)
الجهل الأول (al-jahl al-awwal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ignorance is always blameworthy and therefore chosen.
Correction: The passage shows ignorance is necessary, not chosen, so it is not always blameworthy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relation between the first ignorance (al-jahl al-awwal) and later ignorances?
A) The first ignorance is chosen, later ones are necessary.
B) All ignorances are necessary, including the first.
C) The first ignorance is necessary, later ones are chosen.
D) Ignorances are all chosen.

Key Takeaway
All ignorances in the servant occur by compulsion, not by choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself being ignorant of an obligation to Allah, do you automatically feel guilty or blame yourself? How does this lesson challenge that reaction, and what should you actually do when you encounter your own ignorance?
?
If all ignorance is compelled, does that mean you have no responsibility to seek knowledge? How does this teaching push you to rely more on Allah's guidance while still striving to learn? What will you change in your daily routine to seek knowledge with this understanding?
?
Think of a time you felt ashamed for not knowing something about your religion. After this lesson, do you think that shame was correct? How should you now view your own lack of knowledge, and what concrete step will you take next time you realize you are ignorant of a command of Allah?

Knowledge as Conception and Assent
Classification of knowledge and first dilemma for conception acquisition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of knowledge into conception and assent, and the initial dilemma showing that conceptions cannot be acquired.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either conception without judgment or assent with judgment.

To argue that knowledge is beyond human acquisition, the author first clarifies what knowledge is. He divides it into two fundamental types: conception and assent. This lesson presents that division and then follows the first step of a proof that conceptions cannot be acquired—a dilemma that exposes the impossibility of learning new simple concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of knowledge
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author defines conception and assent based on whether a judgment is made.
These terms are technical in Islamic logic.
2
Understand the dilemma for acquiring conceptions
The author presents a dilemma: when trying to acquire a new conception, either you already have awareness of it or you don't. Trace this logic carefully.
The text cuts off mid-argument; the conclusion is not given here.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this dilemma leads to the conclusion that conceptions cannot be acquired. If you have the conception already, acquisition is pointless; if not, you can't even recognize what to seek.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثاني على هذا المطلوب

إن العلم إما تصور وإما تصديق

وذلك لأنا إذا أدركنا أمراً من الأمور، فإما أن نحكم عليه بحكم [وإما أن لا نحكم عليه بحكم] فإن لم تحكم عليه بحكم، فذاك هو التصور، وإن حكمنا عليه بحكم، فذاك هو التصديق.

إذا عرفت هذا الحصر، فنقول: إنه لا يمكن اكتساب شيء من التصورات. ويدل عليه وجوه:

الأول: إنا إذا حاولنا اكتساب شيء [آخر] من التصورات فحال ما نحاول ذلك الاكتساب، إما أن يكون لنا شعور بماهية ذلك المطلوب، أو لا يكون لنا به شعور [فإن كان لنا به شعور] فحينئذ يكون تصوره حاضراً
The Second Proof for this sought:

Knowledge is either conception or assent.

That is because when we perceive something, we either judge it with a judgment [or we do not judge it with a judgment]. If you do not judge it with a judgment, that is conception, and if you judge it with a judgment, that is assent.

If you know this classification, we say: It is impossible to acquire any of the conceptions. This is indicated by several aspects:

The first: If we attempt to acquire some [another] conception, at the time of attempting that acquisition, either we have an awareness of the quiddity of that sought or we do not have an awareness of it. [If we have an awareness of it,] then its conception is present...
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage begins the second proof for the proposition that knowledge is not within human power. The author first classifies knowledge into two types: conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). He defines them by whether a judgment is made: if we grasp something without affirming or denying anything, that is conception; if we affirm or deny, that is assent. After establishing this division, he states that it is impossible to acquire conceptions, and offers multiple 'aspects' (wujuh) as evidence. The first aspect is presented as a dilemma: when we try to acquire a new conception, either we already have an awareness (shu'ur) of its quiddity (mahiyya), or we do not. If we already have awareness, then the conception is already present, so acquisition is unnecessary. The text cuts off at this point, but the argument would continue that if we lack awareness, we cannot recognize the conception when encountered, so acquisition is impossible. This is a classic argument in Islamic epistemology.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
اكتساب
شعور
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The terms 'tasawwur' and 'tasdiq' are often misunderstood as mere 'opinion' and 'belief'.
Correction: In Islamic logic, tasawwur is a simple concept without judgment (e.g., 'chair'), while tasdiq is a judgment (e.g., 'this is a chair'). They are technical epistemological categories.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how is 'conception' (tasawwur) defined?
A) It is the act of affirming or denying something.
B) It is the grasp of something without a judgment.
C) It is the same as knowledge acquired through proof.
D) It is the awareness of the quiddity of a thing.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is divided into conception (without judgment) and assent (with judgment), and the first step of the argument shows that acquiring a conception is either redundant or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to know Allah, do you realise that your deepest conceptions of Him are not products of your effort but gifts of awareness? How does this humble your pursuit of knowledge?
?
Have you ever labored to grasp a theological concept, only to find it was already present within you, waiting to be uncovered? What does that teach you about the source of true understanding?
?
If all conception is either already present or inaccessible, what does that mean for your reliance on your own intellect versus divine illumination? Will you now ask Allah to open your heart before opening your books?

Proof that necessary knowledge is not within human power
Conclusion of the proof that necessary knowledge is not within human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessary knowledge (badihiyyat) cannot be within human power and how the proof demonstrates that all knowledge ultimately depends on divine grant.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge is not within our power — and neither is the chain of knowledge it starts.

This lesson examines the conclusion of a profound argument by the author that challenges the notion that humans have power over even the most basic knowledge. The argument shows that necessary truths are not acquired by our own striving, and that each step of knowledge derived from them also lies beyond our capacity. Understanding this establishes that all knowledge is ultimately a gift from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument come through before diving into details.
Notice the repeated use of 'ثبت' (it is established) and the phrase 'وهلم جرا' (and so forth).
2
Follow the two refutations
The author rejects two possibilities regarding acquired sciences. Identify what each possibility is and why it is false. The first is rejected because we assume necessary sciences are given; the second because it leads to an infinite regress.
Focus on the phrase 'لأنا فرضنا حصول جميع العلوم البديهية' (because we have assumed the attainment of all necessary sciences).
3
Grasp the chain argument
After refuting the possibilities, the author establishes that necessary truths, their entailment of the first acquired, and all subsequent entailments are not within human power. See how the argument extends 'وهلم جرا' (and so forth) to all degrees.
Think about why the 'first acquired' cannot have a prior acquired knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من العلوم الكسبية . والأول باطل لأنا فرضنا حصول جميع العلوم البديهية . والثاني أيضاً باطل . لأن كلامنا في كفية استلزام البديهيات لأول المكتسبات . وعلى هذا التقدير ، يلزم أن يحصل قبل أول المكتسبات : مكتسب آخر . وذلك محال . لان الذي يكون موصوفاً بأنه أول المكتسبات ، يمتنع أن يحصل قبله مكتسب آخر . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن البديهيات غير داخلة تحت القدرة . وثبت : أن استلزامها للمكتسب الأول غير داخل تحت الوسع . واستلزام المكتسب الأول للمكتسب الثاني ، غير داخل أيضاً تحت القدرة . وهلم جرا . في جميع المراتب . بالغة ما بلغت . فثبت : أن شيئاً من العلوم والمعارف غير واقع بقدرة العبد واختياره . وهو المطلوب
From the acquired sciences. The first is false because we have assumed the attainment of all necessary sciences. The second is also false because our discussion is about the manner of the entailment of necessary truths for the first acquired [knowledge]. On this assumption, it would necessitate that before the first acquired [knowledge] there occurs another acquired [knowledge], and that is impossible because that which is described as the first acquired [knowledge] cannot have another acquired [knowledge] occur before it. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that necessary truths are not within power, and that their entailment of the first acquired [knowledge] is not within capacity, and the entailment of the first acquired for the second acquired is also not within power, and so forth in all degrees, no matter how far they reach. So it is established that nothing of sciences and cognitions occurs by the power and choice of the servant. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the conclusion of a proof that necessary knowledge (badihiyyat) is not within human power. The author begins by considering two possibilities for acquired sciences, refuting both. The first possibility is false because it assumes that necessary sciences are acquired, but the argument already assumed they are given. The second possibility is false because the discussion is about how necessary truths (badihiyyat) entail the first acquired knowledge (awwal al-muktasabat). If the entailment were within human power, then before the first acquired knowledge there would need to be another acquired knowledge, leading to an infinite regress. But a thing described as 'first' cannot have a prior. Thus, it is established that necessary truths are not within human power (qudra), nor is their entailment of the first acquired within capacity (wus'), nor is the entailment of the first acquired for the second acquired within power, and so on for all degrees. Consequently, no knowledge or cognition occurs by the servant's power and choice, which is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
بديهيات (badihiyyat)
مكتسبات (muktasabat)
قدرة (qudra)
وسع (wus')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only the first acquired knowledge could be within human power, but the rest of the chain is not.
Correction: The argument shows that the entire chain, including the entailment of necessary truths for the first acquired and all subsequent entailments, is beyond human power.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about necessary knowledge (badihiyyat)?
A) They are acquired by human power.
B) They are within the capacity of the servant.
C) They are not within human power and neither is their entailment of the first acquired.
D) They are only partially within human power.

Key Takeaway
The author proves that necessary knowledge and the entire chain of derived knowledge are not within human power, showing that all knowledge comes from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your understanding of faith is a gift from Allah, not a product of your own intellect? How would your humility deepen if you realized that even your ability to reason about God is granted by Him?
?
Consider a piece of knowledge you are proud of acquiring. Can you honestly say you attained it without divine facilitation? Will you thank Allah for that knowledge, knowing you could not have reached it by your own power?
?
If all knowledge is beyond your capacity to produce, what does that mean for your reliance on Allah in every moment of learning? Will you begin your next study session with a sincere acknowledgment that only Allah grants understanding?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
10 min
The Proof That Conceptions Cannot Be Acquired
The author divides knowledge into conception and assent, then proves conceptions cannot be acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that conceptions cannot be acquired, using a dilemma based on awareness of the quiddity.

Core Idea
Acquiring a conception is impossible: you either already have it or cannot seek it.

This passage presents a rigorous proof that no new conception can be acquired. The author first divides knowledge into conception and assent, then sets up a clever dilemma: to acquire a conception, you must either already know it (impossible) or be completely unaware of it (cannot seek it). An objection about partial awareness is addressed by showing that each aspect falls into one of the two impossible options.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the logical flow, not every detail.
2
Identify the division of knowledge
Notice how the author defines conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). This division sets the stage for the proof.
Conception is perception without judgment; assent is with judgment.
3
Understand the dilemma
The first evidence presents a dilemma: either you have awareness of the quiddity (mahiyya) or you don't. If you do, it's already present; if you don't, you cannot seek it.
4
Examine the objection and answer
An objector suggests partial awareness might allow acquisition. The author responds by splitting the object into known and unknown aspects, each falling into one horn of the dilemma.
The known aspect cannot be sought; the unknown aspect cannot be sought.
5
Reflect on the proof's structure
Consider how the author uses an exhaustive division (either known or not) and applies it to each aspect, showing acquisition is impossible in all cases.
This is a classic kalam argument form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثاني على هذا المطلوب
إن العلم إما تصور، وإما تصديق
وذلك لأنا إذا أدركنا أمراً من الأمور، فإما أن نحكم عليه بحكم وإما أن لا نحكم عليه بحكم فإن لم تحكم عليه بحكم، فذاك هو التصور، وإن حكمنا عليه بحكم، فذاك هو التصديق. إذا عرفت هذا الحصر . فنقول : إنه لا يمكن اكتساب شيء من التصورات . ويدل عليه وجوه :
الأول : إنا إذا حاولنا اكتساب شيء آخر من التصورات فحال ما نحاول ذلك الاكتساب . إما أن يكون لنا شعور بماهية ذلك المطلوب، أو لا يكون لنا به شعور فإن كان لنا به شعور فحينئذ يكون تصوره حاضراً عندنا . والحاصل لا يمكن تحصيله . وإن لم يكن لنا به شعور، كان الذهن غافلاً عنه . والغافل عن الشيء يمتنع أن يكون طالباً له .
فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الأمر، مشعوراً به من وجه دون وجه، فلأجل أنه مشعور به من بعض الوجوه، يمكن طلبه . ومن حيث إنه غير مشعور به من سائر الوجهه، فإن العقل يحاول تكميل ذلك الشعور وإتمامه، فلا جرم صح طلبه ؟ .
والجواب : إن أحد الوجهين لما كان محكوماً عليه بأنه مشعور به، والوجه الثاني محكوم عليه بأنه غير مشعور به . كان أحد الوجهين مغايراً للآخر، وإلا يصدق على الشيء الواحد، أنه مشعور به، غير مشعور به . وذلك محال وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الوجه الذي هو مشعور به يمتنع كونه مطلوباً . يقتضي تحصيل الحاصل. والوجه الذي هو غير مشعور به، يمتنع لأن ما كان الذهن غافلاً عنه يمتنع طلبه . والحاصل : أن التقسيم الذي
لأنه
ذكرناه أولاً
، يفيد في هذين الوجهين
The second proof for this desired conclusion. Knowledge is either conception (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq). That is because when we perceive something, either we pass a judgment on it or we do not pass a judgment on it. If we do not pass a judgment on it, that is conception, and if we pass a judgment on it, that is assent. When you understand this exhaustive division, we say: It is not possible to acquire any of the conceptions. Several points indicate this: First: If we attempt to acquire another conception, then at the moment we attempt that acquisition, either we have awareness of the quiddity of that sought thing or we do not have awareness of it. If we have awareness of it, then its conception is present to us, and it cannot be obtained (because it would be obtaining the already obtained). If we do not have awareness of it, then the mind is heedless of it, and the one heedless of a thing cannot be a seeker of it. If they say: Why is it not permissible for that thing to be known in some respect and not in another? Because it is known in some respects, it can be sought; and because it is not known in other respects, the intellect attempts to complete and perfect that awareness, so it is valid to seek it? The answer: Since one of the two aspects is judged as known and the second aspect is judged as unknown, the two aspects are different, otherwise it would be true of a single thing that it is known and unknown, which is impossible. When this is established, we say: The aspect that is known cannot be sought because it would be obtaining the already obtained, and the aspect that is unknown cannot be sought because what the mind is heedless of cannot be sought. In sum: The division we mentioned earlier applies to these two aspects.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof of the main thesis, beginning with a division of knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). The author defines them: when we perceive something, if we do not affirm a judgment upon it, that is conception; if we do affirm a judgment, that is assent. Then he asserts that no conception can be acquired. The first evidence (wajh) for this is a dilemma: if we attempt to acquire a conception, either we already have awareness (shu'ur) of its quiddity (mahiyya) or we do not. If we have awareness, the conception is already present and cannot be obtained (it would be obtaining the already obtained, tahsil al-hasil). If we do not have awareness, the mind is heedless (ghafil) of it, and a heedless mind cannot seek what it is unaware of. An objection is raised: what if the thing is known in some respects and unknown in others? Then it could be sought based on the known aspect while the intellect completes the unknown aspects. The author answers that the two aspects (known and unknown) are different attributes of the same thing. If they were not different, it would be contradictory for the same thing to be both known and unknown. But once they are distinct, the known aspect cannot be sought (as it is already known), and the unknown aspect cannot be sought (as the mind is heedless of it). Thus, the original exhaustive division (either known or not known) applies to both aspects, making acquisition impossible.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)
شعور (shu'ur)
ماهية (mahiyya)
تحصيل الحاصل (tahsil al-hasil)
غافل (ghafil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if we have partial awareness of a conception, we can acquire the full conception by completing the unknown parts.
Correction: The author shows that the known part is already present and cannot be sought (tahsil al-hasil), and the unknown part is completely unknown and cannot be sought (heedlessness), so acquisition is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the objection in this passage, how might one argue that a conception can be acquired despite partial awareness?
A) The mind can create new knowledge from nothing.
B) The object is known in some respects and unknown in others, so the intellect can complete the unknown parts.
C) The seeker merely needs to recall forgotten knowledge.
D) The conception is acquired directly from sense perception.

Key Takeaway
Acquiring a conception is impossible because you either already have it (cannot re-acquire) or are completely unaware of it (cannot seek it); partial awareness does not help because each aspect still falls into one of these two categories.

Refutation of Seeking Something Known from One Aspect
Author refutes an objection about seeking something known-unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an object that is partially known and partially unknown cannot be sought by the intellect, and how the author refutes the objection using the principle of non-contradiction.

Core Idea
Partial knowledge does not make seeking possible—it leads to logical contradiction.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle objection: could we seek something we are partially aware of? The objection tries to save the possibility of seeking the unknown by suggesting partial knowledge is a motivation. The author refutes it by showing that the known part cannot be sought (redundant) and the unknown part cannot be sought (heedless), so the original conclusion stands.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an initial claim, an objection, and a detailed answer.
The objection is introduced with 'فإن قالوا' and the answer with 'والجواب'.
2
Identify the two parts of the objector's claim
The objector argues that if something is known from one aspect, seeking is possible; if unknown from another, the intellect tries to complete the awareness. See how these two parts are connected.
The objector is trying to have it both ways: the known part motivates, the unknown part is the target.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author shows the two aspects are distinct, otherwise a contradiction arises. Then he applies the earlier division: the known aspect already acquired, the unknown aspect heedless. Both prevent seeking.
This refutation uses the principle that a single thing cannot be both known and unknown under the same aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عندنا . والحاصل لا يمكن تحصيله . وإن لم يكن لنا به شعور ، كان الذهن غافلاً عنه . والغافل عن الشيء يمتنع [ أن يكون ] طالباً له .
فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الأمر ، مشعوراً به من وجه دون وجه ، فلأجل أنه مشعور به من بعض الوجوه ، يمكن طلبه . ومن حيث إنه غير مشعور به من سائر الوجوه ، فإن العقل يحاول تكميل ذلك الشعور وإتمامه ، فلا جرم صح طلبه ؟ .
والجواب : إن أحد الوجهين لما كان محكوماً عليه بأنه مشعور به ، والوجه الثاني محكوم عليه بأنه غير مشعور به . كان أحد الوجهين مغايراً للآخر ، وإلا يصدق على الشيء الواحد ، أنه مشعور به ، غير مشعور به . وذلك محال وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الوجه الذي هو مشعور به يمتنع [ كونه ] مطلوباً . يقتضي تحصيل الحاصل. والوجه الذي هو غير مشعور به ، يمتنع لأن ما كان الذهن غافلاً [ عنه ] يمتنع طلبه . والحاصل : أن التقسيم الذي ذكرناه أولاً ، يفيد في هذين الوجهين .
With us. The result is that it cannot be obtained. And if we have no awareness of it, the mind is heedless of it. And the one heedless of a thing cannot be a seeker of it.
If they say: Why is it not permissible that that thing be aware of from one aspect and not from another? Because it is aware of from some aspects, its seeking is possible. And insofar as it is not aware of from the other aspects, the intellect attempts to complete that awareness and perfect it, so its seeking is valid.
The answer: Since one of the two aspects is judged as being aware of, and the second aspect is judged as not being aware of, one of the two aspects is different from the other, otherwise a single thing would be described as both aware of and not aware of, which is impossible. And when this is established, we say: The aspect which is aware of cannot be sought, because that would entail the acquisition of what is already acquired. And the aspect which is not aware of cannot be sought, because what the mind is heedless of cannot be sought. The result: The division we mentioned first applies to these two aspects.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the possibility of seeking something that is known from one aspect and unknown from another. The author first establishes that if we have no awareness of something, the mind is heedless of it, and a heedless person cannot seek it. The objector then argues that if something is known from one aspect, seeking it is possible because the intellect attempts to complete the awareness. The author answers by pointing out that the two aspects are different; otherwise the thing would be both known and unknown, which is impossible. The known aspect cannot be sought because that would be redundant acquisition; the unknown aspect cannot be sought because the mind is heedless of it. Thus the initial division stands.

Misconception: Some might think partial knowledge suffices for seeking; the author shows it leads to contradiction.
Correction: Partial knowledge does not enable seeking because the known part is already acquired and the unknown part is heedless. The two aspects cannot be combined without contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Seeking something requires complete unawareness or complete awareness; partial awareness leads to a logical contradiction that makes seeking impossible.

Two Objections Against Knowing the Whole Through Its Parts
Two objections against the method of knowing a quiddity through its parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowing a quiddity through its parts leads to two specific impossibilities: reliance on an invalid division and circular self-definition.

Core Idea
You cannot define a whole by its parts without either leaning on an invalid method or falling into circularity.

In this passage, the author examines a proposed method for gaining conceptual knowledge of a quiddity: first knowing the parts, then the aggregate. He argues that this approach fails in two distinct ways. The first objection shows that it depends on a division of knowledge that has already been invalidated. The second objection reveals that it either relies on another invalid division or reduces to circular self-definition. Understanding these objections is crucial for grasping the principles of definition and conceptual knowledge in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two objections
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice that the passage begins by stating the method, then declares it impossible in two respects. Try to locate where each objection starts.
The first objection begins with 'الأول:' and the second with 'والثاني:'.
2
Understand the first objection
The first objection argues that if knowledge of one part necessitates knowledge of the other, then this falls under the third division of knowledge (deriving knowledge from something external). Since that division has been proven invalid, this objection holds.
The third division refers to knowing something through what is external to it, which is not allowed in definitions.
3
Understand the second objection
The second objection presents a dilemma: either knowledge of the aggregate necessitates knowledge of the whole (but that division is invalid), or we claim to derive knowledge of the whole from knowledge of its parts, which reduces to defining a thing by itself—circular and impossible.
The final part explains why the second horn is circular: the aggregate of parts is the whole itself.
4
Reflect on the takeaway
After studying the objections, reflect on why the method fails. This helps clarify the proper way to define quiddities and the importance of avoiding circularity and invalid divisions.
This ties back to the broader principles of logical definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بوجه يوجب العلم بالجزء الثاني، ثم العلم بمجموع الأجزاء، يفيد العلم بتمام تلك الماهية. إلا أن هذا محال من وجهين: الأول: إن على هذا التقدير، يكون العلم بأحد الجزءين، موجباً للعلم بالجزء الثاني. وهذا إنما يتم إذا كان العلم بالشيء يستفاد من المعلوم بالأمر الخارج عنه. وهذا هو القسم الثالث. فيكون الدليل الدال على إبطال هذا القسم، يوجب فساد هذا الاحتمال. والثاني: إن هذا الكلام إنما يتم إذا قلنا: إن العلم بمجموع الأجزاء، يوجب العلم بتمام الماهية. إلا أن الدليل الدال على فساد هذا القسم، يوجب فساد هذا الاحتمال. وأما إذا قلنا: إنا نتعرف العلم بتلك الماهية من العلم بمجموع أجزائها. فهذا أيضاً: محال. لأن مجموع أجزائها، هو تمام ماهية تلك الماهية. فالقول بأنا نتعرف مجموع تلك الماهية، من معرفة مجموع أجزائها: يعود إلى القسم الأول. وهو تعريف الشيء بنفسه وهو محال.
By a certain way, knowledge of the second part, then knowledge of the aggregate of parts, yields knowledge of the entirety of that quiddity. However, this is impossible in two respects: First: On this supposition, knowledge of one part would necessitate knowledge of the second part. This only holds if knowledge of a thing is derived from a known matter external to it. And this is the third division. Thus the proof indicating the invalidity of this division necessitates the corruption of this probability. Second: This statement only holds if we say that knowledge of the aggregate of parts necessitates knowledge of the entirety of the quiddity. But the proof indicating the corruption of this division necessitates the corruption of this probability. And if we say: we come to know the quiddity from knowledge of its aggregate of parts, this also is impossible, because the aggregate of its parts is the entire quiddity of that quiddity. So saying we come to know the entirety of that quiddity from knowledge of its aggregate of parts returns to the first division, which is defining a thing by itself, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against a specific method of acquiring conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of a quiddity (mahiyya). The method proposes that knowing the parts individually leads to knowing the whole. The first objection argues that if knowledge of one part necessitates knowledge of the other, then this would rely on the third division (knowing something through what is external to it), but that division has already been invalidated. The second objection argues that if knowledge of the aggregate of parts necessitates knowledge of the whole, this also fails because the aggregated parts are the whole itself, leading to circular self-definition, which is impossible.

Key Terms
ماهية (quiddity)
تأثير (influence)
قسم (division)
تعريف الشيء بنفسه (self-definition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing the parts of a quiddity always yields knowledge of the whole.
Correction: The passage shows this leads to reliance on an invalid method or circularity, so it is not valid.

Checkpoint
Why is the second objection considered impossible?
A) Because it relies on the third division
B) Because knowledge of the aggregate is circular, defining the thing by itself
C) Because it requires external knowledge
D) Because it is hypothetical

Key Takeaway
Knowing a quiddity through its parts is impossible because it either relies on an invalid division of knowledge or results in defining the thing by itself, which is circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Impossibility of Knowing Quiddity Through External Attributes
Refutation of acquiring quiddity via external attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an external attribute cannot serve as a reliable means to conceptualize a quiddity without circularity.

Core Idea
An external attribute may be shared – it cannot uniquely define a quiddity.

Al-Isfahani now refutes the third proposed method for acquiring conceptual knowledge of a quiddity: using attributes that are external to it. He shows that this method leads to a logical circularity, because to know that an attribute is exclusive to the quiddity, you already need knowledge of that quiddity. This passage sharpens our understanding of the limits of external attributes in conceptualization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow from the premise to the conclusion.
The argument is condensed; read slowly.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage argues that using an external attribute to conceptualize a quiddity is impossible. Why? Because the attribute could be shared by other quiddities.
3
Follow the argument: shared attributes
The text states that different quiddities can have the same inseparable concomitant (lāzim). Therefore, the attribute alone does not guarantee uniqueness.
This is based on the established principle that quiddities may share attributes.
4
Recognize the circularity
To use the attribute to identify the quiddity, you must first know that the attribute is exclusive to that quiddity. But knowing exclusivity requires prior conceptualization of the quiddity. Hence, circular.
The passage explicitly says: 'knowledge of the relation is preceded by knowledge that such-and-such a quiddity is the one to which such-and-such a concomitant attaches.'
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The method fails because it cannot provide new knowledge of the quiddity's particularity. It only tells you that a certain concomitant attaches to it, not what it is in itself.
This shows the need for direct conceptualization or other valid methods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث: وهو أنا نتعرف تصور تلك الماهية من أمور خارجة عنها. فنقول: إن صريح العقل لا يستبعد حصول ذلك الوصف في غير تلك الماهية. لما ثبت: أن الماهيات المختلفة يجوز اشتراكها في لازم واحد. فعلى هذا التقدير ما لم يعرف أن ذلك الوصف مختص بتلك الماهية، وغير حاصل في غيرها، فإنه لا يمكننا أن نتوسل بمعرفة ذلك الوصف إلى معرفة تلك الماهية. لكن علمنا بأن ذلك الوصف مختص بتلك الماهية، مسبوق بتصور تلك الماهية. لأن العلم بالنسبة، مسبوق بالعلم بكل الماهية الفلانية هي التي يلزمها اللازم الفلاني. فهذا لا يفيد العلم بخصوصية تلك الماهية. فإن بهذا القدر لا تعرف أن تلك الماهية أمراً ما، مجهول الحقيقة. إلا أنه عرف منها، أنه يلزمها اللازم الفلاني. ومن راجع نفسه، علم أن الأمر كما ذكرناه.
As for the third division: that we come to know the concept of that quiddity from matters external to it. We say: clear reason does not deem it improbable that that attribute occurs in other than that quiddity, because it has been established that different quiddities may share a single inseparable concomitant. Thus on this supposition, as long as it is not known that that attribute is specific to that quiddity and does not occur in other than it, we cannot use knowledge of that attribute to reach knowledge of that quiddity. However, our knowledge that that attribute is specific to that quiddity is preceded by the conceptualization of that quiddity, because knowledge of the relation is preceded by knowledge that such-and-such a quiddity is the one to which such-and-such a concomitant attaches. So this does not yield knowledge of the particularity of that quiddity. For by this extent, one does not know that that quiddity is a certain thing of unknown reality, except that it is known that such-and-such a concomitant attaches to it. And whoever examines himself knows the matter as we have stated.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage refutes the third method of acquiring conceptual knowledge: using attributes external to the quiddity. The argument is that an external attribute may be shared by multiple different quiddities, so to identify the quiddity through that attribute, one must first know that the attribute is exclusive to that quiddity. But knowing that exclusivity itself requires prior conceptualization of the quiddity, resulting in circularity. Thus this method fails.

Key Terms
وصف (attribute)
لازم (inseparable concomitant)
خصوصية (particularity)
تصور (conceptualization)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any external attribute can uniquely identify a quiddity.
Correction: An external attribute may be shared by multiple quiddities, so without prior knowledge of exclusivity it cannot uniquely identify the quiddity, leading to circular reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with using an external attribute to know a quiddity?
A) The attribute may be unknown
B) The attribute may be shared by multiple quiddities
C) The attribute is eternal
D) The attribute is physical

Key Takeaway
An external attribute cannot uniquely identify a quiddity because establishing its exclusivity requires prior knowledge of the quiddity, leading to circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that knowing one attribute of Allah (like His Mercy) gives you a complete understanding of His Essence? How does this lesson’s argument about shared attributes and circularity challenge that assumption? What will you do to avoid overconfidence in what you know about Him?
?
Think about your own identity: how often do you define yourself by external attributes like your job, wealth, or relationships? According to this lesson, those attributes could be shared by others and cannot truly capture your unique essence. In what concrete way will you shift your focus from external labels to knowing your core self in light of this truth?
?
The passage ends by saying 'whoever examines himself knows the matter as we have stated.' Have you honestly examined yourself to see if you fall into the trap of trying to know things through external signs? What one change will you make today to seek knowledge of quiddities through their proper avenues, not through circular reasoning?

Refuting the Combined Method for Conceptualizations
Refutation of the combined method and final conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why combining invalid methods cannot yield knowledge of quiddities and how this leads to the conclusion that conceptualizations are not acquired.

Core Idea
If no single method works, combining them does not either.

The author has already refuted three methods for acquiring conceptualizations (tasawwurat). Now he addresses the fourth division, which mistakenly assumes that combining these invalid methods can produce knowledge. This passage shows the logical flaw in that assumption and delivers the decisive conclusion that conceptualizations are not acquired at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the argument is structured: first the claim of the fourth division, then its refutation, then the conclusion.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The passage contains a refutation (first half) and a conclusion (second half). The refutation uses a logical principle: if each individual method fails, their combination also fails. The conclusion states that conceptualizations cannot be acquired.
3
Understand the critical phrase
Note the phrase 'these two decisive proofs' – it refers to the earlier refutations of the third and fourth divisions. The author considers these proofs decisive in showing that no tasawwurat are acquired.
4
Connect to the larger discussion
This passage closes the entire section on the acquisition of conceptualizations. It reinforces the point that knowledge of quiddities is not something we actively produce, but either occurs spontaneously or not at all.

Commentary
This passage refutes the fourth method, which combines the previous three. Since each individual method has been shown impossible, their combination cannot cause knowledge either. The phrase 'these two decisive proofs' likely refers to the previous refutations of the third and fourth divisions.

Third Proof: Introspective Limits of Conception
Third proof that conceptual knowledge is limited to senses, internal states, and combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the introspective proof that all conceptualizations originate from senses, internal states, or combinations, and thus none are acquired from outside revelation.

Core Idea
All conceptual knowledge is either sensed, felt within, or constructed from these — nothing else.

This passage presents the third proof that conceptual knowledge is not acquired. The proof is introspective: by looking within ourselves, we identify the only possible sources of conceptualizations. This lesson walks you through that reflection and its implications for the broader argument.

Read the passage carefully

Identify the three categories
The passage presents three sources of conceptualization: senses, internal states, and compositions of these. Find each one and notice how the author defines them.
3
Understand the conclusion
The proof ends by concluding that no conceptualization is acquired, tying together this proof with the previous two. Reflect on why this matters for the overall argument.
This lesson connects the three proofs into one conclusion.

Commentary
This passage provides a third proof that conceptual knowledge is not acquired. It is introspective: by examining ourselves, we find that we can only conceive of three types: (1) sensory perceptions, (2) internal states like emotions, and (3) compositions of these by the intellect or imagination. Anything beyond these is impossible. Therefore, conceptualizations are not acquired; they are either given directly or constructed from given elements. The conclusion ties together this proof with the previous two (the two objections and the refutation of the divisions) to assert that no tasawwur is acquired.

Self-Evident vs Theoretical Judgments: The First Proof
First proof: distinction between badihi and nazari in tasdiqat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion that distinguishes self-evident (badihi) judgments from theoretical (nazari) judgments, and why this shows that not all knowledge is acquired.

Core Idea
Some judgments are immediate; others require reasoning.

Sanusi begins his proof that not all affirmations (tasdiqat) are acquired by drawing a crucial distinction. Every judgment involves two concepts: subject and predicate. The key question is whether simply bringing those concepts to mind automatically produces the judgment. This passage explains that if it does, the judgment is self-evident; if not, it is theoretical and requires reasoning. Understanding this distinction is the foundation for recognizing that some knowledge is innate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's rhythm and examples sink in before interpreting.
The passage cuts off but the main idea is complete.
2
Identify the two types of judgments
Find where Sanusi defines 'self-evident' (badihi) and 'theoretical' (nazari). Notice the criterion: whether the mere presence of subject and predicate concepts in the mind is sufficient to produce the judgment.
3
Understand the examples
Look at the example of 'one is half of two' as self-evident, and 'the world is originated' as theoretical. Ask yourself: why does the first not require reasoning while the second does?
The theoretical example is incomplete but the contrast is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أن شيئاً من التصديقات غير مكتسب. فيدل عليه أيضاً وجوه: الأول: إن كل تصديق، فلا بد فيه من تصورين. أحدهما: تصور الموضوع. والآخر: تصور المحمول. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إما أن يكون مجرد حضور هذين التصورين في الذهن، مستقلا بإيجاب أن يحكم الذهن بذلك التصديق، أو لا يكون. والأول: هو البديهيات والثاني: هو النظريات. مثال الأول: إنا إذا تصورنا أن الواحد ما هو؟ وتصورنا أن نصف الاثنين ما هو؟ فمجرد حضور هذين التصورين في الذهن، يوجب جزم الذهن بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين. فهذا هو البديهي. ومثال الثاني إنا إذا تصورنا: أن العالم ما هو؟ وأن الحادث ما هو؟ لم يكن مجرد
As for the idea that some affirmations are not acquired, it is also indicated by proofs: The first: Every affirmation necessarily involves two conceptualizations: one is the conceptualization of the subject, and the other is the conceptualization of the predicate. Once you know this, we say: either the mere presence of these two conceptualizations in the mind is independently sufficient to necessitate the mind's judgment with that affirmation, or it is not. The former are the self-evident propositions, and the latter are the theoretical ones. An example of the first: when we conceptualize what 'one' is, and we conceptualize what 'half of two' is, the mere presence of these two conceptualizations in the mind necessitates the mind's certain judgment that one is half of two. That is the self-evident. An example of the second: when we conceptualize what 'the world' is, and what 'the originated' is, the mere [presence does not suffice].
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion on affirmations (tasdiqat) to show that some are not acquired. The first proof distinguishes between self-evident (badihi) and theoretical (nazari) judgments. Every judgment requires two concepts: subject and predicate. If merely bringing those concepts to mind automatically produces the judgment, it is self-evident (e.g., 'one is half of two'). If not, the judgment requires reasoning and is theoretical (e.g., 'the world is originated'). The text cuts off before completing the nazari example, but the point is clear: self-evident judgments are not acquired; they are innate.

تصور (conceptualization)
موضوع (subject)
محمول (predicate)
بديهي (self-evident)

Misconception: All knowledge is acquired through reasoning and experience.
Correction: Some judgments, like 'one is half of two,' are self-evident and do not require reasoning; they are immediately known upon conceptualizing the terms.

Key Takeaway
Self-evident judgments are those where the mere presence of subject and predicate concepts in the mind automatically produces the judgment, proving that not all knowledge is acquired.
