First Proof: Axiomatic and Theoretical Judgments Are Not Acquired
Sanusi argues that humans have no power over any judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Sanusi, humans have no power over any judgment—whether axiomatic or theoretical—and how this establishes that all knowledge is created by God.

Core Idea
No judgment is acquired by humans; all are necessitated by prior conditions beyond human control.

This lesson examines the first argument that humans have no power over any judgments, whether axiomatic or theoretical. Sanusi shows that judgments arise necessarily from their premises, and since we cannot control those premises or their logical consequence, we cannot truly 'acquire' knowledge. This foundational claim supports the Ash'ari doctrine that all actions, including mental acts, are created by God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi first addresses axiomatic judgments, then extends the argument to theoretical ones.
The argument uses the terms 'wājib' (necessary) and 'mumtani‘' (impossible) to describe the outcome.
2
Understand the argument for axiomatic judgments
Identify the two mental images (al-taṣawwurān) and how their presence necessitates or prevents the judgment. Note that man has no power over either the images or the necessity.
The necessity is logical, not voluntary.
3
Extend to theoretical judgments
See how the same logic applies: if the axiomatic premises are sufficient, the theoretical judgment is necessary; if not, it's impossible. Either way, no human power is involved.
This argument generalizes to all acquired knowledge.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider that this argument denies human acquisition (kasb) in the realm of knowledge, aligning with the Ash'ari view that God creates all actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : أما التصديقات البديهية ، فشيء منها غير مكتسب . لأن ذينك التصورين . إن حضرا ، كانا موجبين لذلك التصديق - والإنسان لا قدرة له في تحصيل ذينك التصورين - وعند حضورهما فلا قدرة له في استلزامهما لذلك التصديق . بل إن حضرا لكان عند حضور ذلك التصديق واجباً . وإن لم يحضر إلا واحداً منهما ، كان حضور ذلك التصديق ممتنعاً . فثبت : أن الإنسان لا قدرة له البتة على التصديقات البديهية . وأما التصديقات النظرية . فلا قدرة له أيضاً على شيء منها . لأن تلك البديهيات ، إن كانت متجمعة للأمور المعتبرة في استلزام تلك النظريات ، كان حصول تلك النظريات عقيب تلك البديهيات واجباً ، فلم يكن للإنسان قدرة عليها . وإن لم تكن مستجمعة للأمور المعتبرة في ذلك الاستلزام ، امتنع كونها مستلزمة لتلك النظريات . والممتنع لا قدرة عليه . وتمام تقرير هذا الكلام هو الذي سبق ذكره في البرهان الأول . إلا أن هذا الوجه في الحقيقة غير مختص بالتصديقات النظرية ، بل هو عام في كيفية المكتسبات . علم من علم يتقدمه ، سواء كان ذلك العلم ، علما تصورياً أو تصديقياً .
If you know this, then we say: As for axiomatic judgments (al-tasdiqāt al-badīhiyya), none of them is acquired (muktasab). Because those two mental images (al-taṣawwurān) – if they are present, they necessitate that judgment – and man has no power to acquire those two mental images – and when they are present, he has no power to make them necessitate that judgment. Rather, if they are present, then at their presence that judgment is necessary (wājib). And if only one of them is present, then the presence of that judgment is impossible (mumtani‘). So it is established that man has absolutely no power over axiomatic judgments. And as for theoretical judgments (al-tasdiqāt al-naẓariyya), he also has no power over any of them. Because those axiomatic [judgments], if they are collectively sufficient for the matters considered in necessitating those theoretical [judgments], then the occurrence of those theoretical judgments after those axiomatic [judgments] is necessary, so man has no power over them. And if they are not collectively sufficient for the matters considered in that necessitation, then it is impossible for them to necessitate those theoretical [judgments]. And the impossible cannot be subject to power. The complete exposition of this statement is what was previously mentioned in the first proof (al-burhān al-awwal). However, this aspect (al-wajh) in reality is not specific to theoretical judgments, but is general to the modality of acquired things (kayfiyyat al-muktasabāt): a knowledge that precedes [another] knowledge, whether that knowledge is conceptual (taṣawwurī) or judgmental (taṣdīqī).
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (al-wajh al-awwal) that humans have no power over any judgments, whether axiomatic or theoretical. The argument begins by noting that axiomatic judgments arise from two mental images (al-taṣawwurān) – presumably the subject and predicate concepts. When both images are present, the judgment is necessitated; if only one is present, the judgment is impossible. Since man cannot control the presence of these images nor their necessitation, he has no power over axiomatic judgments. The argument then extends to theoretical judgments: they depend on axiomatic premises. If those premises are sufficient to necessitate the theoretical judgment, the conclusion is necessary and not subject to human will. If they are insufficient, the conclusion is impossible, and thus also beyond power. The author notes that this argument is not restricted to judgments but applies to all acquired knowledge—prior knowledge must be present and necessitates the subsequent knowledge, leaving no room for human acquisition. This establishes the foundational Kalam principle that all knowledge is created by God.

Key Terms
تصديقات بديهية
تصديقات نظرية
مكتسب
تصوران
استلزام
واجب
ممتنع
اكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans actively produce knowledge through reasoning and effort.
Correction: The argument shows that even reasoning is governed by logical necessity or impossibility, leaving no room for human power to bring about the conclusion itself.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why does man have no power over axiomatic judgments?
A) Because they are too complex for the human mind.
B) Because their occurrence depends on the presence of two mental images and their necessitation, both beyond human control.
C) Because God directly creates all judgments without any intermediary.
D) Because axiomatic judgments are illusions.

Key Takeaway
Humans have no power over any judgment—axiomatic or theoretical—because their occurrence is either necessary or impossible based on premises beyond human control.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are convinced of a belief, do you attribute your certainty to your own reasoning or to the necessary connection between premises beyond your control? How does this lesson challenge your sense of intellectual autonomy?

Think of a time you changed your mind after hearing an argument. Can you identify the premises that led to that change? Did you have control over accepting those premises, or were they forced upon you? What does that reveal about the nature of your conviction?
?
If every judgment you hold is either necessary or impossible given its premises, then what responsibility do you have for your beliefs? How does this reshape your accountability before God for what you affirm or deny?

The Dilemma of Necessary vs. Non-Necessary Judgment
Sanusi presents a dilemma to prove no judgment is acquired by human effort.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that no acquired judgment can be within human power, using a logical dilemma that covers both necessary and non-necessary cases.

Core Idea
Every acquired judgment is either forced or uncertain — either way, it is not humanly acquired.

Sanusi presents a second, more refined argument to establish that none of the judgments we call 'acquired' are actually within our power. He sets up a dilemma: when the required mental images are present, the judgment that follows is either necessary or not. Both possibilities lead to the same conclusion — that the judgment cannot be truly acquired by the servant. This passage strengthens the Ashʿarī position that all knowledge comes directly from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and follow the dilemma
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi structures the argument as a dilemma with two horns.
The dilemma is highlighted by the phrase 'either... or...'
2
Examine the first horn (necessary/lāzim)
Understand why if the judgment is necessary, it is beyond human power because both the mental images and the necessitation are beyond control.
3
Examine the second horn (non-necessary)
Understand why if the judgment is not necessary, it lacks certainty and is merely a blind belief that can be doubted — hence it is not real knowledge.
4
Connect to the conclusion
See how both horns lead to the same point: all knowledge, whether conceptual or judgmental, is created by Allah alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في بيان أن شيئاً من التصديقات غير مكتسب : هو أن نقول : لا شك أن تلك التصديقات الكسبية ، لا يمكن إيقاعها إلا في تصورات حاضرة في الذهن . فنقول : عند حضور تلك التصورات ، إما أن يكون ذلك التصديق ضرورياً ، أو لازماً ، أو لا يكون كذلك . فإن كان حصول ذلك التصديق عند حضور تلك التصورات لازماً أو ضرورياً ، لم يكن للعبد قدرة عليه ، ولا اختيار له فيه . لأن تلك التصورات لا قدرة للعبد عليها البتة . وعند حضورها تكون مستلزمة لذلك التصديق استلزاماً لا قدرة للعبد عليه فعلى هذا التقدير ، امتنع أن يكون ذلك التصديق واقعاً بكسب العبد و باختياره. وأما إن كان حصول التصديق عند حصول تلك التصورات غير ضروري ولا لازم ، فحينئذ لم يكن ذلك التصديق علماً ولا يقيناً ، بل هو اعتقاد تقليدي ، أتى به الإنسان من غير موجب . وهو أيضاً محال . ومتى حاول الإنسان تشكيك نفسه فيه ، أمكن ذلك وقبل هذا لا يكون علماً ولا يقيناً . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن العلوم إما تصورات وإما تصديقات . وثبت : أن كل واحد منها خارج عن قدرة العبد وعن وسعه. فثبت : أن المعارف والعلوم خارجة عن قدرة البشر ، وأن حصولها ليس إلا بخلق الله سبحانه .
The second aspect (al-wajh al-thānī) in demonstrating that none of the judgments are acquired: is that we say: There is no doubt that those acquired judgments (al-tasdiqāt al-kasbiyya) can only occur upon mental images present in the mind. So we say: When those images are present, then either that judgment is necessary (ḍarūrī) or necessary-consequent (lāzim), or it is not so. If the occurrence of that judgment upon the presence of those images is lāzim or ḍarūrī, then the servant has no power over it, nor any choice in it. Because those images are absolutely not within the servant's power. And when they are present, they necessitate that judgment with a necessitation that the servant has no power over. Thus, on this assumption, it is impossible for that judgment to occur by the servant's acquisition and choice. And if the occurrence of the judgment upon the presence of those images is neither ḍarūrī nor lāzim, then that judgment is not knowledge (ʿilm) nor certainty (yaqīn). Rather, it is a blind belief (iʿtiqād taqlīdī) that man brings about without justification. And that is also impossible. Whenever man tries to doubt himself concerning it, that is possible, and prior to that it is not knowledge nor certainty. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the sciences are either concepts (taṣawwurāt) or judgments (taṣdīqāt). And it is established that each one of them is beyond the servant's power and his capacity. So it is established that cognitions and sciences are beyond human power, and their occurrence is only by the creation of God, exalted is He.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-thānī) that no judgment (taṣdīq) is acquired by human effort. The argument is a dilemma. Acquired judgments, by definition, occur only when mental images are present in the mind. Given those images, the judgment is either necessarily produced (ḍarūrī or lāzim) or not. If it is necessarily produced, then it is not within the servant's power, because both the images and the necessitation are beyond his control. If it is not necessarily produced, then it is not knowledge (ʿilm) or certainty (yaqīn), but merely a blind belief (iʿtiqād taqlīdī) that can be doubted. Since true knowledge must be certain, such a belief cannot be considered acquired knowledge. Thus, in both cases, the judgment cannot be acquired by human power. The argument concludes that all sciences, whether conceptual or judgmental, are beyond human capacity and are created solely by God. This reinforces the Ashʿarī doctrine that human acts, including cognitive acts, are created by God.

Key Terms
تصديقات كسبية
ضروري
لازم
اعتقاد تقليدي
قدرة العبد
خلق الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can acquire certain knowledge through their own effort and choice without divine creation.
Correction: Sanusi shows that any judgment either arises necessarily (beyond human control) or is doubtful (not certain), so true knowledge cannot be humanly acquired; it must be created by Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what happens if a judgment is not necessary or lāzim when mental images are present?
A) It becomes a probable opinion that can be accepted.
B) It is still knowledge but weaker.
C) It is not knowledge or certainty, but a blind belief that can be doubted.
D) It is automatically acquired by the servant's choice.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that whether a judgment arises necessarily or not, it cannot be acquired by human effort; all knowledge is a direct creation of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your deepest convictions are either forced upon you by your circumstances or are based on blind acceptance, how can you be certain that your faith is true knowledge and not just inherited belief? Will you examine one belief today that you have never doubted?
?
The argument says that when you try to doubt a non-necessary judgment, you can succeed. Is there any belief in your life that you are afraid to doubt? Why do you think that fear exists? What would it take for you to submit to the truth even if it means letting go of a cherished assumption?
?
Sanusi claims that all knowledge comes only from Allah's creation. Does your daily experience feel like that? Or do you act as if your own thinking and reasoning are independent of His power? When you feel proud of an insight or a conclusion, do you acknowledge that it was created for you, or do you attribute it to yourself?

Proof from the Soul's Knowledge
The author concludes rational proofs for creation of actions by arguing from ignorance of the soul's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul's ignorance of its own knowledge proves that Allah creates it.

Core Idea
Our ignorance of how knowledge arises in us proves Allah creates it.

This is the concluding argument in a series of rational proofs for the creation of human actions. The author uses a simple but powerful observation: if we were the creators of our own knowledge, we would know exactly how and where we made it. But we don't. This leads to the conclusion that the creator is Allah. The passage also marks the end of the rational proofs section.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a hypothetical statement followed by a conclusion.
The passage is short but dense; let the words sink in.
2
Identify the key premise
The author says: if I were the creator of these sciences, I would know the place and locus of their creation. This is the hypothetical premise.
3
Notice the observation
The author then states: 'But since all is ambiguous and unknown'. This is the critical observation that refutes the premise.
The ambiguity refers to our lack of knowledge about how knowledge comes to be in us.
4
Understand the conclusion
From the observation, the author deduces that the true Creator is Allah. Reflect on why this follows logically.
The conclusion is explicit: 'we know that the Creator is Allah'.
5
Connect to the larger issue
The passage concludes the rational proofs on 'creation of actions'. Think about how this argument fits into the broader kalam discussion on human agency and divine creation.
This argument is a small but pointed part of a larger debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النفس الناطقة . ولو كنت أنا الخالق لهذه العلوم، لكنت عالماً بأني في أي موضع خلقتها ؟ وفي أي محل أوجدتها وأحدثتها ؟ ولما كان الكل مشتبهاً غير معلوم . علمنا : أن خالق هذه المعارف والعلوم : هو الله سبحانه وليكن مهنا آخر كلامنا في الدلائل العقلية في مسألة خلق الأعمال وبالله التوفيق
The rational soul. And if I were the creator of these sciences, I would know in which place I created them? And in which locus I originated them and brought them into existence? But since all is ambiguous and unknown, we know that the Creator of these cognitions and sciences is Allah, the Exalted. And let here be the end of our speech on the rational proofs concerning the issue of the creation of actions. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the rational proofs in the issue of the creation of actions (خلق الأعمال). The author presents a concise argument: He begins with 'النفس الناطقة' (the rational soul), presumably as the locus of knowledge. He then poses a hypothetical: if he himself were the creator of these sciences (العلوم/المعارف), he would know the precise location and mode of their creation. But since he does not know these details—'كل مشتبها غير معلوم' (all is ambiguous and unknown)—he concludes that the true Creator must be Allah. The argument relies on the self-evident fact that humans are ignorant of the inner workings of their own knowledge, proving it is not self-created. The passage ends with a formal closure of the rational proofs section and a blessing ('وبالله التوفيق').

Refuting the Third Objection on Preponderator
Sanusi refutes the third objection by counter-critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi turns the third objection back on the opponents by showing their doctrine leads to the same consequence they criticize.

Core Idea
The opponents' denial of motive dependence leads to the very absurdity they claim we fall into.

Sanusi has presented three objections attributed to the opponents of human agency. The third objection claims that requiring a preponderator (murajjih) for the servant's action leads to absurdity. In this passage, Sanusi refutes it by showing that the opponents' own position—that action does not depend on the motive (da'i)—entails the same consequence. It is a classic turn of the tables.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the structure: the opening statement about the possible, then a comment, then the answer to the third objection.
The phrase 'two sides of the possible' is a technical term for existence and non-existence of a contingent.
2
Identify the opponent's doctrine
Find where Sanusi states the opponent's view: that the occurrence of an action does not depend on the motive (da'i). Mark that as the key claim.
3
Understand the refutation logic
Sanusi argues that if the opponent's doctrine is true, they are committed to the very same absurdity they accuse us of. Think about why: without a motive, what determines the action?
This is a tu quoque argument.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Consider why this matters for the question of human free will vs. determinism. The opponent's denial of motive leads to a different but equally problematic necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح . ولا شك أن هذا أقبح وأقطع مما ألزموه علينا . وأما الوجه الثالث من الوجوه التي نسبناها إليهم : فجوابه : إن مذهبهم : أن حصول الفعل لا يتوقف على الداعي وحينئذ يلزمهم على هذا المذهب ، عين ما ألزموه علينا . وبالله التوفيق
The two sides of the possible are not without a preponderator. And there is no doubt that this is uglier and more decisive than what they imposed on us. And as for the third aspect of the aspects we attributed to them: its answer is that their doctrine is that the occurrence of the action does not depend on the motive, and then they are obliged by this doctrine the very same thing they imposed on us. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage refutes a third objection raised against the proponents of human agency. The opponents had argued that if the servant's action requires a preponderator (murajjih), it leads to absurdity. The author responds by pointing out that the opponents' own doctrine—that the occurrence of an action does not depend on a motive (da'i)—leads to the very same absurdity they accuse us of. Thus the objection is turned back. The phrase 'two sides of the possible' refers to the two possibilities of existence and non-existence for a contingent thing, which require a preponderator to tip the scale. The author then concludes with 'With God is success.'

Key Terms

داعي
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It depends on a motive
B) It does not depend on a motive
C) It depends on a preponderator
D) It is impossible

Key Takeaway
The third objection is refuted by showing that the opponents' own denial of motive leads to the same necessity they condemn.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Sanusi ends with 'With God is success.' In what area of your faith are you relying on your own arguments rather than seeking God's guidance? Write down one step you will take this week to humble your intellect before divine teaching.

The First Proof: God Creates Human Actions
Razi presents the first proof and its two preliminary matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi combines scriptural and rational arguments to demonstrate that God creates all things, including human actions.

Core Idea
God's creation encompasses every action: scripture and reason agree.

Razi begins his first proof by laying out a simple syllogism from the Qur'an, then strengthens it with a foundational rational principle. Before addressing potential objections, he establishes two indispensable preliminary matters that ground the argument in both revelation and reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the syllogism to the two preliminary matters.
The passage begins with a straightforward deductive argument.
2
Identify the main syllogism
Find the three parts: the minor premise (action is a thing), the major premise (every thing is created by God), and the conclusion (action is created by God).
The syllogism is explicitly marked by 'ينتج' (it yields).
3
Understand the first preliminary
Razi divides existence into necessary-by-itself and possible-by-itself. The possible needs a preponderator (God) to exist. This rational principle confirms the scriptural verse.
This is a standard kalam argument from contingency.
4
Review the second preliminary
Razi lists four Qur'anic verses that affirm God as Creator of everything. This shows the scriptural basis is not isolated but repeated.
The verses are from various surahs, reinforcing the doctrine.
5
Reflect on the combined proof
Notice how Razi presents the rational argument as 'confirming' the apparent meaning of the verse. The proof is both textual and rational.
The rational argument is decisive (قاطع), as he states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى في بيان أن الله تعالى خالق لأعمال العباد : نقول : عمل العبد شيء ، وكل شيء فهو مخلوق الله تعالى . لقوله تعالى : ( الله خالق كل شيء ) ينتج : أن عمل العبد مخلوق الله تعالى . واعلم : أنا قبل الخوض في تعديد السؤالات والجوابات نقدم أمرين لا بد منهما . أما الأول : فهو أن نقول : إن الموجود إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته أو ممكناً لذاته ، والممكن لذاته. لا يترجح عدمه على وجوده ، ووجوده على عدمه ، إلا بترجيح الواجب لذاته. وقد قررنا هذه النكتة في باب الدلائل العقلية . فيثبت : أنه تعالى هو الخالق والموجد والمقدر لجميع الممكنات . ولما كان فعل العبد من جملة الممكنات ، وجب دخوله في هذه القضية . فثبت : أن ظاهر قوله سبحانه : ( الله خالق كل شيء ) قد تأكد بهذا البرهان العقلي القاطع وأما الثاني : فهو أنه تعالى ذكر هذه الآية في مواضع من كتابه : إحداها : قوله تعالى : ( أم جعلوا الله شركاء . خلقوا كخلقه . فتشابه الخلق عليهم . قل : الله خالق كل شيء . وهو الواحد القهار ) وثانيها : قوله تعالى : ( لا إله إلا هو ، خالق كل شيء . فاعبدوه ) وثالثها : قوله : ( وخلق كل شيء ، وهو بكل شيء عليم ) ورابعها : قوله : ( وخلق كل شيء . فقدره تقديراً )
The first proof in clarifying that God Almighty is the Creator of the actions of servants: We say: The action of the servant is a thing, and every thing is created by God Almighty due to His saying: 'Allah is the Creator of everything.' It yields: That the action of the servant is created by God Almighty. Know that before entering into the enumeration of questions and answers, we present two matters that are indispensable. As for the first: It is that we say: The existent is either necessary by itself or possible by itself. The possible by itself does not prefer its non-existence over its existence nor its existence over its non-existence except by a preponderance from the necessary by itself. We have established this point in the chapter on rational proofs. So it is established that He Almighty is the Creator, the Exister, and the Determiner of all possibles. Since the action of the servant is among the possibles, its inclusion in this proposition is necessary. So it is established that the apparent meaning of His saying 'Allah is the Creator of everything' is confirmed by this decisive rational proof. As for the second: It is that He Almighty mentioned this verse in places in His Book: First: His saying: 'Or have they attributed partners to Allah who created like His creation so that the creation seemed alike to them? Say: Allah is the Creator of everything, and He is the One, the Prevailing.' Second: His saying: 'There is no deity except Him, the Creator of everything, so worship Him.' Third: His saying: 'And He created everything, and He is of everything Knowing.' Fourth: His saying: 'And He created everything and determined it with [precise] determination.'
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that God creates human actions. It begins by stating the syllogism: the servant's action is a thing, and every thing is created by God, so the action is created. Then the author introduces two preliminary matters that must be understood before entering into objections and answers. The first preliminary is the rational division of existence into the necessary by itself and the possible by itself. The possible requires a preponderator from the necessary to bring it into existence. Since the servant's action is possible, it must be created by God. This rational argument confirms the scriptural verse. The second preliminary is a listing of four Qur'anic verses that use the phrase 'Creator of everything' or similar, showing the pervasiveness of the scriptural evidence.

Key Terms
واجب لذاته
ممكن لذاته
ترجيح
برهان عقلي
الخالق
الموجد
المقدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies solely on the Qur'anic verse without rational basis.
Correction: Razi explicitly states that the apparent meaning of the verse is confirmed by a decisive rational proof (البرهان العقلي القاطع) from the division of existence into necessary and possible.

Checkpoint
What is the rational premise used to support the verse 'Allah is the Creator of everything'?
A) Everything that exists is necessary.
B) The possible does not preponder without a preponderator.
C) The servant's action is eternal.
D) God is only the estimator.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of all things, including human actions, is established by both the clear text of the Qur'an and the rational argument from contingency.

?

The Third Proof: Allah is Not Called 'Shay'' from the Verse on Beautiful Names
Sanusi presents the third proof that Allah is not named 'shay'' using the verse on beautiful names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof from the Qur'an that Allah is not named 'thing' (al-shay') and how it follows from the command to call upon Allah only by beautiful names.

Core Idea
Calling Allah by a name lacking perfection contradicts the command to use beautiful names.

This lesson examines Sanusi's third proof—a direct Qur'anic argument. The verse 'And to Allah belong the beautiful names, so call upon Him by them' (7:180) is used to show that 'thing' cannot be a divine name because it is not beautiful. We will trace the logic and see how it reinforces divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow: the verse, the hypothetical, and the conclusion.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument hinges on the command to use beautiful names. Locate this premise and the implication if 'thing' were a divine name.
3
Understand the conclusion
Note how the contradiction is derived and what it establishes about the name 'shay'' and the verse from Surah Al-Anbiya'.
The conclusion also ties back to the previous two proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قوله تعالى : ( والله الأسماء الحسنى فادعوه بها ) دل هذا النص : على أنه يجب أن يدعى الله بالأسماء الحسنة . ولو كان لفظ « الشيء ) واقعاً على الله ، لجاز أن يدعى الله بهذا الاسم. لكن هذا الاسم ليس من الأسماء الحسنى . فإنه كونه شيئاً ، لا يدل على صفة من صفات الكمال والجلال . فجواز دعوة الله بهذا الاسم ينافي ما دل عليه قوله : ( والله الأسماء الحسنى فادعوه بها ) قثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أنه تعالى لا يطلق عليه اسم الشيء ، فلم پلزم كون هذه الآية مخصوصة
Third: His saying, exalted is He: (And to Allah belong the beautiful names, so call upon Him by them) – this text indicates that it is obligatory to call upon Allah by the beautiful names. And if the word 'thing' (al-shay') were applied to Allah, it would be permissible to call upon Allah by this name. But this name is not among the beautiful names. For its being a 'thing' does not indicate any attribute of perfection and majesty. Thus, the permissibility of calling upon Allah by this name contradicts what His saying indicates: (And to Allah belong the beautiful names, so call upon Him by them). So it is established by these three proofs that He, exalted is He, is not given the name 'thing' (al-shay'), and therefore this verse is not particularized.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that Allah is not called 'thing' (shay'). It is a proof from the Qur'anic verse 'And to Allah belong the beautiful names, so call upon Him by them.' The argument proceeds: 1) The verse commands that Allah be invoked only by beautiful names. 2) If the word 'thing' were applicable to Allah, then it would be permissible to call upon Him by that name. 3) However, the name 'thing' is not a beautiful name, because calling something a 'thing' does not imply any attribute of perfection or majesty. 4) Therefore, the permissibility of calling upon Allah by 'thing' would contradict the command to use beautiful names. The author concludes that this, along with the two earlier proofs, establishes that Allah is not named 'thing', and consequently the verse 'Allah is the Creator of everything' is not particularized (i.e., it does not need to be excluded from the general meaning).

Key Terms
الشيء (al-shay')
الأسماء الحسنى (al-asma' al-husna)
التخصيص (al-takhsis)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'thing' is a neutral term that can be applied to anything, including God.
Correction: This argument shows that 'thing' lacks the connotation of perfection required for divine names, and using it would contradict the Qur'anic command to invoke Allah only by beautiful names.

Checkpoint

A) Because the Qur'an explicitly forbids it.

C) Because it implies Allah is created.
D) Because it is a name used only for physical objects.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that calling Allah by a name that lacks perfection contradicts the Qur'anic command to use beautiful names, so Allah is not named 'thing'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Refuting the Exclusion of Allah from the Creator Verse
Sanusi presents and refutes the argument that the verse 'Creator of everything' excludes Allah even if He is called a thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the argument that the verse 'Creator of everything' excludes Allah even if He is called a thing.

Core Idea
Even if Allah is a 'thing,' the Creator verse is particularized and becomes ambiguous as proof.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a second level of objection: even if we grant that Allah can be called a 'thing,' the verse 'Creator of everything' is said to exclude Him because the addressee is not included. Sanusi refutes this by showing that the generality is particularized by contextual evidence, which then renders the verse equivocal and not a valid proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's argument first, then the rebuttal.
The rebuttal has three distinct points.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent claims that even if Allah is called 'thing,' the verse 'Creator of everything' explicitly means Creator of everything other than Allah, because the addressee is not included in such expressions.
3
Analyze Sanusi's three rebuttal points
Sanusi gives three responses: (1) consensus establishes Allah as a thing; (2) the general term is particularized by contextual evidence; (3) particularization makes the term equivocal and not a proof.
Focus on how each point weakens the opponent's case.
4
Understand the consequence of particularization
Sanusi argues that a particularized general term loses its apparent meaning and must be interpreted metaphorically. With multiple possibilities, it becomes ambiguous (mujmal) and cannot serve as proof.
This is the key doctrinal move: particularization voids probative force.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why this refutation is important: it preserves the generality of 'Creator of everything' while excluding Allah through contextual evidence, maintaining the verse's probative value for creation.
Sanusi's method shows how to handle apparent conflicts in scripture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني : لو سلمنا أنه تعالى مسمى باسم الشيء ، إلا أنه تعالى لما حكم بكونه خالقاً لكل شيء. كان هذا تصريحاً بأن المراد : كونه خالقاً ، لكل ما عداه ، من الأشياء ، لما ثبت أن المخاطب ، لا يندرج تحت الخطاب في أمثال هذه الألفاظ فهذا مجموع ما قيل في بيان أن هذا العموم غير مخصوص. لأنا نقول : أما بيان أن الله تعالى مسمى باسم الشيء . فلإجماع العلماء المتأخرين عليه . ولا عبرة بمخالفة ( جهم ، فإنه ليس ممن يعتبر قوله في الإجماع . وأما أن هذا التعميم مخصوص . فلأن قوله تعالى : ( كل شيء ) يتناول ذات الله . وإنما أخرجناه لما ذكرتم من القرينة . فكان هذا تخصيصا للعموم . ثم نقول : إن هذا العموم مخصوص ، فوجب أن لا يبقى حجة ، لأنه لما دخله التخصيص، لم يبق ظاهره مراداً ، فوجب صرفه إلى بعض المجازات ، وليس بعضها أولى من بعض، فيصير مجملا .
The second level: If we grant that He, exalted is He, is named by the name 'thing', yet since He, exalted is He, judged Himself to be the Creator of everything, this was an explicit declaration that the intended meaning is that He is the Creator of everything other than Him, from among the things, because it is established that the addressee is not included under the address in examples of such expressions. So this is the totality of what was said in explanation that this generality is not particularized. But we say: As for the explanation that Allah, exalted is He, is named by the name 'thing' – it is by the consensus (ijma') of the later scholars upon it. And no consideration is given to the opposition of Jahm, for he is not among those whose statement is considered in consensus. And as for the fact that this general term is particularized – because His saying, exalted is He: (every thing) includes the essence of Allah. And we only excluded it due to the contextual evidence (qarina) you mentioned. So this was a particularization (takhsis) of the general. Then we say: This general is particularized, therefore it should no longer be a proof (hujja), because when it has been subject to particularization, its apparent meaning is not intended, so it must be redirected to some metaphorical meanings (majazat), and not one of them is more likely than another, so it becomes equivocal (mujmal).
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents a two-part argument. First, it states the opponent's second-level position: even if we grant that Allah is called 'thing', the verse 'Allah is the Creator of everything' explicitly means Creator of everything other than Himself, because the addressee is not included in such expressions. Then the author gives his own rebuttal: (1) The fact that Allah is called 'thing' is established by consensus (ijma') of later scholars, and the objection of Jahm is disregarded. (2) The general term 'every thing' literally includes Allah's essence, but we exclude Him based on contextual evidence (qarina) – that is a particularization (takhsis). (3) Once a general term is particularized, it loses its probative force (hujja) because its literal sense is not intended. It must then be interpreted metaphorically, and since no specific metaphorical interpretation is more likely, it becomes equivocal (mujmal) and cannot be used as proof. Thus the opponent's argument fails.

Key Terms
المقام الثاني (al-maqam al-thani)
الإجماع (ijma')
التخصيص (takhsis)
الحجة (hujja)
المجاز (majaz)
المجمل (mujmal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that particularization of a general term does not affect its probative value; it still remains a valid proof.
Correction: Sanusi argues that once a general term is particularized, its literal meaning is no longer intended, so it must be interpreted metaphorically. With multiple possibilities, it becomes ambiguous and cannot serve as a proof.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the consequence of particularizing the general term 'every thing'?
A) It makes the verse stronger.
B) It makes the verse a clear proof.
C) It renders the verse equivocal (mujmal) and not a proof.
D) It shows that Allah is not a thing.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that even if Allah is called a 'thing', the particularization of the Creator verse renders it equivocal and invalid as proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a religious proof that, when you examined it more closely, turned out to be ambiguous or particularized? Are you willing to let go of arguments that do not withstand scrutiny, even if they support a position you deeply hold?
?
Sanusi dismisses Jahm because he is not part of the consensus. Do you ever dismiss opposing views without giving them a fair hearing simply because they come from a source you consider unreliable? How would you respond if someone dismissed your opinion on similar grounds?
?
The particularized verse became 'mujmal'—unclear and not a proof. Is there any belief you hold that relies on a text you have never examined for possible particularization? What would it take for you to accept that your proof might actually be equivocal?

The Creator of Everything Excludes Allah Himself
Author concedes Allah is called 'thing' but argues the verse specifies creation of other-than-Him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical theologians argue that the verse 'Creator of everything' does not imply Allah creates Himself, even if He is called a 'thing'.

Core Idea
Even if Allah is a 'thing', the verse excludes Him from His own creation.

This lesson presents a nuanced concession. The opponent might say: if Allah is a 'thing', then 'Creator of everything' would include Him. The author answers: no, because in such expressions the speaker (or addressee) is naturally excluded. We'll see how this argument works and why it is logically sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a concession followed by a restriction.
The passage starts with 'If we concede...' which sets up a hypothetical acceptance.
2
Identify the key move
The author concedes that Allah can be called a 'thing' but then argues that the phrase 'Creator of everything' is understood to mean 'everything other than Allah'. Identify the reason given.
The reason is that the addressee (Allah) is not included in the general term in such expressions.
3
Understand the principle
Reflect on the principle: when someone says 'I made everything', they don't include themselves. Similarly, when Allah says 'Creator of everything', He is not including Himself. Grasp why this is a natural linguistic understanding.
This principle is based on common usage and logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني : لو سلمنا أنه تعالى مسمى باسم الشيء ، إلا أنه تعالى لما حكم بكونه خالقاً لكل شيء. كان هذا تصريحاً بأن المراد : كونه خالقاً ، لكل ما عداه ، من الأشياء ، لما ثبت أن المخاطب ، لا يندرج تحت الخطاب في أمثال هذه الألفاظ
The second position: If we concede that He, exalted, is named by the name 'thing' (shay'), yet since He, exalted, ruled that He is Creator of everything, this is a declaration that the intended meaning is that He is Creator of everything other than Him, because it is established that the addressee is not included under the address in similar expressions.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents a concession argument. The author says: even if we accept the premise that Allah can be called a 'thing', the verse 'Creator of everything' is understood to mean 'Creator of everything other than Himself.' Why? Because in such expressions (like 'I made everything' said by a person), the speaker himself is not included. The addressee (here Allah) is not included in the general term 'everything'. So the verse does not imply that Allah creates Himself, and the general term is naturally restricted.

Key Terms
المقام الثاني
اسم الشيء
خالق كل شيء
المخاطب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verse 'Creator of everything' includes Allah Himself as part of 'everything'.
Correction: The passage explains that in such expressions, the addressee (Allah) is not included, so 'everything' means everything other than Allah.

Checkpoint

A) Allah created Himself
B) Allah created everything including Himself
C) Allah created everything other than Himself
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Creator of everything' means everything other than Allah because the addressee (Allah) is not included in such general expressions.

The Three Separate Specifying Proofs and the First: Rational Intuition
Enumerates three separate specifying proofs and explains the first: rational intuition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of the three separate specifying proofs and the argument that rational intuition establishes the servant's creative agency as necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Rational intuition is the strongest proof: what is foundational to necessary knowledge is itself necessary.

In this passage, the author introduces the three types of evidence that can specify the general verses of the Quran regarding human agency. The first and strongest is rational intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql), which provides necessary knowledge that the servant is the creator of his own actions. This argument uses a foundational principle: whatever is the basis for necessary knowledge must itself be necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proofs
Read the enumeration and note the three types: rational intuition, rational proof, and scriptural proof.
The author calls them 'separate specifying evidences'.
2
Understand the argument for rational intuition
Follow the logical chain: necessary knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame leads to its foundation in the servant's creative agency, which then must also be necessary.
The argument relies on a foundational principle: what is the foundation of necessary knowledge is itself necessary.
3
Appreciate the priority of rational intuition
Since rational intuition is certain, it takes precedence over rational proof in specifying Quranic generalities.
The author says specifying by intuition is 'even more appropriate' than by reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدلائل المخصصة المنفصلة . فهي ثلاثة : بديهية العقل، ودليل العقل ، ودليل السمع .
وأما الأول ، وهو بديهة العقل . فتقريره : أن أصحاب : أبي الحسين ) قالوا : حسن المدح والذم والمؤاخذة والمطالبة : معلوم بالضرورة ، ونعلم أيضاً بالضرورة : أن العبد لو لم يكن موجداً الأفعال نفسه ، لما حسنت هذه الأشياء ، فلما كان العلم بحسن المدح والذم ضرورياً، وكان العلم يكون العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه أصلاً لذلك العلم ، وثبت أن ما كان أصلا للعلم الضروري ، : أولى أن يكون ضرورياً ؛ ثبت أن العلم بكون العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، علم ضروري ، ولما جاز تخصيص عموم القرآن بدليل العقل ، فلان يجوز تخصیصه ببديهة العقل ، كان أولى .
وأما الثاني والثالث . وهو بيان الدلائل العقلية والسمعية ، الدالة على أن الموجد لأفعال العبد ، هو العبد. فسيأتي تقريرها إن شاء الله تعالى .
As for the separate specifying evidences, they are three: rational intuition, rational proof, and scriptural proof. As for the first, which is rational intuition, its exposition: that the followers of Abu al-Husayn said: the goodness of praise and blame and punishment and demand is known necessarily, and we also know necessarily that if the servant were not the creator of his own actions, these things would not be good. So since the knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame is necessary, and the knowledge that the servant is the creator of his own actions is the foundation for that knowledge, and it is established that whatever is a foundation for necessary knowledge is more deserving of being necessary; it is established that the knowledge that the servant is the creator of his own actions is necessary knowledge. And since it is permissible to specify the generalities of the Quran by rational proof, then to specify it by rational intuition is even more appropriate. As for the second and third, which are the exposition of the rational and scriptural evidences indicating that the creator of the servant's actions is the servant, their exposition will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage introduces the 'separate specifying evidences' (adilla mukhaṣṣiṣa munfaṣila) which are three: (1) badīhat al-ʿaql (rational intuition), (2) dalīl al-ʿaql (rational proof), and (3) dalīl al-samʿ (scriptural proof). The author then explains the first proof: rational intuition. He reports from the followers of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri that we know necessarily (bi-ḍarūra) the goodness (ḥusn) of praise and blame and punishment and accountability. We also know necessarily that if the servant were not the creator (mūjid) of his own actions, these things would not be good. Since the knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame is necessary, and the knowledge that the servant creates his actions is the foundation (aṣl) for that necessary knowledge, and whatever is a foundation for necessary knowledge is itself more deserving of being necessary, it follows that the knowledge that the servant creates his own actions is also necessary. Then the author argues: since it is permissible to specify the general verses of the Quran by rational proof (dalīl al-ʿaql), it is even more appropriate to specify them by rational intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql), which is stronger. He then notes that the second and third proofs will be presented later.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
دليل العقل
دليل السمع
حسن المدح والذم
علم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational intuition is less reliable than scriptural proof for specifying Quranic verses.
Correction: The author argues that rational intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql) is even more appropriate for specification than rational proof, and by extension than scriptural proof, because it provides necessary knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the foundation (aṣl) for the necessary knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame?
A) The general verse 'Khaliq kulli shay''.
B) The knowledge that the servant is the creator of his own actions.
C) The command to worship God.
D) The following verse about clear proofs.

Key Takeaway
Rational intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql) is the strongest separate specifying proof because it provides necessary knowledge that the servant is the creator of his own actions, which is foundational to moral accountability.

The Necessary Knowledge of Human Agency
Author argues that knowledge of human creation is necessary and defends against objections from Quranic generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers knowledge of the servant's creation of his own actions to be necessary and how rational intuition can specify Quranic general texts.

Core Idea
What is a source of necessary knowledge is itself necessarily known.

This passage tackles a foundational issue in Islamic theology: do humans truly create their own actions? The author presents a rational argument that such knowledge is necessary, not merely theoretical. He then addresses a key objection from the Qur'anic verse 'Creator of everything', explaining how rational intuition can specify general texts. Let's unpack this dense but crucial reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without pausing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall flow.
Focus on the logical connectors: 'ثبت', 'ولما', 'وأما', 'سلمنا'.
2
Identify the core claim about necessary knowledge
The author states that the knowledge that the servant creates his own actions is necessary. Find the reasoning: because such actions are a source (asl) for a certain knowledge?
The logic: if X is a source of necessary knowledge, then knowledge of X is more fittingly necessary.
3
Understand the specification argument
The author argues that rational intuition can specify general Quranic texts, even more readily than rational evidence. Note the phrase 'بديهة العقل' (rational intuition).
This is a key principle in usul al-fiqh and kalam.
4
Follow the concession and rebuttal
The author concedes that we do not know a specifier for 'Khaliq kulli shay', but then counters that lack of knowledge does not prove absence. Next, he rejects the opponent's move to use rational evidence to support the text's generality.
Pay attention to the dialectic: 'لا يقال' and 'لأنا نقول'.
5
Reflect on the epistemological stance
Consider how the author prioritizes rational intuition over textual generality. This reflects a rationalist approach in kalam.
How does this connect to the broader debates on human free will and divine omnipotence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأفعال نفسه أصلاً لذلك العلم، وثبت أن ما كان أصلاً للعلم الضروري أولى أن يكون ضرورياً؛ ثبت أن العلم بكون العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه علم ضروري. ولما جاز تخصيص عموم القرآن بدليل العقل، فلأن يجوز تخصيصه ببديهة العقل كان أولى. وأما الثاني والثالث وهو بيان الدلائل العقلية والسمعية الدالة على أن الموجد لأفعال العبد هو العبد فسيأتي تقريرها إن شاء الله تعالى. سلمنا: أنا لا نعرف لعموم قوله: (خالق كل شيء) مخصصاً. إلا أن عدم العلم بالشيء لا يدل على العلم بعدم الشيء، فلا يمكننا القطع بعدم المخصص، وحينئذ تصير هذه الدلائل ظنية. لا يقال: إنا نقيم الدلالة العقلية على أنه تعالى خالق لأعمال العباد، ونجعل تلك الدلالة العقلية مؤكدة لظاهر هذا النص، ونعلم أن الدليل العقلي لا يعارضه معارض. فبهذا الطريق نعلم أن ظاهر هذا العموم سليم عن المعارض. لأنا نقول: فعلى هذا التقدير ما لم يعرفوا تلك الدلالة العقلية الدالة على أن موجد فعل العبد هو الله، لا يمكنكم التمسك بهذا العموم. لكنكم إذا عرفتم ذلك الدليل، فقد استغنيتم عن التمسك بهذا العموم.
For his own actions are a source for that knowledge, and it is established that whatever is a source for necessary knowledge is more appropriate to be necessary; so it is established that the knowledge that the servant is the creator of his own actions is necessary knowledge. And since it is permissible to specify the general Quranic text by rational evidence, then it is more appropriate to specify it by rational intuition. As for the second and third, which is the exposition of rational and scriptural evidences indicating that the creator of the servant's actions is the servant, their confirmation will come, God willing. We concede: we do not know a specification for the generality of His statement 'Creator of everything' except that lack of knowledge of a thing does not indicate knowledge of the absence of the thing, so we cannot be certain of the absence of a specification, and then these evidences become conjectural. It should not be said: We establish rational evidence that He is the Creator of the servants' actions, and we make that rational evidence confirm the apparent meaning of this text, and we know that rational evidence has no opposing counterevidence. Thus we know that the apparent meaning of this generality is free from opposition. For we say: On this assumption, as long as they do not know that rational evidence indicating that the creator of the servant's action is God, you cannot adhere to this generality. But if you know that evidence, then you have dispensed with adhering to this generality.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a key argument about the nature of knowledge concerning human agency. The author begins by stating that if something is the source (asl) of necessary knowledge (ilm daruri), then that knowledge itself is more appropriately necessary. Since the knowledge that the servant creates his own actions is derived from such a source, it is necessary. Then he addresses a potential objection: the Qur'anic verse 'Khaliq kulli shay'' (Creator of everything) might seem to contradict this, but the generality can be specified by rational evidence, especially by intuitive reason (badiha al-aql). He concedes that we do not know of a specifier for this verse, but that does not prove its absence. The opponent might argue that rational evidence can support the text, but the author replies that if the rational evidence is known, then the text becomes redundant.

Key Terms
علم ضروري
بديهة العقل
تخصيص عموم القرآن
خالق كل شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we do not know a specifier for the verse 'Creator of everything', then the verse must be general and contradicts human agency.
Correction: The author points out that lack of knowledge of a specifier does not prove the absence of a specifier; the verse may have an unknown specification.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that knowledge of the servant being the creator of his actions is necessary?
A) Because it is derived from a source that itself is necessary.
B) Because the Quran explicitly says so.
C) Because it is a matter of consensus.
D) Because it is a logical tautology.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of human agency is necessary because it is the source of necessary knowledge, and Quranic generality can be specified by rational intuition.

The author states that lack of knowledge does not prove absence. Where in your spiritual life do you assume something is false simply because you haven't seen evidence (e.g., the reality of Hell, the mercy of God)? How will you actively seek knowledge rather than judgment?

What would change in your practice if you took this principle seriously?

Refuting Objections on 'Creator of All Things'
Author refutes linguistic objections against the general term 'كل' and its specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the general term 'كل' (all) against objections, showing that even if specification occurs, the term remains evidence for the unspecified portions.

Core Idea
A specified general term still proves for the rest.

In this passage, the author addresses several objections raised against the claim that Allah is the Creator of all things. Opponents argue that 'كل' does not imply universality, its indication is only conjectural, and that specification renders it useless. The author refutes each, providing logical proofs that a general term retains its evidential force for what remains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection and response
Read the objection that 'كل' does not indicate comprehensiveness. Notice the author's counterexample: if a master says 'All my slaves are free,' all are freed. This shows the term does imply universality.
The counterexample is from everyday language.
2
Understand the second objection and response
The opponent says the indication is only conjectural. The author accepts this, stating that verbal evidence yields only probability, which is sufficient in juridical arguments.
This concedes a point but argues it does not undermine the proof.
3
Examine the third objection and the two proofs
The opponent claims specification makes the general term useless. The author first denies specification, then concedes and gives two demonstrations that even if specified, it remains proof for the unspecified parts.
Focus on the two proofs: independence of indication and effective cause.
4
Note the fourth objection and brief denial
The opponent mentions connected specifying indicators. The author simply denies their existence, reserving fuller refutation for later.
This is a short rebuttal; the main arguments are the two proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولا نسلم أن لفظة كل تفيد الاستغراق ، قلنا : والدليل عليه : أن السيد إذا قال : كل عبيدي أحرار : عنق الكل . قوله : ( دلالة لفظ كل على الاستغراق ، دلالة ظنية ، قلنا : عندنا : أن الدلائل اللفظية لا تفيد إلا الظن . قوله : ( هذا عام ، دخله التخصيص ، فلا يكون حجة ، قلنا : لا نسلم أنه دخله التخصيص ، ولا نسلم وقوع لفظ الشيء على الله سبحانه وقد سبق تقريره . سلمنا : أنه عام دخله التخصيص ، لكن لا نسلم أنه لا يبقى حجة . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إن العام هو الذي تناول صورتين ، فصاعداً من غير حصر . ودلالته على هذه الصورة ، وعلى تلك الصورة، إما أن تتوقف كل واحدة منهما على الأخرى، فيلزم الدور، أو تتوقف إحداهما على الأخرى، ولا تتوقف الأخرى على الأولى ، فيلزم الترجيح لا المرجح ، أو لا تتوقف واحدة منهما على الأخرى . وحينئذ لا يلزم من زوال إحداهما ، زوال الأخرى . وهو المطلوب . الثاني : إن المقتضى لثبوت الحكم قائم في غير محل التخصيص . لان الصيغة العامة متناولة له ، وتلك الصيغة مقتضية لذلك الحكم ، والعارض الموجود ، وهو التخصيص لا يصلح معارضاً لهذا المقتضى . لأن انتفاء الحكم في إحدى الصورتين ، لا ينافي ثبوته في الصور الأخرى ، فوجب الحكم بانتفاء دلالة العام المخصوص على الحكم في غير محل التخصيص . قوله : ( الدلائل المخصصة المتصلة موجودة ، قلنا : لا نسلم .
His statement: 'And we do not accept that the word 'kull' (all) indicates comprehensiveness.' We said: 'The evidence for it is that when the master says: All my slaves are free, the whole are freed.' His statement: 'The indication of the word 'kull' to comprehensiveness is a conjectural indication.' We said: 'According to us, verbal indications only yield conjecture.' His statement: 'This is a general term, specification has entered it, so it is not a proof.' We said: 'We do not accept that specification has entered it, and we do not accept the occurrence of the word 'thing' to God Almighty, and its establishment has preceded. We concede: that it is a general term that has been specified, but we do not accept that it remains not a proof. And two proofs indicate it: First: The general term is that which encompasses two or more forms without enumeration. Its indication of this form and of that form – either each of them depends on the other, which leads to circularity; or one of them depends on the other and the other does not depend on the first, which leads to preference without a preferrer; or neither depends on the other. In that case, the removal of one does not entail the removal of the other. That is what is sought. Second: The effective cause for the establishment of the ruling exists in the location other than the location of specification, because the general form encompasses it, and that form necessitates that ruling, and the incidental occurrence, which is specification, is not a valid counterargument to this necessitation, because the negation of the ruling in one of the two forms does not contradict its establishment in the other forms. Thus, it is necessary to judge that the specified general term indicates the ruling in the location other than the location of specification.' His statement: 'Connected specifying indicators exist.' We said: 'We do not accept.'
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a series of three objections and responses regarding the generality of the term 'كل' (all) in the phrase 'خالق كل شيء' (Creator of all things). The first objection: the word 'كل' does not imply universality. The author responds by giving a counterexample: if a master says 'All my slaves are free,' the freeing applies to all of them, proving that 'كل' does indicate universality. The second objection: the indication is only conjectural. The author accepts this, stating that all verbal evidence yields only probability, but that is sufficient in juridical arguments. The third objection: this general term has been specified (by exclusion of God Himself and other exceptions), so it cannot be used as evidence. The author first denies that specification has occurred, then concedes for argument's sake and provides two logical proofs that a specified general term remains evidence for the unspecified portions. Proof one: the indication of a general term for each individual object is independent; the departure of one does not necessitate departure of others. Proof two: the effective cause for the ruling applies to unspecified items and specification does not negate it, because negation in some forms does not contradict affirmation in others. Then a fourth objection is raised: connected specifying indicators exist (that specify the general immediately). The author simply denies this, reserving fuller refutation for later.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Once a general term is specified, it can no longer be used as evidence for anything.
Correction: The author shows that a specified general term remains evidence for the unspecified parts, using two logical proofs.

Checkpoint

A) He concedes and provides two proofs that it remains a proof.

D) He relies on the consensus of the scholars.

Key Takeaway
A general term, even if specified, continues to serve as evidence for the cases it still covers.

Refutation of Interpreting 'Ja'ala' as Judicial Declaration
Answers the first objection by defining 'ja'ala' as making a thing possess a quality and refutes the interpretation as mere declaration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mutakallimun refute the interpretation of the verb 'ja'ala' as mere judgment or naming, and how the true meaning entails creation of qualities.

Core Idea
The verb 'ja'ala' means to make something possess a quality, not merely to declare it.

In this passage, the author responds to the first objection against the Ash'ari doctrine of God's creation of human acts. The objection had misinterpreted the Qur'anic verb 'ja'ala' (to make) as mere judicial declaration. The author defines its linguistic reality and provides three decisive arguments to show that even if one accepted the opponent's interpretation, it would lead to absurd consequences or actually prove the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation to understand the overall argument.
2
Understand the definition of 'ja'ala'
Notice how the author defines 'ja'ala' with two objects as 'tasyir' — making a thing described by a quality. This is the literal meaning.
The verse 'Rabbana waj'alna muslimayni laka' is used as evidence.
3
Identify the three arguments
The author gives three distinct arguments against the interpretation of 'ja'ala' as judgment (hukm) and naming (tasmiya). List each one and understand its logical structure.
The first is a reductio ad absurdum, the second concerns linguistic homonymy, the third deals with compulsion even if the interpretation were accepted.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that even if the opponent's interpretation were correct, it would still lead to compulsion (jabr) and thus support the Ash'ari doctrine. Consider how this strengthens the author's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن السؤال الأول من وجهين: الأول: إن الجعل المتعدي إلى مفعولين، هو أن يصير موصوفاً بصفة. فقوله: (ربنا واجعلنا مسلمين لك) أي صيرنا موصوفين بصفة الإسلام ولا معنى للتصبير إلا إيجاد الصفة فيه. وأما الوجوه التي ذكروها. فقد اتفق أهل اللغة على أنها مجازات. والأصل في الكلام هو الحقيقة. ثم نقول: أما حمل الجعل على الحكم والتسمية فباطل من وجوه: الأول: لو كان كذلك، لكان من أخبر أن الله: موجود، حي، عالم، قادر. لزم جواز أن يقال: إنه جعل الله موجودا عالماً قادراً. ومعلوم أنه باطل. الثاني: لا نزاع أن الجعل حقيقة في التصيير، فوجب أن لا يكون حقيقة في غيره، دفعاً للاشتراك. الثالث: إن بتقدير أن يكون المراد منه هو الحكم والتسمية. لكن الجبر على هذا التقدير أيضاً لازم، لما ثبت في باب العقليات: أن ما أخبر الله عنه، فإن خلافه يفضي إلى الكذب في كلام الله. وذلك محال، والمفضي إلى محال محال، فكان خلافه محالاً، فكان حصوله واجباً، ضرورة أنه لا خروج عن النقيضين، فثبت: أن هذا الوجه الذي ذكروه، فإنه مع غاية ضعفه، لو صح؛ فإن يقوي مذهبنا ويؤكده لوجوه: للظاهر
And the answer to the first question is from two aspects: The first: that 'ja'ala' (to make) that takes two objects is that something becomes described by a quality. So His saying: (Our Lord, make us Muslims to You) means make us described by the quality of Islam, and there is no meaning to 'making' (tasyir) except the creation of the quality in it. As for the aspects they mentioned, the people of language have agreed that they are metaphorical, and the basis in speech is the literal. Then we say: as for carrying 'ja'ala' on judgment and naming, it is invalid from several respects: The first: if it were so, then whoever informs that God is existent, living, knowing, powerful, it would be permissible to say: he made God existent, living, knowing, powerful, and it is known that this is invalid. The second: there is no dispute that 'ja'ala' is literal in making (tasyir), so it must not be literal in other than it, to avoid homonymy. The third: even if we assume that the intended meaning is judgment and naming, yet compulsion (al-jabr) according to this assumption is also necessary, because it has been established in the domain of rational matters: that what God has informed about, its opposite leads to lying in the speech of God, and that is impossible, and that which leads to the impossible is impossible, so its opposite is impossible, so its occurrence is necessary, by virtue of the fact that there is no escape from the two contradictories. So it is established that this aspect which they mentioned, despite its extreme weakness, if it were correct, it would strengthen and confirm our doctrine for reasons: for the apparent.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the first objection, which likely claimed that the verse proves God creates human faith directly. The author gives two aspects of the answer, but the text focuses on the first: the meaning of the verb 'ja'ala' (جعل) in the verse. The author defines 'ja'ala' when used with two objects as causing something to be described by a quality (تصيير), which is the creation of that quality in the thing. The alternative interpretations (the 'وجوه' mentioned) that the opponent gave are dismissed as metaphorical; the literal meaning (الحقيقة) is the primary one. Then the author refutes the interpretation of 'ja'ala' as mere judgment or naming (الحكم والتسمية) with three arguments:

1. If 'ja'ala' meant declaring something to be, then one could say 'he made God existent, living, knowing, powerful' just by declaring it, which is absurd.
2. Linguists agree that 'ja'ala' is literal in the sense of making (تصيير), so it cannot be equally literal in another sense without equivocation (اشتراك).
3. Even if we accept the interpretation of judgment and naming, it still implies compulsion (الجبر) because God's information (خبر) about something necessitates its occurrence; otherwise God's speech would be false, which is impossible. Thus the thing must happen necessarily.

The author concludes that even this weak interpretation, if accepted, would actually support his own doctrine (that God creates all acts) rather than refute it.

Correction: The literal meaning is to bring about a quality in the thing; even if interpreted as declaration, it still implies necessity and thus compulsion.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that the linguistic and rational evidence supports the Ash'ari view that God's making of human actions involves the creation of their qualities.

Refutation of Interpreting 'Ja'l' as Judgment and Naming
The author refutes the claim that 'ja'l' means judgment and naming with three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting the term 'ja'l' (making) as merely judgment or naming is linguistically and rationally untenable, and how it inadvertently supports the doctrine of compulsion (jabr).

Core Idea
Reading 'ja'l' as judgment conceals the logical necessity of divine compulsion.

This passage is a focused refutation of an opposing interpretation of the Quranic term 'ja'l'. The author argues that the opponents' reading is not only linguistically forced but also leads to absurd theological consequences. By examining three distinct proofs, he shows that 'ja'l' must imply real transformation and existence, and that even if we accept their reading, it backfires by entailing the very compulsion they deny.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic foundation
The author first establishes that 'tasbīr' (making into) means bringing an attribute into existence, and that alternative readings are metaphorical. This sets the stage for the proofs.
Notice that the consensus of linguists is cited for the metaphorical status of alternative readings.
2
Examine the first two proofs
The first proof shows an absurd consequence: if 'ja'l' meant judgment, then one could say 'he made God existent,' which is false. The second proof argues against homonymy: since 'ja'l' is literal for transformation, it cannot be literal for judgment.
These proofs target the linguistic and logical flaws directly.
3
Analyze the third proof
Even on the opponents' own interpretation, compulsion (jabr) follows logically. Because God's information about something makes its opposite impossible, so the reported state must necessarily occur. Thus their weak interpretation actually strengthens the author's position.
This is a turning the tables argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا معنى للتصبير إلا إيجاد الصفة فيه. وأما الوجوه التي ذكروها فقد اتفق أهل اللغة على أنها مجازات. والأصل في الكلام هو الحقيقة. ثم نقول: أما حمل الجعل على الحكم والتسمية فباطل من وجوه: الأول: لو كان كذلك، لكان من أخبر أن الله موجود، حي، عالم، قادر لزم جواز أن يقال: إنه جعل الله موجودا عالما قادرا. ومعلوم أنه باطل. الثاني: لا نزاع أن الجعل حقيقة في التصيير، فوجب أن لا يكون حقيقة في غيره، دفعا للاشتراك. الثالث: إن بتقدير أن يكون المراد منه هو الحكم والتسمية لكن الجبر على هذا التقدير أيضا لازم، لما ثبت في باب العقليات: أن ما أخبر الله عنه، فإن خلافه يفضي إلى الكذب في كلام الله. وذلك محال، والمفضي إلى المحال محال، فكان خلافه محالا، فكان حصوله واجبا، ضرورة أنه لا خروج عن النقيضين، فثبت: أن هذا الوجه الذي ذكروه، فإنه مع غاية ضعفه، لو صح؛ فإن يقوى مذهبنا ويؤكده
And there is no meaning for 'tasbīr' (making into) except bringing the attribute into existence. As for the faces they mentioned, the people of language have agreed that they are metaphors. The basis in speech is the literal. Then we say: As for carrying 'ja'l' (making) on judgment and naming, it is false from faces: First: If it were so, then whoever informs that God is existent, living, knowing, powerful, it would be permissible to say: He made God existent, knowing, powerful. And it is known that this is false. Second: There is no dispute that 'ja'l' is literal in transformation (taṣyīr), so it must not be literal in other than it, to avoid homonymy. Third: Even if it is assumed that what is intended is judgment and naming, but compulsion (jabr) on this assumption is also necessary, because it is established in rational matters that what God informs about, its opposite leads to lying in God's speech. That is impossible, and what leads to the impossible is impossible. So its opposite is impossible, so its occurrence is necessary, by necessity that there is no escape from the two opposites. So it is established that this face they mentioned, with its extreme weakness, if it were valid, it would strengthen our doctrine and confirm it.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opposing interpretation of the term 'ja'l' (making) in a specific context, likely a verse. The author argues that the opponents' reading of 'ja'l' as meaning 'judgment' or 'naming' is invalid. He first establishes that 'tasbīr' (making into something) means to bring an attribute into existence. He notes that the linguistic alternatives the opponents propose are metaphorical, while the default is literal. Then he gives three proofs against their interpretation. The first proof is that if 'ja'l' were merely judgment or naming, then a person who reports God's attributes could be said to 'make God existent,' which is absurd. The second proof is that 'ja'l' is literally used for transformation implying existence, and to also use it for judgment would create homonymy without necessity. The third proof is a logical argument: even if we accept their interpretation, compulsion (jabr) still follows. This is because God's information about something entails that its opposite would be lying, which is impossible. Therefore the opposite is impossible, and the reported state must necessarily occur. Thus their weak interpretation would actually support the author's deterministic position.
