The Trial of Satan's Castings
The author refutes an objection to the verse about Satan's castings being a trial from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse about Satan's castings as a trial supports the Ash'ari argument for divine causation and refutes the objection that it attributes evil to Satan.

Core Idea
The trial from Satan's castings must imply divine causation, not independence.

This lesson examines a key proof from the Quran about Satan's castings being a trial from God. An objector claims the verse supports the opponent by attributing the casting to Satan. The author responds by unpacking the meaning of 'fitna' (trial) and arguing that if it is truly a test, it must have a causal connection to guidance and misguidance, leading to the conclusion of divine determination. This passage sharpens the Ash'ari doctrine of causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the flow: proof verse, objection, and the author's answer.
The passage is dense with logical reasoning.
2
Understand the objection
The objector argues that the verse attributes the casting to Satan, not God, which seems to contradict the Ash'ari position that all actions are from God.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author explains that 'fitna' (trial) is a distinguishing mark. He then argues that if the trial has a relation to guidance and misguidance, it must imply divine determination; otherwise it would be meaningless. This leads to obligation (wujub) on God's part.
Pay attention to the logical step about preponderance (rujhan) leading to obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : قوله تعالى : ( ليجعل ما يلقى الشيطان فتنة (٢) ) فإن قيل : ظاهر هذا النص حجة عليكم . لأنه تعالى أضاف الإلقاء إلى الشيطان . ثم نقول : هذا النص يدل على أنه تعالى يجعل ما يلقيه الشيطان فتنة . والمراد من الفتنة : العلامة المميزة للذين في قلوبهم مرض ، عن الذين ما كانوا كذلك . يقال : فتنة . إذا امتحنته . ولا يلزم من قولنا : جعل ما يلقيه الشيطان علامة لبعض الأمور . قولنا : إنه تعالى هو الذي خلق تلك الوساوس الباطلة التي يلقيها الشيطان في القلوب والجواب : قوله : إنه تعالى أضاف الإلقاء إلى الشيطان، إضافة الفعل إلى الفاعل . قلنا : سيجيء الجواب عنه إن شاء الله تعالى . قوله : ( المراد الفتنة : الامتحان ، قلنا : هذه الفتنة المفسرة بالامتحان . إما أن تكون نسبتها إلى الهداية والضلالة على السوية ، أو لا تكون . فإن كان الثاني كان كسائر الأمور ، التي لا تعلق لها بهذا الباب ، فلم تكن فتنة وامتحاناً . وإن كان الأول ، كان له مزيد اقتضاء للجهل والضلال . وقد دللنا على أنه متى حصل الرجحان ، فقد حصل الوجوب . وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب
The eighth proof: His statement, the Exalted: (That He may make what Satan casts a trial). If it is said: The apparent meaning of this text is an argument against you, because the Exalted attributed the casting to Satan. Then we say: This text indicates that the Exalted makes what Satan casts a trial. And the meaning of 'trial' (fitna) is the distinguishing mark for those in whose hearts there is disease from those who are not like that. It is said: 'fitna' if you test him. And it does not follow from our statement: 'He made what Satan casts a mark for some matters' that the Exalted is the one who created those false whisperings which Satan casts into the hearts. The answer: His saying that the Exalted attributed the casting to Satan as an attribution of an action to the agent. We say: The answer to this will come later, God willing. His saying: 'The meaning of fitna is the test' – we say: This fitna interpreted as the test – either its relation to guidance and misguidance is equal, or it is not. If it is the second, then it is like other matters that have no connection to this subject, so it would not be a trial and test. And if it is the first, then it has an additional implication of ignorance and misguidance. And we have demonstrated that whenever preponderance occurs, obligation occurs. And at that point the desired conclusion is obtained.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof and an objection and answer. The proof is the verse: 'That He may make what Satan casts a trial.' The objector argues that this verse supports the opponent because it attributes the casting to Satan, not God. Then the author responds by explaining the meaning of 'fitna' (trial) as a distinguishing mark. He argues that if the trial has no relation to guidance and misguidance, it is meaningless; if it does, then it necessarily leads to ignorance and misguidance, which implies obligation (wujub) on God's part. The author acknowledges that the attribution of the action to Satan will be answered later. This is a dense logical argument trying to show that the trial must be causally connected to misguidance, supporting the deterministic view.

Key Terms

Rational Proof for Dependence of Human Will on God's Will
Rational argument from impossibility of infinite regress to establish dependency of human will on God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that human actions ultimately depend on God's will, based on the impossibility of infinite regress in the chain of preponderance.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible; the preponderant for human will must be God.

The author presents a concise rational proof that complements the Quranic evidence. The argument focuses on the principle that any preference (tarjih) requires a preponderant (murajjih) outside the will itself. If the servant were the preponderant, an infinite regress would occur. This lesson will trace the logical steps of this proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible wills
Notice that the servant can have either a will for faith or for disbelief, and the choice between them demands a cause (preponderant).
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
If the servant were the cause, then the same question applies to that act of causing, leading to an endless chain. Infinite regress is deemed impossible by rational intuition.
3
Grasp the conclusion and its logical structure
The proof uses two premises (action depends on will; will depends on God) and a transitive principle: if A depends on B and B depends on C, then A depends on C.
This shows the proof is not just theological but logical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعقول : فهو أنه يمكن أن يحصل للعبد مشيئة الإيمان ، وأن يحصل له مشيئة الكفر بدلاً عن مشيئة الإيمان . فحصول إحدى المشيئين بدلاً عن الأخرى ، لا بد وأن يكون لترجيح مرجح . فإن كان ذلك للعبد ، عاد الطلب فيه . ويلزم التسلسل . وهو محال . وإن كان هو الله فهو المطلوب . ثبت بالقرآن والبرهان : أن صدور الفعل عن العبد ، موقوف على مشيئة لذلك الفعل، وثبت أيضاً بالقرآن والبرهان أن حدوث تلك المشيئة في قلب العبد متوقف على إن شاء الله إحداث تلك المشيئة في قلبه ، وثبت في بدائه العقول : أن الموقوف على الموقوف على الشيء ، موقوف على الشيء . فيلزم القطع بأن صدور الفعل عن العبد ، موقوف على مشيئة الله . وذلك هو المطلوب. وهذا برهان نفيس شريف ، مركب من مقدمتين مقررتين بالنص القاطع ، والبرهان الساطع
As for the rational: it is that it is possible for the servant to acquire a will for faith, and to acquire a will for disbelief instead of the will for faith. So the occurrence of one of the two wills instead of the other must be due to a preponderating preponderant. If that is from the servant, the demand returns in it, and infinite regress is necessitated, and that is impossible. And if it is from God, then that is what is sought. It is established by the Quran and proof: that the occurrence of an action from the servant is dependent on a will for that action, and it is also established by the Quran and proof that the occurrence of that will in the heart of the servant is dependent on God's willing the bringing about of that will in his heart, and it is established in the primary [intuitions] of intellects: that that which is dependent on that which is dependent on a thing is dependent on that thing. So it necessarily follows that the occurrence of an action from the servant is dependent on the will of God. That is what is sought. And this is a noble, excellent proof, composed of two premises established by the decisive text and the brilliant proof.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof (al-ma'qul) that the human will is dependent on God's will. The proof is in three parts. First, the servant can have a will for faith or a will for disbelief. The choice of one over the other requires a preponderant (murajjih). If the servant himself were that preponderant, then a similar question would arise about that act of preponderating, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore the preponderant must be God. Second, the author summarizes two premises: (a) the servant's action depends on his own will (proved by Quran and reason), and (b) the occurrence of that will depends on God's will (proved similarly). Third, by a principle of logic—if A depends on B, and B depends on C, then A depends on C—it follows that the servant's action depends directly on God's will. The author concludes by praising this proof as noble and excellent, based on decisive text (al-nass al-qati') and clear demonstration (al-burhan al-sati').

Key Terms
مشيئة
ترجيح
مرجح
تسلسل
بدائه العقول
النص القاطع
البرهان الساطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof denies human choice entirely, making human will illusory.
Correction: The proof only establishes that the ultimate origin of the will is from God, not that human willing is nonexistent. The human will is real and effective, but its existence depends on God's creative will.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the servant himself were the preponderant for his own will?
A) The will would be self-caused and eternal.
B) It would lead to an infinite regress (tasalsul).
C) It would contradict the Quranic verses.
D) It would make God unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The rational proof shows that human will must be dependent on God's will because otherwise an infinite regress would occur, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself choosing between right and wrong, do you ever stop to realize that your very desire to choose good is not entirely your own? How can this truth deepen your sense of gratitude and urgency in seeking God's guidance before every decision?
?
If your will to obey God ultimately depends on His will, how does that reshape your response to your own sins? Are you genuinely blaming yourself, or do you subtly feel less accountable? Will you today repent not only for the sin but also for the neglect of asking God to give you the will to obey?
?
This proof shows that even your most private intentions are not self-generated. How comfortable are you with being completely dependent on God for your moral choices? Will you start each morning by saying, 'O Allah, I cannot will anything unless You will it; so grant me the will to follow Your guidance'?

Second Answer: 'Aghfalna' Means Creation of Forgetfulness
Al-Razi argues that 'aghfalna' must mean creation of forgetfulness, with a proof that the servant cannot create his own forgetfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that 'aghfalna' must mean creation of forgetfulness, based on a rational proof that the servant cannot create his own forgetfulness.

Core Idea
The servant cannot create his own forgetfulness; only Allah can.

Al-Razi now provides a second answer to the objection, conceding that the word 'aghfalna' could apply to multiple meanings. However, he insists that the context forces it to mean 'creation of forgetfulness.' He supports this with a powerful rational proof showing that the human being is incapable of originating their own forgetfulness, thus it must be from Allah. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting how al-Razi structures his argument. He first states his position, then provides a proof by dividing the attempt to create forgetfulness into two cases.
Pay attention to the logical flow: premise, division, refutation of each part, conclusion.
2
Understand the two cases
Al-Razi says if the servant tried to create forgetfulness, it would be either absolute forgetfulness or forgetfulness of a specific thing. Grasp why each is impossible.
Absolute forgetfulness has no specific object; specific forgetfulness requires awareness of the object, creating a contradiction.
3
Follow the refutation of each case
For absolute forgetfulness: there is no reason it would attach to one thing over another. For specific forgetfulness: intending it requires knowing the thing, but knowing prevents forgetting.
The second case is the core of the proof.
4
See the conclusion
Since both cases are impossible, the servant cannot create forgetfulness. Therefore, the originator must be Allah. The Quranic verse then confirms this rational proof.
Al-Razi calls this a 'decisive rational point' (nuktah qāṭi‘ah ‘aqliyyah).
5
Reflect on the broader significance
This argument defends divine omnipotence: even forgetfulness, which seems like a lack, is a positive creation from Allah.
Reinforces that nothing occurs in existence without Allah's creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في الجواب : إنا نسلم كون اللفظ ، بالنسبة إلى هذين المفهومين . إلا أنا نقول : يجب حمل قوله : ( أغفلنا ) على خلق الغفلة . وذلك لأن العبد يمتنع كونه موجداً لغفلة نفسه. والدليل عليه : أنه إذا حاول إيجاد الغفلة. فإما أن يحاول إيجاد مطلق الغفلة ، أو يحاول إيجاد الغفلة عن شيء معين . والأول باطل . وإلا لم يكن بأن تحصل له الغفلة عن هذا أولى من أن تحصل له الغفلة عن ذاك . لأن الطبيعة الحسنة ، نسبتها إلى كل الأنواع على السوية . وأما الثاني - فهو أيضاً محال . لأن الغفلة عن كذا عبارة عن غفلة لا تمتاز عن سائر الغفلات ، إلا بكونها منتسبة إلى ذلك الشيء المعين ، فلا يمكنه أن يقصد إلى إيقاع الغفلة عن كذا ، إلا إذا اعتقد كونه غفلة عن كذا . ولا يمكنه أن يعتقد كون هذا الاعتقاد غفلة عن كذا ، لا إذا تصور ذلك الشيء . لأن العلم بنسبة أمر إلى أمر ، مشروط بتصور كل واحد من المنتسبين . فثبت : أنه لا يمكنه أن يقصد إلى تحصيل الغفلة عن كذا ، إلا مع الشعور بكذا ، لكن الغفلة عن كذا مضادة للشعور . فثبت : أن العبد لا يمكنه إيجاد هذه الغفلة ، إلا عند اجتماع الضدين. وهذا محال ، فوجب أن يكون ذاك أيضاً محالاً . وإذا ثبت هذا ، ثبت أنه لا موجد لغفلات العباد إلا الله تعالى . وهذه نكتة قاطعة عقلية في بيان أن خالق الغفلات هو الله تعالى . وحينئذ نجعل نص القرآن مؤكداً لهذا البرهان . فثبت أن قوله : ( ولا تطع من أغفلنا قلبه عن ذكرنا ) لا يمكن حمله ، إلا على خلق الغفلة في القلب
And the second aspect in the answer: We concede the word's application to both concepts. However, we say: It is necessary to interpret His saying (aghfalna) as the creation of forgetfulness. That is because the servant cannot be the originator (mūjid) of his own forgetfulness. The proof of that: If he attempts to bring about forgetfulness, either he attempts to bring about absolute forgetfulness, or he attempts to bring about forgetfulness of a specific thing. The first is false; otherwise he would not be more likely to have forgetfulness of this than of that, because the simple nature (al-tabi‘ah al-ḥasanah) relates equally to all types. As for the second, it is also impossible. Because forgetfulness of something is a forgetfulness that is distinguished from other forgetfulnesses only by being related to that specific thing. So he cannot intend to produce forgetfulness of something unless he believes it to be forgetfulness of that thing. And he cannot believe that this belief is forgetfulness of something unless he has a conception of that thing. For knowledge of the relation of one thing to another is conditional upon the conception of each of the two relata. So it is established that he cannot intend to acquire forgetfulness of something except with awareness of that thing, but forgetfulness of something is contrary to awareness. So it is established that the servant cannot bring about this forgetfulness except with the conjunction of two opposites. This is impossible, so that must also be impossible. And once this is established, it is established that there is no originator of the servants' forgetfulness except Allah the Exalted. And this is a decisive rational point (nuktah qāṭi‘ah ‘aqliyyah) in demonstrating that the creator of forgetfulness is Allah the Exalted. And then we make the text of the Qur’an confirm this proof. So it is established that His saying: (And do not obey him whose heart We have made forgetful of Our remembrance) cannot be interpreted except as the creation of forgetfulness in the heart.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents the second answer to the objection. Al-Razi begins by conceding that the word 'aghfalna' could apply to both meanings, but then argues that the context of the verse forces it to mean creation of forgetfulness. He provides a rational proof that the human servant cannot be the originator (mūjid) of his own forgetfulness. The proof proceeds by dividing the attempt to create forgetfulness into two cases: (1) trying to create absolute forgetfulness, and (2) trying to create forgetfulness of a specific thing. The first is impossible because absolute forgetfulness would have no specific object, so there is no reason it would occur concerning one thing over another. The second is also impossible because to intend a specific forgetfulness, one must conceive of the thing to be forgotten; but if one is aware of it, one cannot simultaneously be forgetful of it. This would require the conjunction of opposites (awareness and unawareness), which is impossible. Therefore, the servant cannot create his own forgetfulness. The only possible originator is Allah. Al-Razi calls this a decisive rational point (nuktah qāṭi‘ah ‘aqliyyah) and says that the Quranic verse confirms it. Thus, the verse 'aghfalna' must mean creation of forgetfulness.

Key Terms
موجد (mūjid)
غفلة (ghaflah)
ضدان (ḍiddān)
مطلق (muṭlaq)
معين (mu‘ayyan)
نكتة قاطعة عقلية (nuktah qāṭi‘ah ‘aqliyyah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forgetfulness is merely the absence of knowledge and does not require an active creator; it just happens naturally.
Correction: Al-Razi's proof shows that forgetfulness cannot be created by the servant because intending it leads to a contradiction. Therefore, it must be a positive act of creation by Allah.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi argue that the servant cannot create forgetfulness of a specific thing?
A) Because forgetfulness is a kind of ignorance and ignorance is non-existence.
B) Because intending a specific forgetfulness requires awareness of that thing, which contradicts forgetfulness.
C) Because the Quran explicitly says so.
D) Because forgetfulness comes only from Satan.

Key Takeaway
Forgetfulness is a positive creation by Allah, as the rational proof shows the servant cannot bring it about.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself forgetful of Allah's remembrance, do you recognize that even that forgetfulness is created by Him? How does that change how you respond—do you turn to Him in repentance or remain passive?
?
Think of a time you tried to force yourself to forget a painful memory. Did you realize that the very act of trying required you to hold that memory in mind? What does this teach you about the limits of your own power?
?
If only Allah creates forgetfulness, what responsibility does that place on you to seek His help in staying mindful? Will you commit today to a specific du'a or practice that acknowledges your utter dependence on Him for every state, including heedlessness?

The Second Proof: Linguistic Argument from the Quran and Arabic Usage
Linguistic proof showing 'hasanah' in the Quran refers to worldly events, not acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Arabic phrasing of the Quran provides evidence that 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' refer to worldly events, not moral acts.

Core Idea
Arabic usage shows hasanah/sayyi'ah in the verse are events, not deeds.

This passage presents a linguistic proof from the Quranic verse. The author argues that the way the words 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' are used in Arabic—specifically the verb 'اصابت' (befell)—indicates that they refer to things that happen to a person, not acts they commit. Understanding this distinction is crucial for interpreting the verse correctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Pay attention to the verb 'أصابت' and its construction.
2
Identify the key linguistic distinction
Notice the difference between 'أصابتني حسنة' (a hasanah befell me) and 'أصبت حسنة' (I did a hasanah). The author argues that the former is not used for acts.
This is the core of the proof.
3
Apply the distinction to the verse
The Quran says 'وإن تصبهم حسنة' (if a hasanah befalls them). The author concludes that this matches 'أصابتهم' not 'أصابوا', so it must refer to a worldly event.
4
Consider the counterfactual
The author says that if the verse meant an act, it would have said 'إن أصبتم حسنة' (if you do a hasanah). This reinforces the conclusion.
This step solidifies the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن الحسنة إذا أريد بها الخير والطاعة . لا يقال فيها : أصابتني . وإنما يقال : أصبتها . وليس في كلام العرب : أصابت فلانا حسنة . بمعنى أنه عمل خيراً، وأصابته سيئة بمعنى عمل معصية . بل نقول : لو كان المراد ما ذكرتم، لقال : إن أصبتم حسنة .
The second: If by 'hasanah' is meant goodness and obedience, it is not said 'it befell me' but rather 'I did it.' And it is not in the speech of the Arabs: 'a hasanah befell so-and-so' meaning he did a good deed, nor 'a sayyi'ah befell him' meaning he committed an act of disobedience. Rather we say: if what you mentioned were intended, He would have said: 'if you do a hasanah.'
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is the second proof in a series of arguments. The author argues that the words 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' in the verse refer to worldly benefits and calamities, not acts of obedience and disobedience. The proof is linguistic: in Arabic, one does not say 'a good deed befell me' when one performs an act of obedience; rather, one says 'I did a good deed.' Similarly, for a sin, one says 'I committed a sin,' not 'a sin befell me.' The verse, however, uses the expression 'if a hasanah befalls them' (وإن تصبهم حسنة), indicating that the intended meaning is not an act but a worldly blessing or calamity that happens to a person. If the intended meaning were an act, the wording would have been 'if you do a hasanah.' Thus, the linguistic usage confirms the interpretation.

Key Terms
حسنة
سيئة
طاعة
معصية
أصابت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: 'Hasanah' in this verse means a good deed (obedience).
Correction: The Arabic linguistic construction 'اصابتهم حسنة' (a hasanah befell them) is not used for acts; it is used for events that happen to a person. Therefore, 'hasanah' here must refer to a worldly blessing.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the second proof?
A) 'Hasanah' must mean an act of obedience because it is used in the Quran.
B) The word 'hasanah' cannot refer to an act because the Arabic language does not say 'a hasanah befell him' for an act.
C) The verse is about predestination and free will.
D) The word 'hasanah' is ambiguous and can mean both good deeds and worldly blessings.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's use of 'أصابتهم حسنة' indicates that 'hasanah' here refers to a worldly blessing, not a moral act, based on Arabic linguistic patterns.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you ever impose your own assumptions on the meaning of words, or do you pause to check the linguistic usage as taught by this lesson? Will you commit to studying Arabic now, even if only a few key terms, to safeguard your understanding?
?
This lesson shows that a simple linguistic error can change the entire meaning of a verse. Take a moment: in what areas of your faith have you assumed a meaning without evidence? Are you willing to humble yourself and verify, even if it challenges what you thought you knew?
?
If you had never encountered this linguistic argument, you might have persisted in a wrong interpretation of the Quran. How many other verses might you be misunderstanding due to language? What will you do today to begin correcting that?

The Third Proof: The Word 'Hasanah' is Ambiguous
Presents the third proof that 'hasanah' is used homonymously and context specifies worldly meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the word 'hasanah' in the verse cannot refer to both worldly benefits and acts of obedience simultaneously, based on the principle of homonymy.

Core Idea
A homonym cannot convey multiple meanings at once; context chooses one.

This lesson presents the third proof from the author. He argues that the word 'hasanah' is a homonym, and since the commentators agree that worldly benefits are intended in this context, it cannot also refer to acts of obedience. This relies on a key linguistic principle about homonyms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the main argument.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the key term 'homonym'
A homonym (مشترك) is a word that has multiple distinct meanings. In this case, 'hasanah' can mean either acts of obedience or worldly benefits.
3
Identify the commentators' consensus
Note that the author cites the consensus of the commentators (المفسرون) that worldly benefits are intended. This is the contextual clue that specifies one meaning.
4
Grasp the logical conclusion
Because the word is a homonym and the context specifies worldly benefits, it is impossible for acts of obedience to also be intended. A homonym cannot refer to both meanings simultaneously in a single utterance.
5
Reflect on the principle
Consider why using a homonym for both meanings is not permissible. This principle is fundamental in linguistic interpretation and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لفظ الحسنة واقع بالاشتراك على الطاعات، وعلى المنافع الدنيوية، وههنا أجمع المفسرون على أن المنافع مرادة، فيمتنع كون الطاعات مرادة . ضرورة أنه لا يجوز استعمال اللفظ المشترك في مفهوميه جميعاً
The third: The word 'hasanah' is used homonymously for acts of obedience and for worldly benefits. And here the commentators have agreed that worldly benefits are intended, so it is impossible that acts of obedience are intended. Necessarily, because it is not permissible to use a homonymous word for both of its meanings simultaneously.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. The author states that the word 'hasanah' is a homonym (mushtarak) that can mean 'acts of obedience' or 'worldly benefits.' In this particular verse, the consensus of the commentators (ajma'a al-mufassirun) is that worldly benefits (al-manafi' al-dunyawiyyah) are intended. Therefore, it is impossible that acts of obedience are also intended, because a homonymous word cannot be used to convey both of its meanings at the same time in a single utterance. This argument relies on a principle of linguistic interpretation: when a word has multiple distinct meanings, the context must specify which meaning is intended, and it cannot refer to multiple incompatible meanings simultaneously.

Key Terms
اشتراك
مشترك
طاعات
منافع دنيوية
مفسرون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the word 'hasanah' can mean both a good deed and a worldly blessing at once in this verse.
Correction: The author explains that because 'hasanah' is a homonym and the context specifies worldly benefits (by consensus of commentators), it cannot simultaneously refer to acts of obedience. A homonym can only convey one meaning per usage.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say acts of obedience cannot be intended by 'hasanah' in this verse?
A) Because the word 'hasanah' is only used for worldly blessings.
B) Because the commentators agreed that worldly benefits are meant, and a homonym cannot convey both meanings at once.
C) Because the verse is about predestination.
D) Because the word is not actually a homonym.

Key Takeaway
The word 'hasanah' in this context refers only to worldly benefits, not acts of obedience, because a homonym cannot have both meanings at once.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you ever assume a word means what you want it to mean, rather than what the context and scholars have determined? How does this lesson challenge you to submit to the intended meaning even when it contradicts your desires?
?
Think of a time you interpreted a Quranic verse in a way that justified your own actions or beliefs without checking scholarly consensus. Will you now seek the context and the tafsir before forming your understanding?
?
This lesson shows that a single word can have multiple meanings, but only one is intended at a time. Are you willing to accept that you might have misunderstood some verses and to correct your beliefs based on sound interpretation? What is one verse you need to revisit?

The First Aspect: Wonder at Lack of Understanding
First argument: wonder verse shows knowledge is by servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse expressing wonder at people's failure to understand indicates that knowledge is produced by the servant.

Core Idea
Wonder at lack of understanding implies that understanding is the servant's own action.

This lesson examines the first of two arguments from subsequent verses. The author cites a verse where Allah expresses wonder at people who hardly understand any statement. This wonder, he argues, would be pointless if knowledge were created directly by Allah. Instead, it shows that knowledge is brought into existence by the servant's own act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the verse quoted.
2
Understand the argument
The wonder would be meaningless if Allah was the only creator of understanding. The wonder implies the servant is responsible.
3
Reflect on the implication
This supports the author's claim that 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' are not acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنه تعالى قال بعد هذه الآية : ما دل على أن المراد بالحسنة والسيئة في هذه الآيات، ليس هو الطاعات والمعاصي . وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : إن تعالى قال بعد هذه الآية : فمال هؤلاء القوم ، لا يكادون يفقهون حديثاً ) ولو كان حصول العلم والمعرفة بخلق الله ، لم يبق لهذا التعجب معنى . لأن السبب في أن لا تحصل هذه المعرفة على قول من يقول خالق أعمال العباد هو الله : هو أنه تعالى ما خلقها وما أوجدها . وذلك يبطل هذا التعجب . فلما حصل هذا التعجب ، دل ذلك على أن العلم والمعرفة إنما يحصلان بإيجاد العبد . الثاني : إنه تعالى قال بعد هذه الآية : ( ما أصابك من حسنة فمن الله . وما أصابك من سيئة فمن نفسك ) وجه الاستدلال به : أن لفظ السيئة تارة يقع على البلية والمحنة ، وتارة يقع على الذنب والمعصية . ثم إنه تعالى اضاف السيئة إلى نفسه بقوله : ( قل : كل من عند الله ) وأضاف السيئة في آخر هذه الآية إلى العبد بقوله : ( وما أصابك من سيئة فمن نفسك ) ولا بد من التوفيق بين هاتين الآيتين. ولما كانت السيئة بمعنى البلية والمحنة ، مضافة إلى الله تعالى ، وجب أن تكون السيئة بمعنى المعصية ، مضافة إلى العبد ، حتى يزول التناقض بين هاتين الآيتين المتجاورتين
The fourth: Allah said after this verse what indicates that what is meant by 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' in these verses is not acts of obedience and disobedience. Its explanation is from two aspects: The first: Allah said after this verse: 'What is the matter with these people that they hardly understand any statement?' And if knowledge and understanding occurred by Allah's creation, there would be no meaning for this wonder. Because the reason for not obtaining this knowledge, according to the one who says that the Creator of the deeds of servants is Allah, is that He did not create them and did not bring them into existence. And that nullifies this wonder. So when this wonder occurred, it indicates that knowledge and understanding only occur by the servant's own bringing into existence. The second: Allah said after this verse: 'Whatever hasanah befalls you is from Allah, and whatever sayyi'ah befalls you is from yourself.' The way of inferring from it is that the word 'sayyi'ah' sometimes applies to affliction and hardship, and sometimes applies to sin and disobedience. Then Allah attributed 'sayyi'ah' to Himself in His saying: 'Say: All is from Allah,' and He attributed 'sayyi'ah' in the end of this verse to the servant in His saying: 'and whatever sayyi'ah befalls you is from yourself.' There must be reconciliation between these two verses. And since 'sayyi'ah' in the sense of affliction and hardship is attributed to Allah, it follows that 'sayyi'ah' in the sense of sin must be attributed to the servant, so that the contradiction between these two adjacent verses is removed.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, which draws evidence from two verses that follow the verse in question. The author first cites the verse 'What is the matter with these people that they hardly understand any statement?' (Quran 4:78). If knowledge and understanding were created directly by Allah, then the astonishment expressed in this verse would be meaningless, because the reason people fail to understand would be that Allah did not create that knowledge. But the verse expresses wonder at their lack of understanding, implying that the cause is their own failure to bring about understanding through their own action. This indicates that knowledge and understanding are produced by the servant, not by Allah. The second aspect is the verse: 'Whatever hasanah befalls you is from Allah, and whatever sayyi'ah befalls you is from yourself' (Quran 4:79). The author notes that 'sayyi'ah' can mean either affliction or sin. In an earlier verse, 'sayyi'ah' is attributed to Allah ('all is from Allah'), so if 'sayyi'ah' there meant sin, it would contradict the later verse attributing sin to the servant. Therefore, 'sayyi'ah' in the earlier verse must mean affliction, so that in the later verse 'sayyi'ah' meaning sin is attributed to the servant. This reconciliation shows that the words in the original verse refer to worldly events, not moral acts.

فهم
خلق
إيجاد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the wonder verse is simply expressing surprise without theological implication.
Correction: The author shows that the wonder would be meaningless unless the servant is the agent of understanding.

Checkpoint
What does the verse 'What is the matter with these people that they hardly understand any statement?' imply according to the author?
A) That Allah did not create understanding in them.
B) That the people are themselves responsible for not understanding.
C) That understanding is created by Allah.
D) That the verse is not relevant.

Key Takeaway
The wonder verse implies that understanding is produced by the servant, not by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to understand a matter in your deen, do you fall back on the excuse that Allah hasn't 'given' you understanding, or do you take ownership to seek it out? What would it look like to truly bring that understanding into existence through effort?
?
The verse expresses wonder at those who hardly understand. Do you want to be the object of that divine wonder? What are you doing today to ensure you are a person who 'fathoms' the speech of Allah?
?
If knowledge is something you produce by your own act, not passively received, then what are you actually producing? Is your mind engaged or lazy? What step will you take before this day ends to actively bring understanding into existence?

The Second Aspect: Reconciling the Two Verses
Second argument: reconciliation of two verses shows sayyi'ah as sin is from servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reconciling the two verses about sayyi'ah proves that the original verse refers to worldly events.

Core Idea
Sayyi'ah as sin must be from the servant; as affliction, from Allah.

In this second argument, the author draws on two adjacent verses. One says 'all is from Allah', the other says 'sayyi'ah is from yourself'. To avoid contradiction, the author explains that 'sayyi'ah' has two meanings: affliction (from Allah) and sin (from the servant). This reconciliation shows that in the original verse, 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' refer to worldly blessings and afflictions, not moral acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two verses.
2
Understand the ambiguity of sayyi'ah
The word can mean affliction or sin.
3
See the reconciliation
Attributing sin to the servant avoids contradiction.

When Is Repudiation of Evil Good or Evil?
The author responds to an objection that repudiation of evil is not evil, showing when it is good and when it is evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the moral status of repudiating evil depends on the intention behind the act, not on the act itself.

Core Idea
Repudiation of evil is only good when done because it is evil, not to lead to another evil.

This passage presents a subtle ethical principle: the same action—repudiating evil—can be either good or evil depending on the motivation. The author uses the example of inciting enmity between Christians and Jews to illustrate this point, and then provides the key distinction that resolves the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the objection and answer structure.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that repudiation of evil is always good, so inciting enmity (which is a form of repudiation) cannot be evil.
The objection concludes that the enmity is not among evil things.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer introduces a condition: repudiation is only good when done because the action is evil. If done to call to another evil, it becomes evil.
This distinction saves the possibility that incitement can be evil if motivated by a corrupt purpose.
4
Apply the distinction
Consider the example: if the incitement was done to lead to another evil (like promoting disbelief), it is evil. Thus the objection fails.
The author concludes that the desired conclusion is obtained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أغرى بين النصارى واليهود، فيما كان يفعله كل فريق منهم من ضروب الكفر بالله . وإنكار القبيح ليس بقبيح، بل هو حسن . فدل ذلك على أن تلك العداوة ، ليست من القبائح .
والجواب : إن إنكار القبيح إنما يحسن إذا أنكره ، لكونه قبيحاً . أما إذا أنكره لغرض أن يدعوه إلى قبيح آخر ؛ كان قبيحاً . وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب
He incited enmity between the Christians and the Jews, in what each group was doing of sorts of disbelief in God. And repudiation of evil is not evil, rather it is good. So that indicates that that enmity is not among the evil things. The answer: Indeed, repudiation of evil is only good when he repudiates it because it is evil. But if he repudiates it for the purpose of calling him to another evil, it is evil. And thus the desired conclusion is obtained.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage records an objection and its answer concerning the nature of repudiation of evil (إنكار القبيح). The objection states that inciting enmity between Christians and Jews is not evil because repudiation of evil is itself good, not evil. The answer clarifies that repudiation of evil is only good when the motivation is the evil itself; if the repudiation is done to lead to another evil, it becomes evil. Thus, in the case at hand, if the incitement of enmity is done for a corrupt purpose, it is evil, and the objection fails. The passage concludes that the desired conclusion is reached.

Key Terms
إنكار القبيح
قبيح
حسن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think repudiation of evil is always categorically good.
Correction: The author shows that its goodness depends on the intention: it is good only when done because the action is evil, not when done to lead to another evil.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, when is repudiation of evil (إنكار القبيح) good?
A) When it is done for any reason
B) When it is done to lead to another evil
C) When it is done because the action itself is evil
D) It is never good

Key Takeaway
The moral value of repudiating evil depends on the intention: it is good when motivated by the evil itself, but evil if used as a means to a corrupt end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you criticize someone's wrongdoing, do you examine your own heart to see if you are doing it because the action is truly evil, or because it serves your own agenda? Will you ask Allah to purify your intentions before your next act of forbidding evil?
?
Have you ever used the excuse of 'repudiating evil' to justify harshness or harm towards others? How can you ensure that your repudiation is only for the sake of Allah and not for worldly gain or pride?
?
If your repudiation of evil leads to a greater evil, would you be willing to remain silent? How committed are you to the truth even when it costs you personally?

The Adhan and Iqama: A Test of Certainty in Transmission
First aspect of second proof: adhan and iqama as a test case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author uses the example of the adhan and iqama to argue that even the most public and frequent practices were not transmitted with certainty, thereby challenging the reliability of isolated reports in doctrinal matters.

Core Idea
If even the daily call to prayer cannot be agreed upon, can we trust isolated reports on subtle matters?

The author begins the second proof by asserting that even the most honorable narrators—the Companions—failed to achieve certainty in transmitting simple, observable acts. He focuses on the call to prayer (adhan) and the second call (iqama), which were performed publicly for years. Despite this, narrators disagreed on key details. This passage sets the stage for questioning the reliability of ahad reports in matters of belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the author's claim that even the most apparent matters (أظهر الأمور) were not accurately transmitted.
Pay attention to the rhetorical questions.
2
Identify the two disputed points
Find the two details about the adhan and iqama that narrators could not agree on: whether tarjih is part of the adhan and whether the iqama is recited singly or doubly.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author argues that if such widely observed practices could not be agreed upon, how can we trust narrations on subtle issues heard only once? Consider the force of this argument.
This connects to the broader critique of ahad reports in yaqiniyyat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على أنه لا يجوز التمسك بأخبار الآحاد في اليقينيات : أن نقول : أشرف طبقات الرواة : طبقة الصحابة ، ثم إن رواياتهم لا تفيد اليقين . أما في حق الصحابة . فيدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن أظهر الأمور وأجلاها : أمر الأذان والإقامة . وذلك لأنه - عليه السلام - واظب عليهما مدة عشر سنين ، أو أكثر أو أقل . والكل كانوا يشاهدونه ، في كل يوم خمس مرات . والخلفاء الراشدون واظبوا عليها مدة ثلاثين سنة مع الجمع العظيم. ثم إن الرواة ما ضبطوا أحوال الأذان ، ولا أحوال الإقامة . أما أحوال الأذان . فهو أن الترجيح هل هو معتبر في الأذان أم لا ؟ وأما الإقامة. فإنها هي فرادى أو مثناة ؟ وليس لقائل أن يقول : لعلها كانت مرة فرادى، ومرة مثناة. لأنا نقول : لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن ينقل الراوي أنها كانت مرة فرادى، ومرة مثناة ، فلما عجزوا عن ضبط هذا القدر مع أن الكل شاهدوا هذه المدة الطويلة ، فكيف يبقى الوثوق برواياتهم في المسائل الدقيقة ، وفي المباحث الغامضة. مع أنهم - بزعمهم - ما سمعوها إلا مرة واحدة ؟ . ومن ذلك ما روي أنه - عليه السلام - أفرد الحج ، أو قارن ، أو تمتع . مع أن الحج : حجة واحدة، ومع أنا نعلم أنه لم يأت بالجميع ومن ذلك أنه ـ عليه السلام - هل كان يرفع يديه عند الركوع والانتصاب ؟ فابن مسعود ، روى عدم الرفع وابن عمر روى حصول الرفع . ومرة ذكر أنه في التكبيرة الأولى ، كان يرفع يديه إلى حذو المنكبين ، أو إلى حذو الأذنين . فكل هذه الأمور أمور مشاهدة محسوسة . يكفي في معرفتها سلامة الحاسة وأصل العقل . فلما عجزوا عن ضبط هذا القدر ، فكيف يقدرون على ضبط المسائل الفقهية ، التي ما سمعوها إلا مرة واحدة ، ثم إنهم ما كتبوا ذلك المجموع . بل اعتمدوا على مجرد حفظهم . ثم نقلوه بعد خمسين سنة أو أكثر
The second proof that it is not permissible to adhere to isolated reports (akhbar al-ahad) in certainties (yaqiniyyat): we say: The most honourable class of narrators is the class of the Companions, yet their narrations do not yield certainty. As for the right of the Companions, that is indicated by aspects: The first: The most apparent and clearest of matters are the matter of the call to prayer (adhan) and the second call (iqama). Because he—peace be upon him—persisted in them for ten years, or more or less. And everyone used to observe him five times every day. And the Rightly Guided Caliphs persisted in them for thirty years with a great multitude. Yet the narrators did not record the conditions of the adhan nor the conditions of the iqama. As for the conditions of the adhan: whether raising the voice (tarjih) is considered in the adhan or not. And as for the iqama: is it single (furada) or double (mathna)? And no one may say: Perhaps it was once single and once double. Because we say: If that were the case, then the narrator would have to report that it was once single and once double. When they failed to record this amount, even though everyone witnessed this long period, how can trust remain in their narrations in subtle matters and in obscure investigations, especially since—in their claim—they heard them only once? And from that is what was narrated that he—peace be upon him—performed hajj individually (ifrad), or in conjunction (qiran), or in tamattu', even though the hajj was one hajj, and we know that he did not do all. And from that: whether he—peace be upon him—used to raise his hands at bowing (ruku') and standing up (intisab)? Ibn Mas'ud narrated the non-raising, and Ibn 'Umar narrated the raising. And once it is mentioned that in the first takbirah he used to raise his hands to the level of the shoulders or to the level of the ears. All these matters are observable, sensible matters; sufficient for knowing them is soundness of the senses and basic intellect. When they failed to record this amount, how can they be able to record the fiqhi issues which they heard only once, and then they did not write that collection, but relied solely on their memory, and then transmitted it after fifty years or more?
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents the first aspect (wajh) of the second proof that isolated reports (akhbar al-ahad) cannot yield certainty in matters of belief. The argument begins by acknowledging the high status of the Companions as narrators, but then demonstrates that even their narrations fail to provide certainty. The author uses the example of the most public and frequently repeated acts of worship: the adhan (call to prayer) and iqama (second call). The Prophet performed these daily for many years, and the caliphs continued them for decades in front of large congregations. Despite this, narrators could not agree on the details: whether the adhan includes tarjih (raising the voice), and whether the iqama is recited singly or in pairs. The author refutes the possibility that both forms occurred interchangeably, arguing that if that were the case, narrators would have transmitted the variation. Their failure to do so, despite the abundance of witnesses, undermines confidence in their transmission of more subtle matters heard only once. The passage then adds examples: the type of hajj performed by the Prophet (ifrad, qiran, or tamattu'), and whether he raised his hands at ruku' and intisab. Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn 'Umar disagreed on the latter. The author concludes with a rhetorical question: if they could not agree on such clear and commonly observed acts, how can they be trusted with fiqhi issues they heard only once, never wrote down, and transmitted decades later from memory? This argument is used to challenge the reliability of ahad reports in establishing certain knowledge.

Key Terms
أخبار الآحاد
اليقينيات
الصحابة
الأذان
الإقامة
الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is attacking the integrity of the Companions.
Correction: The argument is about the impossibility of achieving certainty through ahad reports, not about the trustworthiness of the Sahaba.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the adhan and iqama?
A) They were performed only once by the Prophet.
B) They were performed daily for years, yet narrators could not agree on their details.
C) The Companions disagreed on whether they are obligatory.
D) They were not transmitted by any Companion.

Key Takeaway
Even the most public and frequently observed practices like the call to prayer were not transmitted with certainty, demonstrating the limitations of ahad reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the most public act of worship—the call to prayer—cannot be transmitted with full agreement, what does that say about the certainty of your own religious knowledge? Are you willing to hold your beliefs with the same humility the passage demands, or do you insist on absolute certainty where it may not be warranted?
?
The Sahaba, despite their virtue, failed to preserve precise details of the adhan and iqama. How does this challenge your confidence in the accuracy of a single hadith you rely on for a practice or belief? Will you investigate whether that report is supported by mass transmission, or remain uncritically attached?
?
This passage argues that we cannot trust narrations on subtle matters if even obvious ones are disputed. When you hear a claim about religion from a single source—whether a scholar, a book, or a video—do you demand corroboration, or do you accept it as truth? What would it take for you to change that habit?

Further Examples: Hajj and Hand-Raising
Additional examples and conclusion of the first wajh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the additional examples of hajj and hand-raising during prayer further illustrate the failure of sahaha to achieve certainty, strengthening the argument against ahad reports in doctrinal matters.

Core Idea
If the Prophet's own hajj and hand movements are disputed, how can isolated reports on complex fiqh be trusted?

The author continues the first aspect of the second proof by providing two more examples: the type of hajj performed by the Prophet and whether he raised his hands during prayer. Both are observable acts by large numbers of people, yet conflicting narrations exist. The author uses these to deepen the critique and conclude that if such basic matters are uncertain, ahad reports on more complex issues heard only once and transmitted decades later are even less reliable.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples
Read the examples about hajj (ifrad, qiran, tamattu') and hand-raising at ruku'. Notice the conflicting reports from Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn 'Umar.
2
Analyze the author's reasoning
The author argues that these are observable, sensible matters that require only basic intellect. Disagreement shows the unreliability of ahad reports.
Think about why he chooses these specific examples.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by comparing these clear matters to more complex fiqhi issues that were heard once and transmitted long after. Understand the force of the argument.
This completes the first wajh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على أنه لا يجوز التمسك بأخبار الآحاد في اليقينيات : أن نقول : أشرف طبقات الرواة : طبقة الصحابة ، ثم إن رواياتهم لا تفيد اليقين . أما في حق الصحابة . فيدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن أظهر الأمور وأجلاها : أمر الأذان والإقامة . وذلك لأنه - عليه السلام - واظب عليهما مدة عشر سنين ، أو أكثر أو أقل . والكل كانوا يشاهدونه ، في كل يوم خمس مرات . والخلفاء الراشدون واظبوا عليها مدة ثلاثين سنة مع الجمع العظيم. ثم إن الرواة ما ضبطوا أحوال الأذان ، ولا أحوال الإقامة . أما أحوال الأذان . فهو أن الترجيح هل هو معتبر في الأذان أم لا ؟ وأما الإقامة. فإنها هي فرادى أو مثناة ؟ وليس لقائل أن يقول : لعلها كانت مرة فرادى، ومرة مثناة. لأنا نقول : لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن ينقل الراوي أنها كانت مرة فرادى، ومرة مثناة ، فلما عجزوا عن ضبط هذا القدر مع أن الكل شاهدوا هذه المدة الطويلة ، فكيف يبقى الوثوق برواياتهم في المسائل الدقيقة ، وفي المباحث الغامضة. مع أنهم - بزعمهم - ما سمعوها إلا مرة واحدة ؟ . ومن ذلك ما روي أنه - عليه السلام - أفرد الحج ، أو قارن ، أو تمتع . مع أن الحج : حجة واحدة، ومع أنا نعلم أنه لم يأت بالجميع ومن ذلك أنه ـ عليه السلام - هل كان يرفع يديه عند الركوع والانتصاب ؟ فابن مسعود ، روى عدم الرفع وابن عمر روى حصول الرفع . ومرة ذكر أنه في التكبيرة الأولى ، كان يرفع يديه إلى حذو المنكبين ، أو إلى حذو الأذنين . فكل هذه الأمور أمور مشاهدة محسوسة . يكفي في معرفتها سلامة الحاسة وأصل العقل . فلما عجزوا عن ضبط هذا القدر ، فكيف يقدرون على ضبط المسائل الفقهية ، التي ما سمعوها إلا مرة واحدة ، ثم إنهم ما كتبوا ذلك المجموع . بل اعتمدوا على مجرد حفظهم . ثم نقلوه بعد خمسين سنة أو أكثر
The second proof that it is not permissible to adhere to isolated reports (akhbar al-ahad) in certainties (yaqiniyyat): we say: The most honourable class of narrators is the class of the Companions, yet their narrations do not yield certainty. As for the right of the Companions, that is indicated by aspects: The first: The most apparent and clearest of matters are the matter of the call to prayer (adhan) and the second call (iqama). Because he—peace be upon him—persisted in them for ten years, or more or less. And everyone used to observe him five times every day. And the Rightly Guided Caliphs persisted in them for thirty years with a great multitude. Yet the narrators did not record the conditions of the adhan nor the conditions of the iqama. As for the conditions of the adhan: whether raising the voice (tarjih) is considered in the adhan or not. And as for the iqama: is it single (furada) or double (mathna)? And no one may say: Perhaps it was once single and once double. Because we say: If that were the case, then the narrator would have to report that it was once single and once double. When they failed to record this amount, even though everyone witnessed this long period, how can trust remain in their narrations in subtle matters and in obscure investigations, especially since—in their claim—they heard them only once? And from that is what was narrated that he—peace be upon him—performed hajj individually (ifrad), or in conjunction (qiran), or in tamattu', even though the hajj was one hajj, and we know that he did not do all. And from that: whether he—peace be upon him—used to raise his hands at bowing (ruku') and standing up (intisab)? Ibn Mas'ud narrated the non-raising, and Ibn 'Umar narrated the raising. And once it is mentioned that in the first takbirah he used to raise his hands to the level of the shoulders or to the level of the ears. All these matters are observable, sensible matters; sufficient for knowing them is soundness of the senses and basic intellect. When they failed to record this amount, how can they be able to record the fiqhi issues which they heard only once, and then they did not write that collection, but relied solely on their memory, and then transmitted it after fifty years or more?
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents the first aspect (wajh) of the second proof that isolated reports (akhbar al-ahad) cannot yield certainty in matters of belief. The argument begins by acknowledging the high status of the Companions as narrators, but then demonstrates that even their narrations fail to provide certainty. The author uses the example of the most public and frequently repeated acts of worship: the adhan (call to prayer) and iqama (second call). The Prophet performed these daily for many years, and the caliphs continued them for decades in front of large congregations. Despite this, narrators could not agree on the details: whether the adhan includes tarjih (raising the voice), and whether the iqama is recited singly or in pairs. The author refutes the possibility that both forms occurred interchangeably, arguing that if that were the case, narrators would have transmitted the variation. Their failure to do so, despite the abundance of witnesses, undermines confidence in their transmission of more subtle matters heard only once. The passage then adds examples: the type of hajj performed by the Prophet (ifrad, qiran, or tamattu'), and whether he raised his hands at ruku' and intisab. Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn 'Umar disagreed on the latter. The author concludes with a rhetorical question: if they could not agree on such clear and commonly observed acts, how can they be trusted with fiqhi issues they heard only once, never wrote down, and transmitted decades later from memory? This argument is used to challenge the reliability of ahad reports in establishing certain knowledge.

Key Terms
الإفراد
القران
التمتع
رفع اليدين
الركوع
الانتصاب

Key Takeaway
The disagreements on hajj and hand-raising serve as further evidence that isolated reports cannot achieve certainty, even for obvious acts.

What does this demand from you today in preserving and transmitting true knowledge?
?
If the Prophet's own hajj—a single, public event—can be reported in three contradictory ways, how do you approach the hadith you follow? Does this shake your trust or deepen it? What is one practice you follow based on a single report, and can you honestly say you are certain it is exactly as the Prophet did it?

Introspection as Evidence for Human Agency
Opening of the first proof using introspection and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof uses introspection to establish that humans have necessary knowledge of their own agency.

Core Idea
We know ourselves as agents through immediate self-awareness.

This first proof from the rationalist school begins with the most basic kind of knowledge: what every person finds within themselves. By reflecting on how our actions depend on our motives, we discover a truth that is both necessary and universally recognized. The author invites us to examine this self-evident starting point.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Begin by reading the passage that states Abu al-Husayn's claim that necessary knowledge affirms the servant as creator of his own acts. Notice that this is a claim about immediate, non-inferential knowledge.
The author is presenting a proof from a specific theological school.
2
Examine the introspective basis
The proof starts from what every rational person knows from themselves: that their actions depend on their motives. Reflect on how this feels—when you act, you are aware of your motivation.
3
Consider the examples
Read the examples of hunger leading to eating and the belief of poison leading to refraining. These illustrate the necessary connection between motive and action.
The examples are meant to be self-evident to any rational person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكاية قيل من يقول. قد نقلنا في أول المسألة: أن أبا الحسين يدعي العلم الضروري بأن العبد موجد الأفعال نفسه. واحتجوا على أن العلم الضروري حاصل بذلك بوجوه ثلاثة: الأول: إن كل عاقل يعلم من نفسه: أن وقوع تصرفاته موقوف على دواعيه، ولولا دواعيه لما وقع شيء منها. فمتى اشتد به الجوع، وكان تناول الطعام ممكناً، فإنه يصدر منه تناول الطعام. ومتى اعتقد أن في الطعام سما، ينصرف عنه. وكذلك يعلم من غيره من العقلاء الذين أحوالهم سليمة: أنه يجب وقوع أفعالهم بحسب دواعيهم. فإن الولد العاقل السليم إذا علم ما له من المنفعة في شرب الماء، حال شدة عطشه، ولم يمنعه منه مانع فإنه يصدر منه الشرب، لا محالة. ومتى علم ما له من المضرة في دخول النار، فإنه لا يدخل فيها. وإذا كان معنى الموجد للشيء: هو الذي يحدث منه الفعل، موافقاً لدواعيه، ثبت أن العقلاء قد يعرفون ببدائه عقولهم كون العبد كذلك. علمنا: أن علمهم بكون العبيد محدثين لتصرفاتهم: علم ضروري.
Narration of what is said. We have already transmitted at the beginning of the question: that Abu al-Husayn claims necessary knowledge that the servant is the creator of his own actions. And they argued that necessary knowledge is attained by that through three proofs: The first: that every rational person knows from himself that the occurrence of his actions is dependent upon his motives, and were it not for his motives, nothing of them would occur. So when hunger intensifies for him, and eating food is possible, then eating food issues from him. And when he believes that there is poison in the food, he turns away from it. And likewise he knows from other rational people whose conditions are sound: that it is necessary that their actions occur according to their motives. For the sound rational child, when he knows the benefit for him in drinking water, in the state of severe thirst, and no preventer prevents him, then drinking issues from him, inevitably. And when he knows the harm for him in entering fire, then he does not enter it. And if the meaning of the 'creator' of a thing is: the one from whom the act occurs in accordance with his motives, then it is established that rational people may know by the primary dictates of their intellects that the servant is such. We know: that their knowledge that servants are agents of their actions is necessary knowledge.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three proofs that necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) affirms that human beings are the creators (mūjidūn) of their own actions. The author begins by recalling that Abu al-Husayn claims this necessary knowledge, and then outlines the proof. The argument proceeds in several steps: First, every rational person knows from introspection that his actions depend on his motives (dawāʿī); without motives, no action would occur. Examples illustrate this: when hungry and food is available, one eats; when one believes food is poisoned, one refrains. Second, one also knows from observing other rational people in sound condition that their actions necessarily follow their motives—e.g., a rational child who knows water benefits him when thirsty will inevitably drink, and if he knows fire harms him, he will not enter it. Third, the definition of 'creator' (mūjid) is that from whom the act proceeds in accordance with his motives. Therefore, rational people know by the primary dictates of their intellects (badāʾih ʿuqūlihim) that humans are such creators. The conclusion is that this knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī). The proof relies on immediate self-awareness and universal observation, not on theoretical reasoning, to establish the certainty of human agency.

Key Terms
العلم الضروري
الموجد
الدواعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, how does each rational person know that their actions depend on motives?
A) By studying theology.
B) By introspection (from themselves).
C) By hearing from prophets.
D) By consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The first proof begins with immediate self-awareness: we know directly that our actions follow our motives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you most often deny your own agency—blaming circumstances, upbringing, or other people? How does the claim of this lesson that 'every rational person knows from himself' confront that excuse? Will you take ownership of that area today?
?
The lesson says that even a thirsty child knows to drink unless prevented. When you delay obeying Allah, are you acting as if you lack awareness of your motives? What prevents you from moving from 'I know' to 'I act'?
?
Think of a moment when you chose to sin despite knowing it was wrong. At that moment, were you truly aware that you were the agent? How does the immediate self-knowledge described here make you accountable for that choice? What will you do the next time a similar situation arises?

Observation, Definition, and Conclusion of the Proof
Completion of the first proof: fire example, definition, and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how observing others and the definition of 'creator' complete the first proof for necessary knowledge of human agency.

Core Idea
Rational people know by intellect that humans are creators of their acts.

The proof continues from introspection to a universal observation: all rational people, when sound, act according to their motives. This leads to a definition of what it means to be a 'creator' (mūjid) of an action. The conclusion affirms that our knowledge of human agency is necessary, not acquired through reasoning. Let's see how the author wraps up this first argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the continuation
Read the passage from the fire example through the definition and conclusion. Notice how the author ties the earlier examples to the definition of 'creator'.
This part completes the argument.
2
Understand the definition of mūjid
The author defines 'creator' (mūjid) as the one from whom the act proceeds in accordance with his motives. This is key to connecting the examples to necessary knowledge.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Rational people know by the primary dictates of their intellects (badāʾih al-ʿuqūl) that humans are such creators. This knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī).
The phrase 'primary dictates of the intellect' indicates self-evident truths.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that this proof denies divine power or that human agency excludes God's role.

The First Proof: Disagreement Proves Knowledge is Inferential
Author refutes Abu al-Husayn's necessary knowledge claim using historical disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how historical disagreement among scholars serves as a rational proof that a theological claim is not self-evident but inferential.

Core Idea
Disagreement among rational people proves the matter is not self-evident.

In this lesson, we examine the first proof against Abu al-Husayn's claim that knowledge of human agency is necessary. The author points to historical disagreement before Abu al-Husayn: two groups held opposing views, and even those who agreed with him (the Mu'tazila) used rational proofs. This shows that the knowledge is not self-evident, because if it were, all reasonable people would agree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the two groups mentioned before Abu al-Husayn.
The passage is a single logical argument, not a list.
2
Identify the key claim
The author declares Abu al-Husayn's claim of necessity false. Underline that statement.
3
Understand the argument
The author argues that if knowledge of human agency were necessary, earlier scholars would be denying self-evident truths. Why is that absurd?
Consider what 'necessary knowledge' means here.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that Abu al-Husayn's claim is false. Ask yourself: Is this argument convincing? Why or why not?
This prepares you for the next lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن دعوى الضرورة في كون العبد موجداً لأفعاله باطل ويدل عليه وجوه . الأول : إن الناس كانوا قبل أبي الحسين فريقان : منهم من يزعم : أن فعل العبد مخلوق الله تعالى، ومنهم من يزعم : أنه يحصل بإيجاد العبد وتكوينه . والعلم بذلك علم استدلالي. ولذلك فإن جميع مشايخ المعتزلة إنما كانوا يثبتون هذا المطلوب بناء على الدلائل والبينات . ولو كان العلم بهذا المطلوب ضرورياً ، لكان جميع الناس الذين كانوا قبل أبي الحسين منكرين للبديهيات . أما مخالفوا المعتزلة فلا شك أنه يلزم على قول أبي الحسين كونهم منكرين للبديهيات. وأما جميع مشايخ المعتزلة فكذلك . لأنهم لما كانوا مطبقين على أنه لا سبيل إلى إثبات هذا المطلوب إلا بالدلائل ، كانوا متفقين على أن العلم بهذا المطلوب ليس بضروري . فثبت : أن العلم بهذا المطلوب لو كان ضرورياً ، لزم أن يقال : إن جميع الخلق الذين كانوا قبل أبي الحسين كانوا مطبقين متفقين على إمكان البديهيات والضروريات . ولما كان ذلك باطلاً . علمنا فساد قول أبي الحسين .
Know that the claim of necessity that the servant is the creator of his actions is false, and it is indicated by [several] points. First: Before Abu al-Husayn, people were two groups: those who claim that the action of the servant is created by God, and those who claim that it occurs by the servant's bringing into existence and formation. Knowledge of this is inferential. For this reason, all the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila used to establish this claim based on proofs and evidences. And if knowledge of this claim were necessary, then all people who were before Abu al-Husayn would be deniers of self-evident truths. As for the opponents of the Mu'tazila, there is no doubt that according to Abu al-Husayn's statement, they would be deniers of self-evident truths. And as for all the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila, likewise. Because they were agreed that there is no way to establish this claim except through proofs, they were in agreement that knowledge of this claim is not necessary. So it is established that if knowledge of this claim were necessary, it would be necessary to say that all creatures who were before Abu al-Husayn were unanimously agreeing on the possibility of denying self-evident and necessary [truths]. And since that is false, we know the corruption of Abu al-Husayn's statement.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's claim that knowledge of the human being as the creator of his actions is necessary. The author begins by declaring this claim false and then presents the first of three proofs. The proof is based on the historical fact that before Abu al-Husayn, there were two groups: those who said actions are created by God (the Ash'aris and others) and those who said they are brought into existence by the servant (the Mu'tazila). The author notes that even the Mu'tazilite shaykhs used rational proofs to establish their position, meaning they considered such knowledge to be inferential, not necessary. If it were necessary, then all people before Abu al-Husayn (both Mu'tazila and their opponents) would be denying self-evident truths, which is absurd. Therefore, Abu al-Husayn's claim is false.

Key Terms
ضرورة
بديهي
استدلالي
مخلوق
إيجاد
عبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if many scholars believe something, it must be true.
Correction: The author shows that disagreement itself proves the matter is not self-evident; the fact that scholars disagreed and used proofs indicates the knowledge is inferential, not necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the first proof against Abu al-Husayn's claim?
A) That all people before Abu al-Husayn were Mu'tazilites
B) That if knowledge of human agency were necessary, earlier scholars would be denying self-evident truths
C) That introspection shows we are not the creators of our actions
D) That Abu al-Husayn contradicted himself

Key Takeaway
Historical disagreement among rational scholars proves that the claim about human agency is not self-evident but requires inferential reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief so firmly that you assumed it was obvious to everyone, only to encounter disagreement? How does this lesson's argument that disagreement disproves self-evidence challenge the certainty you have in matters of faith? Will you examine your beliefs more critically?
?
The shaykhs of the Mu'tazila defended their position with rational proofs rather than claiming necessity. Are you willing to engage with evidence for your beliefs, or do you rely on assumptions? What one step can you take this week to strengthen the inferential foundation of your faith?
?
If knowledge of human agency were self-evident, there would be no disagreement among scholars—yet we see deep division. What does this teach you about intellectual humility? Will you hold your current conviction as a conclusion reached through reasoning, or as an unchallenged axiom? Commit to re-examining one core belief in light of this lesson's method.

Concurrence of Action with Power and Will: Known but Not Causal
The author distinguishes known simultaneity from unknown causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the simultaneity of an action with power and will is empirically known, but that the action being caused by that power is unknown and cannot be asserted.

Core Idea
Simultaneity is known; causation by that power is unknown.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah addresses a critical point in the debate over human agency and divine determinism. It affirms that we can observe the concurrence of an action with the presence of power and will, but insists that whether the action is actually caused by that power is beyond our knowledge. This distinction protects against both fatalism and rationalist overreach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong language used against those who claim causation: 'مكابراً معانداً'.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage presents two distinct claims: one about what is known (simultaneity) and one about what is unknown (causation). Find and highlight these.
3
Consider why the author labels the opposite view as obstinate
Ask yourself what is at stake. The author is defending a theological position that limits human causal power. Reflect on why someone might insist on causation and why that would be problematic.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Think about how this distinction relates to free will, divine omnipotence, and human responsibility. Where does this leave human agency?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يخلق ذلك المراد على وفق تلك الإرادة. والحاصل : أن مقارنة الفعل مع حصول القدرة والإرادة معلومة . فأما كون الفعل به ، فذاك البتة غير معلوم . ومن قال خلاف ذلك ، كان مكابراً معانداً
He creates that intended object in accordance with that will. The conclusion: that the concomitance of the act with the occurrence of power and will is known. As for the act being by it, that is absolutely unknown. Whoever says otherwise is being obstinate and stubborn.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents a definitive statement about the relationship between divine creation, will, and human action. It opens by stating that the intended object is created in accordance with the will. Then it draws a conclusion: the fact that the act occurs simultaneously with the presence of power and will is known (معلوم). However, the crucial point is that whether the act is actually caused by that power (being 'by it') is completely unknown (غير معلوم بتة). The author declares that anyone who claims otherwise is engaging in obstinate denial (مكابر معاند). This is a key distinction in the debate over human agency versus divine determinism.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The known simultaneity of action and power implies that the power causes the action.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the act being by that power is unknown, so we cannot infer causation from concurrence.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is known about the act in relation to power and will?
A) The act is caused by the power.
B) The act occurs simultaneously with power and will.
C) The act is not connected to power.
D) The act is created by the power.

Key Takeaway
The simultaneity of an action with power and will is known, but whether the action is caused by that power is unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live your life feeling that your choices and efforts cause your actions. But this lesson says the connection between your power and your act is unknown, only that they occur together. How does that change the way you view your own achievements and failures? ?
If the causation of your act by your power is unknown, then attributing success to yourself is presumptuous. What specific success are you proud of that you now need to re-evaluate? Will you still take credit for it, or will you say 'Allah created it in accordance with His will'? Write down one concrete way you will adjust your self-praise today.
?
The author says denying this distinction is obstinate. Is there an area of your life where you stubbornly insist on your own causal power—perhaps in your work, relationships, or spiritual growth? What would it look like to surrender that insistence and acknowledge that only Allah creates? Commit to one action this week that reflects this surrender.
