The First Argument: Actions Follow Intentions
The author presents a two-premise proof for human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for human agency, which argues that our actions must correspond to our intentions and aversions, and why this proves they occur by us.

Core Idea
Our actions are our own because they always follow what we truly intend.

The author begins the first proof for human agency with a simple yet powerful observation: our actions invariably align with our genuine intentions and aversions. This self-evident link between will and action suggests that we are the true originators of our deeds. In this lesson, we will unpack the two premises of this argument and see how they logically lead to the conclusion that actions occur by us.

Guided Path
1
Read the full argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the structure: a claim, then two premises, each explained.
2
Analyze the first premise
Focus on the examples: the fasting person and the person who knows fire is painful. How do these examples illustrate that actions necessarily follow intentions and aversions?
The author uses extreme cases to show the necessity.
3
Understand the second premise
Read the second premise and its evidence. Why would the correspondence break if another agent caused our actions? Consider the counterfactual.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if another caused our actions, they could go against our will.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Combine both premises: actions must follow our will, and only we can ensure that correspondence. So actions must occur by us.
This conclusion directly supports human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : أنهم قالوا : إن أفعالنا يجب وقوعها على وفق دواعينا ، ويجب انتفاؤها على حسب كراهاتنا لها . وذلك يدل على وقوعها بنا . أما المقدمة الأولى : فبيانها : أن الصائم في الصيف الصائف إذا اشتدت شهوته إلى شرب الماء ، والشارع يشير إليه بذلك ، والطبيب يشير إليه به . وعلم قطعاً أنه لا تبعة عليه في شرب ذلك الماء ، في الحال ولا في الاستقبال . فإنه لا بد وأن يشربه. وكذلك العالم بما في دخول النار من الألم إذا علم أنه لا نفع له في دخولها البتة ، وعلم أنه لو دخلها حصلت آلام عظيمة في جسده . فإنه مع هذا العلم لا يدخلها البتة . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن أفعالنا يجب وقوعها بحسب دواعينا ، ويجب انتفاؤها بحسب صوارفنا . وذلك هو تمام المقدمة الأولى . وأما المقدمة الثانية : فهي قولنا : إن الأمر لما كان كذلك ، وجب أن يكون حدوث أفعالنا بنا . والدليل عليه : أنه لو كان المحدث لها غيرنا ، لصح من ذلك الغير أن لا يحدثها عند حصول إرادتنا ، وأن يحدثها عند حصول كراهتنا . وذلك يبطل ما بيناه في المقدمة الأولى من وجوب مطابقة أفعالنا لدواعينا نفياً وإثباتاً .
The first proof: They said: Our actions must occur in accordance with our intentions, and must be absent in accordance with our dislikes for them. That indicates that they occur by us. As for the first premise: its explanation: that the fasting person in the hot summer, when his desire for drinking water intensifies, and the legislator indicates it to him, and the physician indicates it to him, and he knows with certainty that there is no blame upon him in drinking that water, neither immediately nor in the future, then he must drink it. Likewise, the one who knows the pain in entering the fire, if he knows that there is no benefit for him in entering it at all, and knows that if he entered it, great pains would occur in his body, then with this knowledge he will not enter it at all. So it is established by what we mentioned: that our actions must occur according to our intentions, and must be absent according to our aversions. That is the completion of the first premise. As for the second premise: it is our statement: Since the matter is so, then it is necessary that the occurrence of our actions be from us. The evidence for it: that if the one who brings them into existence were other than us, then it would be possible for that other not to bring them about when our will occurs, and to bring them about when our aversion occurs. That would invalidate what we established in the first premise of the necessary correspondence of our actions to our intentions, both negatively and positively.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-ḥujjah al-ūlā) for the claim that the servant is the creator (mūjid) of his own actions. It consists of two premises and a conclusion. The author first states the overall argument: actions must occur in accordance with our intentions (dawāʿīnā) and must be absent in accordance with our aversions (karāhāt). This indicates that the actions occur by us (bi-nā). Then he explains the first premise in detail: the example of a fasting person who, when intensely thirsty and knowing there is no blame, inevitably drinks; and the example of someone who knows the pain of fire and thus avoids it. These show that actions necessarily follow intentions and aversions. The second premise argues that if the action were brought about by another (ghayrunā), that other could act contrary to our will, violating the first premise. Therefore, the occurrence of actions must be from us (ḥudūthu afʿālinā bi-nā). The argument is self-contained: it relies on the observable, self-evident link between will and action to prove human agency.

Key Terms
دواعي
صوارف
مقدمة
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first premise implies that actions are determined or compelled, leaving no room for free choice.
Correction: The author only means a natural, necessary correspondence under normal conditions, not an external compulsion. The correspondence reflects our own genuine will and aversion.

Checkpoint
What does the first premise of the first argument seek to establish?
A) That actions are caused by God.
B) That actions must occur according to intentions and aversions.
C) That actions are random.
D) That actions are caused by external factors.

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that our actions are our own because they invariably follow our genuine intentions and aversions, and only we can guarantee that correspondence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time you did something you later regretted. According to this argument, your action necessarily followed your intention at that moment. What does that reveal about your true intention, regardless of what you claimed afterward? How can you guard against self-deception in your intentions?
?
If your actions are a perfect mirror of your deepest intentions, what do your daily habits—especially the ones you wish you didn't have—reveal about what you truly love and fear? Are you ready to face that truth before Allah?
?
The argument proves you are the cause of your actions. Knowing this, which action in your life right now can you no longer blame on circumstances, habits, or others? What will you do today to take full ownership of it for the sake of Allah?

The Fourth Proof: Reductio on Divine Obligation
Author's reductio argument against divine obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a rational argument to show that God cannot be obligated to deliver reward and compensation, and why the assumption of obligation leads to a contradiction with God's praiseworthiness.

Core Idea
If God were obligated, His act would be necessary, removing praise and blame—an impossible outcome.

This lesson examines the fourth proof against the Ash'ari position that God is obligated to reward the obedient. The author constructs a logical reductio: if obligation were true, then God's failure would imply ignorance or need; thus God must act, making the act necessary. But necessity cancels praise and blame, contradicting God's praiseworthiness. By tracing this argument, we see why the premise of obligation is rationally untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation side by side. Notice the logical connectors: 'if... then...', 'both are impossible', 'that which leads to the impossible is impossible'.
The argument is compact; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Find the conditional statement: 'if it is necessary for God to deliver reward and compensation, then failure to do so implies ignorance or need.' Understand why these are impossible for God.
Ignorance and need are attributes of contingent beings, not the Necessary Being.
3
Trace the reductio chain
Follow how the impossibility of ignorance and need leads to the conclusion that refraining from the act is impossible, hence the act is necessary. Then see how this necessity would prevent praise and blame.
The argument shows that obligation leads to a contradiction with God's praiseworthiness.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the original assumption (obligation) must be false. Reflect on why this argument is considered a reductio ad absurdum.
The conclusion is negative: God is not obligated in the way the Ash'aris claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنه تعالى إذا وجب عليه إيصال الثواب والعوض إلى العبد ، فإذا لم يفعل ذلك ، لزم إما الجهل أو الحاجة . وهما محالان . والمفضي إلى المحال : محال . وهذا يقتضي أنه يستحيل من الله تعالى عقلاً أن لا يفعل ذلك الواجب . وإذا امتنع الترك ، وجب الفعل . فإن كان هذا الوجوب يمنع من المدح والذم ، وجب أن لا يستحق الله تعالى بذلك مدحاً ، ولا بتركه ذماً . وذلك باطل
The fourth: He, exalted, if it is necessary for Him to deliver reward and compensation to the servant, and if He does not do that, then ignorance or need necessarily follows. Both are impossible. And that which leads to the impossible is impossible. And this necessitates that it is intellectually impossible for God, exalted, not to do that obligation. And if refraining is impossible, then the act is necessary. If this necessity prevents praise and blame, then it necessarily follows that God, exalted, does not deserve praise for it, nor blame for refraining from it. And that is false.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument. It begins by positing a conditional: if God is obligated to deliver reward and compensation to the servant, then His failure to do so would imply either ignorance or need—both attributes impossible for God. Therefore, it is impossible for God not to perform that obligatory act. This makes the act necessary. The argument then considers the consequence: if the act is necessary, then God cannot be praised for doing it nor blamed for omitting it, since necessity removes the possibility of praise or blame. But that conclusion is false, because God does deserve praise. Hence, the original assumption that God is obligated must be false. The structure follows: premise, inference, reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
وجوب
ثواب
عوض
جهل
حاجة
محال
مدح
ذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the argument denies God's praiseworthiness rather than rejecting the premise of obligation.
Correction: The argument does not deny that God is praiseworthy; it uses His praiseworthiness as a premise to show that the assumption of obligation is false.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument conclude about the assumption that God is obligated to deliver reward?
A) God must deliver reward, so He is worthy of praise.
B) The assumption is false because it leads to the impossibility of praise and blame.
C) God is free to act without any obligation.
D) The obligation is only rational, not moral.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof demonstrates that assuming divine obligation leads to a contradiction, thereby proving that God is not subject to obligations in the sense required by the Ash'ari position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat your acts of worship as transactions, expecting God to repay you? How does the argument that God is not obligated challenge that mindset? Repent of any sense of entitlement and resolve to worship purely out of love and gratitude.
?
If God's actions are not necessary but entirely free, then every blessing you have is a gift—not a duty. Take a moment to list three blessings you usually take for granted and thank God for them specifically, acknowledging His freedom.
?
The proof shows that necessity would strip actions of moral worth. Have you ever felt that your good deeds are 'required' and therefore less valuable? Remember that God accepts them by His grace. How can you cultivate a heart that sees all virtue as a gift from Him?

The Fifth Proof: The Necessity of Motives and the Refutation of Chance
The fifth reductio argument on motives and chance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof which establishes the necessity of motives for action and refutes the notion of chance, showing that the opponents' premises actually lead to compulsion.

Core Idea
Motives necessitate action; chance is impossible, and compulsion is unavoidable.

In this passage, the author presents the fifth argument, a multi-layered reductio against opponents who reject the Asharite position. The argument begins with the intuitive dependence of action on motives, then explores the absurdities that follow when this dependence or its necessity is denied. Each scenario forces the opponents into the very conclusion they sought to avoid.

Guided Path
1
The First Premise: Motives and Necessity
Read the opening lines where the author states that intuition shows action depends on motives, and when the motive is complete, the action is necessary. This leads to the conclusion that compulsion follows.
Note that this is the starting point that the opponents might accept, but it becomes a problem for them.
2
The First Absurdity: Denying the Dependence
Consider the first hypothetical: if one claims the agent does not need a motive, then action occurs by chance without specification. The author argues that chance is not within human power, so such an obligation is impossible.
The author uses this to show that denying dependence leads to an impossibility.
3
The Second Absurdity: Denying the Necessity
Consider the second hypothetical: if one accepts dependence but denies that the motive necessitates the action, then the specification of one time over another is arbitrary, again implying compulsion.
Thus, all routes lead to compulsion, turning the opponents' criticism back on them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إنا بينا : أن البديهة دلت على أن الفعل يتوقف على الدواعي وبينا : أن عند حصول الداعية يجب الفعل . وعلى هذا التقدير فالجبر لازم ، ويلزمهم ما الزموه علينا . وأيضاً : فبتقدير أن نقول : فاعل الفعل لا يتوقف على الداعي ، كان معناه : أن القادر يصدر عنه أحد الفعلين من غير أن يميل طبع ذلك الفاعل إلى ذلك الواقع من غير أن يخص ذلك الفاعل ، ذلك الواقع بأمر البتة . وعلى هذا التقدير يكون حصول ذلك الفعل حصولا على الاتفاق من غير تخصيص ، ومن غير ترجيح . والاتفاقيات لا تكون في الوسع والقدرة . فحينئذ يكون التكليف به ، تكليفاً بما لا يطاق . وأيضاً : بتقدير أن يقول قائل : الفعل يتوقف على الداعي، لكن عند حصول الداعي لا يجب. فإنه إذا لم يجب جاز تارة ، وأن لا يجوز أخرى . وحينئذ يكون به اختصاص أحد الوقتين بالوقوع دون الوقت الثاني ، يكون اختصاصه اتفاقياً ، لا لمرجح أصلا . وذلك يوجب الجبر ـ على ما قررناه ـ وحينئذ يلزمهم كل ما ألزموه علينا
The fifth: We have shown that intuition indicates that action depends on motives, and we have shown that when the motive occurs, the action is necessary. On this basis, compulsion is necessary, and what they imposed on us is required of them. Also, if we suppose saying: the agent of the action does not depend on the motive, its meaning would be that the capable agent brings about one of two actions without the nature of that agent inclining to that actualized one, without that agent specifying that actualized one by any matter at all. On this basis, the occurrence of that action is by chance without specification and without preponderance. And chance occurrences are not within capacity and power. Then the obligation concerning it would be an obligation of what is impossible. Also, if someone says: the action depends on the motive, but when the motive occurs, it is not necessary, then if it is not necessary, it is possible sometimes and not possible other times. Then the specification of one of the two times for occurrence without the second time would be a specification by chance, without any preponderant at all. That necessitates compulsion according to what we have established, and then all that they imposed on us is required of them.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, which is a multi-part refutation. First, the author states that intuition (بديهة) shows that action depends on motives (دواعي), and when the motive is complete, the action is necessary. This implies compulsion (جبر), which the opponents accuse the author of but now applies to them. Second, if one denies dependence on motives, then action would be purely by chance (اتفاق), which is not within human capacity (وسع and قدرة), making the obligation impossible. Third, even if one accepts dependence but denies necessity when the motive is present, then the specification of one time for action over another would be arbitrary (اتفاقي), again implying compulsion. Thus, the opponents' own arguments turn back on them. The argument uses a reductio approach: assuming the opponent's premises leads to absurdities.

Key Terms
بديهة
داعي
داعية
جبر
اتفاق
وسع
قدرة

Correction: The argument actually refutes the opponents by showing their premises lead to compulsion, which they reject.

B) That action does not depend on motives, so it is chosen freely.
C) That the author accepts compulsion as correct.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof demonstrates that any denial of the necessary relationship between motives and action leads to either chance or compulsion, undermining the opponents' position.

The Seventh Proof: Impossibility of Obligation with Equal Motives
The author presents the seventh argument on the impossibility of obligation under equal or preponderant motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh argument against the possibility of obligation when motives are equal or preponderant, and see why any command under such conditions leads to a logical impossibility.

Core Idea
Obligation is impossible when motives are equal; it would require the impossible.

The seventh argument examines the conditions under which a command (taklif) can be directed at a person. It shows that whether motives are equal or one is preponderant, any command leads to a logical contradiction. This argument was used in theological debates to challenge the coherence of divine obligation in certain scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Follow the logic of the two main cases and the objection.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two scenarios and the objection
Note how the argument splits into equal motives and preponderant motives. Then see the objection about a later time and its rebuttal.
Each scenario is shown to be impossible.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider why each option leads to a contradiction. How does the rebuttal refute the objection?
This is a classic example of reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : إن التكليف . إما أن يتوجه على العبد حال استواء الداعي إلى الفعل والترك ، أو حال الرجحان . فإن كان الأول لزم أن يصير مأموراً بالترجيح حال الاستواء ، وذلك تكليف بالجمع بين النقيضين . وإن كان الثاني فهو محال . لأنه إن أمر بترجيح الطرف الراجح كان ذلك أمراً بتكوين الكائن . وهو محال . وإن أمر بترجيح الطرف المرجوح كان ذلك أمراً بأن يحصل المرجوح حال كونه مرجوحاً راجحا . فيكون أمراً بالجمع بين النقيضين . وهو محال . ولا يقال : إنه حال الاستواء يكون مأمورا بأن يرجح بعد ذلك . لأنا نقول : هذا باطل . لأنه إما أن يكون المراد منه : أنه حال الاستواء مأموراً بأن يرجح في الزمان الثاني ، قبل مجيء الزمان الثاني ، أو عند مجيئه . والأول محال . لأن إحداث الترجيح في الزمان الثاني قبل حضور الزمان الثاني محال . وإن كان الحق هو الثاني ، فنقول : إن عند مجيء الزمان الثاني يعود التقسيم المذكور فيه . لأن عند مجيء الزمان الثاني ، إما أن يكون الطرفان على السوية ، أو لا يكون على السوية ، وحينئذ تعود المحالات المذكورة
The seventh: Obligation is either directed at the servant when the motive to act and the motive to refrain are equal, or when there is preponderance. If the first, then he necessarily becomes commanded to bring about preponderance while [the motives] are equal, and that is an obligation to combine two opposites. If the second, it is impossible. Because if he is commanded to bring about the preponderant side, that is a command to actualize what already exists, which is impossible. If he is commanded to bring about the outweighed side, that is a command that the outweighed side occurs while it is outweighed and preponderant, so it is a command to combine two opposites, which is impossible. And it cannot be said that when [the motives] are equal, he is commanded to bring about preponderance later, because we say: that is false. Because either what is meant is that while equal, he is commanded to bring about preponderance in the second time before the arrival of the second time, or at its arrival. The first is impossible because causing preponderance in the second time before the presence of the second time is impossible. If the correct is the second, we say: at the arrival of the second time, the aforementioned division returns. Because at the arrival of the second time, either the two sides are equal or not equal, and then the aforementioned impossibilities return.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument, which analyzes the condition under which obligation is directed. It splits into two scenarios: either the motives for action and refraining are equal (استواء) or one is preponderant (رجحان). If equal, commanding the servant to preponderate one side is commanding the impossible (جمع بين النقيضين) because he has no reason to choose. If one side is preponderant, commanding the preponderant side is commanding what already exists (تكوين الكائن) —impossible—and commanding the outweighed side requires it to be both outweighed and preponderant, again a contradiction. The argument then anticipates an objection: perhaps the command applies when equal but the servant is to preponderate later. The author refutes this by considering the time: if he is commanded to preponderate in the second time before it arrives, that is impossible; if at its arrival, the same dilemma recurs. Thus, no coherent obligation is possible.

Key Terms
تكليف
استواء
رجحان
ترجيح
جمع بين النقيضين
تكوين الكائن
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The seventh argument denies human free will entirely.
Correction: The argument does not deny human choice; it denies the coherence of divine obligation under conditions of equal or preponderant motives by showing logical contradictions.

Checkpoint
Why does the seventh argument consider commanding the preponderant side impossible?
A) Because it would be a command to do nothing.
B) Because it would be a command to actualize what already exists.
C) Because the servant would have no motive.
D) Because the command would be from God only.

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof demonstrates that obligation under equal or preponderant motives leads to logical impossibilities, thereby challenging the concept of divine command in such contexts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used the excuse that your desires are equally balanced to postpone obeying a clear command from Allah? How does this argument expose the logical flaw in that delay?
?
When you feel a strong inclination to sin, do you assume that because the motive is preponderant, you are not accountable? This argument shows that commanding the outweighed side is impossible—so what does that mean for your responsibility when the right choice is clear?
?
This proof dismantles the idea that obligation can exist when motives are equal. Are there areas of your life where you are waiting for perfect balance before acting? What would it look like to obey now, even with unequal motives?

The Eighth Proof: Power and the Obligation of the Impossible
The author presents the eighth argument on power and obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that power cannot coherently be assigned to a non-existent action, making the obligation of the impossible contradictory.

Core Idea
Power cannot exist over non-existence, so commanding the impossible is absurd.

This lesson presents the eighth argument from the text, which tackles a central issue in theology: how can a person be commanded to do something they have no power to do? The author divides the concept of power and shows that whether power is tied to the action's existence or its non-existence, the opponent's position collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the structure: division of power into two cases, then a counterargument and its refutation.
2
Understand the division of power
Identify the two possibilities: power at the time of the action's existence, or power at the time of its non-existence.
Each case is shown to lead to a problem.
3
Analyze the problems with each division
For the first case, if power exists only when the action exists, then before the act the person has no power, making the command an obligation of the impossible. For the second case, power over non-existence is impossible because non-existence is not an object of power.
4
Examine the refutation of the counterargument
The author considers a potential objection that power at the time of non-existence could necessitate existence later. He then shows that this leads to a false dichotomy: either power necessitates existence before time T or after T, both absurd.
This reinforces the initial conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : إن القدرة إما أن تكون قدرة على الفعل حال وجود الفعل أو حال عدمه. والأول يقتضي أن يقال : الكافر لا قدرة له على الإيمان . مع أنه مأمور بالإيمان . فيكون هذا تكليف بما لا يطاق . والثاني محال . لان قبل الوجود يكون الشيء باقياً على عدمه الأصلي ، والعدم الأصلي لا يكون مقدوراً ، فيمتنع أن تكون القدرة ، قدرة عليه ، ولا يقال : القدرة حال عدم الفعل تقتضي حصول وجود ذلك الفعل في الزمان الثاني . لأنا نقول : إما أن يكون المراد أن القدرة تقتضي حصول الوجود في الزمان الثاني قبل حضور الزمان الثاني ، أو بعد حضوره . والقسمان باطلان . على ما سبق تقريره
The eighth: Power is either power over the action at the time of the action's existence or at the time of its non-existence. The first necessitates that it be said: the disbeliever has no power over faith, while he is commanded to have faith. So this would be an obligation of what is impossible. The second is impossible, because before existence, the thing remains in its original non-existence, and original non-existence is not something over which one has power, so it is impossible for power to be over it. And it cannot be said: power at the time of the non-existence of the action necessitates the occurrence of the existence of that action in the second time. Because we say: either what is meant is that power necessitates the occurrence of existence in the second time before the arrival of the second time, or after its arrival. Both divisions are false, according to what has been previously established.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument, which examines the nature of power (قدرة) in relation to obligation. It divides power into two kinds: power at the time of the action's existence (حال وجود الفعل) or at the time of its non-existence (حال عدمه). If power exists only when the action exists, then before performing faith, the disbeliever has no power to believe, yet he is commanded to believe—this is an obligation of the impossible. If power exists before the action (at the time of non-existence), that is impossible because the non-existent is not something over which one can have power (العدم الأصلي لا يكون مقدوراً). The author then refutes a potential counterargument that power at the time of non-existence necessitates existence in the second time: either the power necessitates existence before the second time arrives (impossible) or after it arrives (but then the same dilemma applies). Both are false. Thus, no coherent account of power supports the obligation of the impossible.

عدم

عدم أصلي
زمان ثان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the argument denies human power altogether.
Correction: The argument actually shows that the opponent's concept of power leads to absurdity, not that humans have no power.

Checkpoint

A) The action would not be real.
B) The servant would have power only after the act, so before it he is incapable.
C) Power would be unlimited.
D) It would make God unjust.

Key Takeaway
The eighth proof demonstrates that any account of power that makes obligation of the impossible necessary is self-defeating.

This lesson shows that the concept of 'power' must be consistent. Think of a command of Allah that you find difficult. Do you believe that at the very moment you need to obey, Allah has given you the power to do so? If not, what does that imply about your view of His justice? How will you adjust your belief?
?
The author argues that power cannot be over non-existence. Reflect on a time you made excuses for not changing a bad habit by saying 'I can't'. The Seventh Argument: Taklif at Equality or Preponderance
The author presents the seventh argument about the impossibility of commanding preponderance when motives are equal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why commanding preponderance when motives are equal leads to a logical impossibility, and why a temporal delay does not resolve the problem.

Core Idea
Commanding preponderance when motives are equal requires combining opposites, which is impossible.

In this seventh argument, the author examines a critical dilemma regarding divine obligation (taklīf). If God commands an action when the motives for and against are perfectly balanced, how can preponderance occur? The argument systematically eliminates possibilities, showing that all lead to contradiction. Let's trace the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial dichotomy
The author presents two scenarios: taklif is directed either when motives are equal or when one side is preponderant. Recognize that this is an exhaustive division.
If motives are equal, there is no reason to prefer action over omission.
2
Analyze the first branch (equality)
If taklif occurs at equality, then the servant is commanded to create preponderance while still at equality. This is a command to combine two opposites (simultaneously equal and preponderant). Note why this is impossible.
Combining opposites is rationally impossible.
3
Analyze the second branch (preponderance)
If taklif occurs when one side is already preponderant, the author splits into two sub-cases: commanding the preponderant side (redundant) or the preponderated side (requires both weak and strong). Both are impossible.
Commanding what already exists is also impossible.
4
Consider the objection and its refutation
Someone might say that at equality, the command applies to a later time. The author dissects this: either the command is given before that time (impossible) or at that time (then the same division recurs).
The objection fails because it reintroduces the original problem.
5
Synthesize the argument
Conclude that no scenario makes taklif at equality or preponderance rationally possible. This supports the broader theological discussion on divine obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : إن التكليف إما أن يتوجه على العبد حال استواء الداعي إلى الفعل والترك، أو حال الرجحان. فإن كان الأول لزم أن يصير مأموراً بالترجيح حال الاستواء، وذلك تكليف بالجمع بين النقيضين. وإن كان الثاني فهو محال. لأنه إن أمر بترجيح الطرف الراجح كان ذلك أمراً بتكوين الكائن وهو محال. وإن أمر بترجيح الطرف المرجوح كان ذلك أمراً بأن يحصل المرجوح حال كونه مرجوحاً راجحاً. فيكون أمراً بالجمع بين النقيضين وهو محال. ولا يقال: إنه حال الاستواء يكون مأموراً بأن يرجح بعد ذلك. لأنا نقول: هذا باطل. لأنه إما أن يكون المراد منه: أنه حال الاستواء مأموراً بأن يرجح في الزمان الثاني، قبل مجيء الزمان الثاني، أو عند مجيئه. والأول محال. لأن إحداث الترجيح في الزمان الثاني قبل حضور الزمان الثاني محال. وإن كان الحق هو الثاني، فنقول: إن عند مجيء الزمان الثاني يعود التقسيم المذكور فيه. لأن عند مجيء الزمان الثاني، إما أن يكون الطرفان على السوية، أو لا يكون على السوية، وحينئذ تعود المحالات المذكورة.
The seventh: Indeed, obligation (al-taklīf) is either directed upon the servant when the motive for the act and the omission are equal, or when there is preponderance. If it is the first, it necessitates that he become commanded to preponderance (al-tarjīḥ) while at the time of equality, and that is an obligation to combine two opposites. And if it is the second, it is impossible. Because if he is commanded to preponderance of the preponderant aspect, that is a command to bring about what already is (takwīn al-kā'in), and that is impossible. And if he is commanded to preponderance of the preponderated aspect, that is a command that the preponderated obtains while being preponderated and preponderant, so it is a command to combine two opposites, and that is impossible. And it cannot be said: that at the time of equality he is commanded to preponderance after that. For we say: this is false. Because it is either that the meaning is: that at the time of equality he is commanded to preponderance in the second time, before the arrival of the second time, or upon its arrival. The first is impossible, because causing preponderance in the second time before the arrival of the second time is impossible. And if the truth is the second, we say: indeed upon the arrival of the second time, the aforementioned division returns. Because upon the arrival of the second time, either the two aspects are equal, or they are not equal, and then the aforementioned impossibilities return.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument in a series of ten arguments against the possibility of certain types of divine obligation (taklīf). The author sets up a dichotomy: obligation is either directed when the motivating factors for action and omission are perfectly equal, or when one side is preponderant. In the first case, commanding a preponderance at the point of equality would require an impossible combination of opposites. In the second case, if the preponderant side is already stronger, commanding it is redundant and impossible (commanding the bringing about of what already exists), and commanding the weaker side would require it to be both weak and strong at once. The author then considers and refutes a possible response: that the command to preponderance might apply only after the moment of equality, in a subsequent time. This response is dissected: either the command is given before that second time (impossible) or at the time itself (then the same problem recurs). The argument is tightly structured, using logical dichotomies to show that any attempt to conceive of such an obligation leads to contradiction.

Key Terms
تكليف
الترجيح
النقيضان
الزمان الثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about whether God can command at all.
Correction: The argument is specifically about the logical impossibility of commanding preponderance when motives are equal; it does not deny God's power to command in general.

Checkpoint
In the seventh argument, what is the problem with commanding preponderance when the motives for action and omission are equal?
A) It is redundant
B) It combines two opposites
C) It requires knowledge of the future
D) It imposes an infinite regress

Key Takeaway
Taklīf at the point of equal motives leads to an impossible command to combine opposites, and delaying the command to a later time replicates the same problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

The Eighth Argument: Power Relates to the Act at Existence or Non-Existence
The author presents the eighth argument: a dichotomy of power and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why relating power to the act at the moment of existence leads to obligation beyond capacity, and why relating it to non-existence is impossible.

Core Idea
Power cannot be over non-existence; if only at existence, the disbeliever is asked the impossible.

This passage presents the eighth argument concerning the relationship between power and the act. The author sets up a dichotomy: power is either at the moment of the act's existence or prior to it. Both options lead to problems, showing that the relation of power to the act cannot justify obligation. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dichotomy
Read the opening and notice the two options: power relates to the act either when it exists or when it does not yet exist. This sets up the entire argument.
2
Understand the first option
If power is only at the moment the act exists, then the disbeliever commanded to believe lacks power at that moment — so the command becomes an obligation beyond capacity (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq).
This option leads to an absurdity according to the author.
3
Understand the second option
If power is before the act exists, then it is power over something that does not yet exist. But original non-existence is not a valid object of power, making this impossible.
This relies on the premise that non-existence cannot be an object of power.
4
Follow the objection and its refutation
The passage anticipates an objection: maybe prior power brings about existence later. The author splits this into two sub-options (before or after the second time) and declares both false, referencing earlier proofs.
This shows the thoroughness of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : إن القدرة إما أن تكون قدرة على الفعل حال وجود الفعل أو حال عدمه. والأول يقتضي أن يقال : الكافر لا قدرة له على الإيمان. مع أنه مأمور بالإيمان. فيكون هذا تكليف بما لا يطاق. والثاني محال. لأن قبل الوجود يكون الشيء باقياً على عدمه الأصلي، والعدم الأصلي لا يكون مقدوراً، فيمتنع أن تكون القدرة قدرة عليه. ولا يقال : القدرة حال عدم الفعل تقتضي حصول وجود ذلك الفعل في الزمان الثاني. لأنا نقول : إما أن يكون المراد أن القدرة تقتضي حصول الوجود في الزمان الثاني قبل حضور الزمان الثاني، أو بعد حضوره. والقسمان باطلان. على ما سبق تقريره.
The eighth: Indeed, power (al-qudra) is either power over the act at the time of the act's existence or at the time of its non-existence. The first necessitates that it be said: the disbeliever has no power over faith, while he is commanded to have faith. So this is an obligation beyond capacity. The second is impossible, because before existence, the thing remains in its original non-existence, and original non-existence is not something over which there is power (maqdūr), so it is impossible that power be power over it. And it cannot be said: power at the time of non-existence necessitates the occurrence of the existence of that act in the second time. For we say: either it is meant that power necessitates the occurrence of existence in the second time before the arrival of the second time, or after its arrival. And both divisions are false, according to what has already been established.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument. It concerns the relationship between power (qudra) and the act. The author proposes a dichotomy: either power relates to the act at the very moment the act exists, or at a time when the act does not yet exist. If power coincides with the act's existence, then a disbeliever commanded to believe lacks that power at the moment of belief, making the command an obligation beyond capacity (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). If power is considered prior to the act's existence, then it would be power over something that does not yet exist — but non-existence (al-ʿadam) is not a proper object of power. The author then considers a potential objection: that power at the time of non-existence might bring about existence in a second moment. This is refuted by another dichotomy: either the power brings about existence before that second moment (impossible) or at that moment (then the first problem recurs). Thus both options fail. The argument relies on the premise that non-existence cannot be an object of power, a key principle in kalām.

Key Terms
القدرة
المقدور
العدم الأصلي
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That power can relate to an act at a time when the act does not exist, because the act will exist later.
Correction: The author argues that non-existence itself is not an object of power; power over a future act must be analyzed either as power at the time of the act or prior to it, and neither option works.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about power when it is considered prior to the existence of the act?
A) It is valid because the act exists later
B) It is impossible because non-existence is not a proper object of power
C) It is possible if the future act is known
D) It is the same as power at the moment of existence

Key Takeaway
The eighth argument shows that power cannot be over non-existence, and if it is only at the moment of the act, then the disbeliever is obligated beyond capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a command of Allah was beyond your capacity? How does this argument help you examine whether your inability is real or a matter of your choice?
?
When you fail to obey, do you tend to excuse yourself by saying 'I didn't have the power'? In light of this argument, how can you hold yourself accountable for the power you had at the moment of your action?
?
The argument shows that power must be at the moment of the act. In your life, how does this truth affect the way you pray for strength? Do you truly rely on Allah for power at the moment of need?

The Tenth Argument: The Impossibility of Unknown Parts
The author presents the tenth argument about the impossibility of commanding an act that requires knowledge of infinite parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tenth argument from the kalam tradition which shows that commanding a simple act like moving a finger entails an impossible obligation due to unknown infinite parts.

Core Idea
Even a simple command implies unknown infinite parts, making it beyond capacity.

This argument explores the implications of a very simple command: move your finger. Under the atomistic worldview of kalam, motion involves discrete parts of matter, space, and time. The author argues that the precise quantities of these parts are unknown, so the command becomes an impossible obligation. Let's see how this reasoning unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow. Notice how the author breaks down the act into parts, spaces, and moments.
2
Identify the three components
The argument lists three unknown quantities: parts of the finger, spaces of the distance, and moments of time. Notice the repetition of 'مجهولة'.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that commanding something unknown is beyond capacity. Think about why this is an impossibility in Islamic theology.
This connects to the concept of taklīf mā lā yuṭāq.
4
Reflect on the atomistic framework
Consider how this argument relies on the kalām view of atoms of matter, space, and time. Without that framework, the argument might not hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : إن العبد إذا صار مأموراً بأن يحرك أصبع نفسه، فقد صار مأموراً بتحريك جملة الأجزاء التي منها تركيبت ذلك الإصبع، في جميع تلك الأحياز التي منها تركيبت تلك المسافة في جميع تلك الآنات التي منها تركيب ذلك الزمان. ثم أن كمية تلك الأجزاء مجهولة، وكمية تلك الأحياز مجهولة، وكمية تلك الآنات مجهولة. فكان الأمر بهذا الفعل أمراً بتكوين ما هو مجهول من هذه الوجوه. وذلك تكليف بما ليس في الوسع. لأن القصد إلى تكوين ما هو غير معلوم ولا متصور تكليف بما لا يطاق.
The tenth: Indeed, if the servant becomes commanded to move his finger, he becomes commanded to move the totality of the parts from which that finger is composed, in all those spaces from which that distance is composed, in all those moments (ānāt) from which that time is composed. Then the quantity of those parts is unknown, the quantity of those spaces is unknown, and the quantity of those moments is unknown. So the command for this act becomes a command to bring about something that is unknown from these aspects. And that is an obligation beyond what is in capacity. Because intending to bring about what is not known and not conceivable is an obligation beyond capacity.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth argument, which considers the implications of a seemingly simple command — to move one's finger. The author argues that this simple act entails moving all the atomic parts that make up the finger, through all the spatial positions that constitute the distance, and across all the indivisible moments (ānāt) that constitute the time. The exact number of these parts, positions, and moments is unknown to the agent. Therefore, the command effectively orders the agent to bring about something whose precise constitution is unknown and inconceivable. Since intending to produce something unknown is impossible for a limited agent, the obligation is beyond capacity (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). This argument draws on the kalām atomistic conception of matter, time, and space, where motion is analysed as a change of position across discrete particles.

Key Terms
الأجزاء
الأحياز
الآنات
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the physical inability to move a finger.
Correction: The argument is not about factual inability but about the logical analysis of what such a command implies given the atomic structure of matter, space, and time.

Checkpoint
According to the tenth argument, why is commanding someone to move a finger an impossibility?
A) Fingers are too small to move
B) The number of parts, spaces, and moments involved is unknown
C) The person may not want to move
D) God does not command such actions

Key Takeaway
The tenth argument shows that a seemingly simple command can imply an impossible obligation when the underlying composition is unknown and infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This argument reveals that only the Creator fully knows the minute details of your actions, down to the atoms and moments. Does this humble you, and how can you turn that awareness into a deeper gratitude for His mercy in commanding only what He enables you to do?
?
If it is impossible for Allah to command something entirely unknown to the servant, what does that tell you about His justice and care? Are there areas in your life where you feel His commands are too burdensome? How does this teaching reassure you that He does not ask beyond your capacity?

First Objection: Essences and States as Objects of Power
The author presents the first additional objection regarding essences and attributes not being objects of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first additional objection raised by Ash'ari scholars against the Mu'tazila regarding the impossibility of power acting upon non-existent essences and states, and its implication of obligation beyond capacity.

Core Idea
If essences are non-existent and existence is a state, neither can be an object of power, making acts impossible to command.

The author now presents objections from his own school against the opponents' views on essences and attributes. The first objection targets the core Mu'tazili doctrine that essences exist as 'things' in non-existence and that existence is a mere state. By dissecting the components of an act, the objection argues that no part can be acted upon by divine power, leading to an impossible obligation. However, this objection does not apply to those who deny the reality of non-existent things.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is structured as a conditional argument: if essences are in non-existence, then power cannot affect them; if existence is a state, it cannot be an object of power; therefore the composite cannot be an object of power; therefore obligation is beyond capacity.
The author then adds an exception for Abul Husayn.
2
Identify the ontological components
The objection splits the act into essences (dhātāt) and existence (wujūd). Understand each: essences are 'established in non-existence', existence is a 'state' (ḥāl). According to the opponents, states are neither known nor objects of power. Why would that be a problem?
This is a technical point from Mu'tazili ontology.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
If neither essence alone nor existence alone is a valid object of power, then their combination (the existing essence) also cannot be an object of power. So commanding an act, which involves creating such an existing essence, is like commanding what cannot be done. This is a powerful objection against divine obligation.
The author from his own school is using their opponents' own premises against them.
4
Note the exception
The author clarifies that this objection does not apply to Abul Husayn al-Basri, because he denies that the non-existent is a thing (shay'). This avoids the initial premise about essences in non-existence.
This shows the importance of starting assumptions in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أصحابنا فقد أوردوا عليهم الزامات أخرى: أحدها: أن الذوات عندهم حاصلة في العدم، فلا يمكن أن يكون للقدر أثر فيها. وأما الصفة صفة الوجود فهي عندهم حال، والحال لا تكون معلومة ولا مقدورة. وأما الماهية الموجودة فهي ليست إلا الماهية وإلا الوجود. ولما لم يكن واحد منهما صالحا للمقدورية، لم يكن المجموع أيضاً صالحاً للمقدورية. فيلزمهم أن يكون التكليف بالفعل تكليفاً بما لا يطاق. إلا أن هذا الإلزام غير وارد على أبي الحسين، فإنه ينكر كون المعدوم شيئاً.
And as for our colleagues, they have presented other objections to them: One of them: that essences (al-dhātāt), according to them, are established in non-existence, so it is not possible for power to have an effect in them. And as for the attribute of existence (ṣifat al-wujūd), it is, according to them, a state (ḥāl), and the state is neither known nor able to be an object of power. And as for the existing essence (al-māhiyya al-mawjūda), it is nothing but the essence and the existence. And since neither of them is fit to be an object of power, the composite also is not fit to be an object of power. So it follows for them that the obligation to act is an obligation beyond capacity. However, this objection does not apply to Abū al-Ḥusayn, because he denies that the non-existent is a thing.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage begins a new set of objections raised by the author's own colleagues (aṣḥābunā) against the opponents. The first objection focuses on the ontological status of essences (dhātāt) and existence (wujūd). According to the opponents (likely the Muʿtazila), essences are 'established in non-existence' (ḥāṣila fī al-ʿadam) before creation, and existence is a 'state' (ḥāl) superadded to the essence. The author argues that power (qudra) cannot act upon an essence that is non-existent, nor upon a state, since states are neither known nor objects of power. The existing thing is the combination of essence and existence, but since neither component is a proper object of power, the composite cannot be either. Thus, obligating an act (which involves bringing such a composite into existence) would be an obligation beyond capacity. The author notes an exception: this objection does not apply to Abū al-Ḥusayn (likely Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī), who denies that the non-existent is a thing (shay'), thereby avoiding the problem of acting upon non-existent essences.

أبو الحسين

Misconception: The objection applies to all opponents universally.
Correction: It does not apply to those, like Abul Husayn, who deny that the non-existent is a thing.

D) He rejects the concept of states

Key Takeaway
The first objection shows that if essences are non-existent and existence is a state, then neither can be an object of power, making the command of an act an obligation beyond capacity, though this does not affect those who deny the reality of non-existent things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objection shows that if we impose human categories on God's action, we end up with contradictions. Are there any areas of your life where you try to fit God's commands into your own categories of 'possible' or 'reasonable'? What would it mean to let go of that and obey even when it seems beyond capacity?
?
This passage reminds us that God's power is not limited by the frameworks we construct. When you face a command from Allah that feels overwhelming, do you instinctively retreat into intellectual objections? How can you instead use that moment to deepen your reliance on the One whose power is not constrained by your understanding?

The Second Objection: Obligating the Unknown
The author presents the second objection regarding the impossibility of obligating what is unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the concept of ka'iniyya: if the act's being-ness is caused by a meaning that most people cannot conceive, then any obligation to acquire that meaning is an obligation of the impossible.

Core Idea
Obligating what is inconceivable is obligating the impossible.

This lesson presents a key objection from the author's colleagues against a particular theological position. The objection focuses on the notion of 'being-ness' (ka'iniyya) and the meaning that supposedly necessitates it. The argument proceeds by dividing the possibilities: either the obligation targets the being-ness itself or its necessitating meaning. Both paths lead to an impossible burden, since either the agent lacks power over the being-ness or the meaning is beyond conception for most people.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premises
The objection starts with two premises: (1) being-ness is caused by a meaning; (2) most people cannot conceive that meaning. Also, power has no effect on being-ness.
These premises are taken from the opponents' own views.
2
Follow the two branches
The obligation is considered in two ways: either to acquire the being-ness itself or to acquire its necessitating meaning. The author shows both are impossible.
This is a classic dilemma argument.
3
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is that any obligation involving the being-ness or its meaning is beyond capacity (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). The passage is cut off but the reasoning up to that point is clear.
The final phrase 'because bringing about...' is incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : إن عندهم الكائنية معللة بمعنى يوجبها. ثم إن أكثر الخلق لا يتصورون هذا المعنى. والقدرة لا تأثير لها في الكائنية أصلا. إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول : التكليف إن وقع بتحصيل الكائنية، كان ذلك تكليفا بما لا يطاق لان العبد لا قدرة له على الكائنية. وإن وقع بتحصيل ذلك الموجب، فذلك غير متصور الماهية، عند أكثر الخلق. فكان التكليف بإيجاده تكليفاً بتحصيل ما لا تكون ماهيته متصورة عند الذهن. وذلك تكليف بما لا يطاق. لأن إيجاد
And the second: Indeed, according to them, the being-ness (al-ka'iniyya) is caused by a meaning that necessitates it. Then indeed, most of creation do not conceive this meaning. And power has no effect on the being-ness at all. If this is established, we say: the obligation, if it concerns the acquisition of the being-ness, then that is an obligation beyond capacity because the servant has no power over the being-ness. And if it concerns the acquisition of that necessitator (al-mūjib), then that is inconceivable in its quiddity (māhiyya) for most of creation. So the obligation to bring it about is an obligation to acquire something whose quiddity is not conceived by the mind. And that is an obligation beyond capacity. Because bringing about...
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection from the author's colleagues. It targets the opponents' concept of 'being-ness' (ka'iniyya), which they claim is caused by a meaning that necessitates it. The author states that most people cannot conceive of this meaning, and that power (qudra) has no influence on the being-ness itself. From this, two possibilities follow: if the obligation is to obtain the being-ness itself, it is impossible because the agent has no power over it. If the obligation is to obtain the necessitating meaning, that meaning is inconceivable in its essence for most people, so commanding it is commanding something whose nature is unknown, which is also impossible. The argument is cut off at the beginning of a phrase ('because bringing about...'), but the core reasoning is complete: both options lead to taklīf mā lā yuṭāq. This objection relies on the premise that an obligation must involve something knowable and within the agent's power.

Key Takeaway
The second objection argues that any obligation involving ka'iniyya or its necessitating meaning is impossible because either the agent lacks power or the object is inconceivable.

Answering the Third Objection: Two Responses
Author gives two responses to the objection that God does not burden beyond capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways the author refutes the objection that God does not burden beyond capacity.

Core Idea
Most objections to taklif collapse once you deny rational good and evil.

The third objection claims that God would not burden beyond capacity because it is rationally unjust. The author offers two direct responses: first, he cites the ten proofs that God does exactly that; second, he exposes the objection's reliance on rational good and evil, a principle he rejects. Many examples reduce to this foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Note the two 'aspects' (وجهان) mentioned at the start.
2
Identify the two responses
Look for the first response: the ten proofs already show God burdens beyond capacity. The second: the objection is based on rational good and evil, which is invalid.
3
Examine the examples
The author lists several examples from opponents (e.g., sending messengers to inanimate objects). Notice how each example assumes an action is 'ugly' rationally.
The author claims these all reduce to the principle of rational good and evil.
4
Reflect on the implication
If the foundation of rational good and evil is denied, the entire objection and its examples collapse. This is a powerful methodological move.
This connects to the core idea of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثالثة : فالجواب عنها من وجهين : الأول : إنا بينا بالدلائل العشرة : أنه تعالى كلف بما لا يطاق . وذلك يبطل قولهم : أنه تعالى لا يفعل ذلك والثاني : إنه بناء على الحسن والقبح العقلين . وذلك باطل على ما سيأتي تقريره إن شاء الله واعلم : أن أكثر ما ذكروه من الوجوه راجع إلى هذا الأصل . مثل : قولهم : لو جاز تكليف العبد بما لا قدرة له عليه ، لجاز إرسال الرسل إلى الجمادات . وأيضاً : لا يجوز من الحكيم أن يخلق شتم نفسه . وأيضاً : لو جاز مثل هذا التكليف ، لجاز أن يقيد يديه ورجليه ، ويلقيه من شاهق الجبل ، ثم يضربه ، ويقول له : قف في الهواء. فإن هذه الكلمات يرجع حاصلها إلى أنها قبيحة في العقول . فإذا نازعنا في هذا الأصل ، فقد سقط الكل
As for the third doubt: the answer to it is from two aspects: First: We have demonstrated by the ten proofs that He, exalted, imposed an unbearable burden. That invalidates their statement that He, exalted, does not do that. Second: It is based on the rational good and evil. That is invalid according to what will be established later, God willing. And know that most of what they mentioned of arguments revert to this principle. Such as their statement: If it were permissible to burden the servant with what he has no power over, then it would be permissible to send messengers to inanimate objects. Also: It is not permissible for the Wise to create the reviling of Himself. Also: If such a burden were permissible, then it would be permissible to bind his hands and feet, throw him from a high mountain, then hit him, and say to him: 'Stay in the air.' For the upshot of these words is that they are deemed ugly by intellects. So if we dispute this principle, then everything collapses.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the author's response to the third objection (shubha) commonly raised against the Ash'ari position on divine burdening (taklif). The author identifies two main responses. First, he asserts that through ten proofs (al-dala'il al-'ashara), it has been established that God does indeed burden His servants beyond their capacity (kallafa bi ma la yutaq). This directly refutes the opponent's claim that God would not do such a thing. Second, the objection rests on the premise of rational good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqliyyayn), which the author states is invalid, promising a later refutation. The author then notes that many of the opponent's examples reduce to this same principle of rational good and evil. He lists a few: the impermissibility of sending messengers to inanimate objects, the impermissibility of the Wise creating reviling of Himself, and the absurd scenario of binding someone and commanding him to stay in the air. All these are deemed rationally wrong (qabiha fi al-'uqul). Thus, if one denies the foundation of rational good and evil, all these objections lose their force. The passage is both a refutation and a methodological reduction, showing the opponent's reliance on a principle the author rejects.

Key Terms
الشبهة الثالثة
تكليف ما لا يطاق
الحسن والقبح العقلين
الدلائل العشرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection assumes that God's actions can be evaluated as good or evil by human reason independently of revelation.
Correction: The author argues that rational good and evil is not a valid foundation; thus, any objection based on it is invalid. The ten proofs from revelation establish that God does burden beyond capacity.

Checkpoint
What is the second aspect of the author's answer to the third objection?
A) That they have proven by ten proofs that God burdens beyond capacity
B) That the objection is based on rational good and evil, which is invalid
C) That the examples are valid
D) That the objection is about messengers to inanimate objects

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the third objection by denying the principle of rational good and evil, thereby collapsing all examples that depend on it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says most objections to taklif collapse if we deny that human reason can judge good and evil. Is there an area where you still hold God to a human standard—like expecting immediate reward for good deeds? Are you willing to let go of that expectation completely?
?

The Meaning of 'Allah' and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Refutation of Mu'tazila on the meaning of 'Allah' and proof it is a proper noun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna argue that 'Allah' does not mean 'the one deserving of worship' and how they refute the Mu'tazila objection.

Core Idea
The name 'Allah' is a proper noun, not derived from a meaning like 'deserving of worship'.

The Mu'tazila argued that if only God is capable of worship, then He must worship Himself, which is absurd. Ahl al-Sunna respond by showing that this argument rests on a false premise about the meaning of the word 'Allah'. In this passage, they present two key proofs that the name 'Allah' is not derived from the idea of being deserving of worship. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Understand the objection that if the servant cannot worship, God must worship Himself.
This sets up the need for refutation.
2
Identify the first proof
The first proof states that 'Allah' is a proper noun, not derived from any root.
This is a linguistic argument.
3
Examine the second proof
Even if derived, it does not mean 'deserving of worship'; there are two sub-proofs.
The sub-proofs involve absurd consequences and rational impossibility.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how this refutation undermines the Mu'tazila objection and clarifies the nature of the name 'Allah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على فعل (١) العبادة . أما إذا كان العبد غير قادر على العبادة . بل يكون القادر على العبادة هو الله تعالى لزم أن يقال : إنه استحق على نفسه أن يعبد نفسه ، وذلك غير معقول . قال أهل السنة : كلامكم مبني على أن لفظ و الله ، معناه : المستحق للعبادة . وهذا باطل . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنا بينا في تفسير أسماء الله : أن « الله ) : اسم [ علم (۲) ] غير مشتق . [ الثاني (٣) ] : لو سلمنا أنه مشتق ، لكن لا نسلم أن معناه : أنه الذي يستحق العبادة . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنه لو كان كذلك ، لوجب أن لا يكون إنها للجمادات والبهائم ، لأنه - تعالى - يستحق عليها العبادة ، ولزم أن لا يكون إلهاً في الأزل لم يخلق أحداً ، ولم ينعم على أحد فلم يكن مستحقاً للعبادة . الثاني : إنا بينا : أن العقل لا يدل على حصول الاستحقاق . لأنه لا يتفاوت حال المعبود بسبب هذه العبادة - وهي شاقة على العابد - فوجب أن لا يحكم العقل بوجوبه . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : الإله هو القادر على الاختراع . والدليل عليه : أنه تعالى إنما ظهر الامتياز بينه وبين سائر الذوات بصفته الخلاقية . فقال : أفمن يخلق كمن لا يخلق (٤) ) ؟ ولما كان المذكور في معرض الامتياز هو هذه الصفة ، علمنا : أن معني الإلهية ليس إلا الخلاقية . فلو كان العبـد مـوجــداً لأفعال نفسه ، لكان خالقاً لها ، وحينئذ يبطل الفرق بين الإله ، وبين العبد في المخص أوصاف الإلهية . فكان قولنا ( بسم الله ) دالا على أن العبد غير موجد من هذا الوجه .
Upon the act of worship. But if the servant is not capable of worship, rather the One capable of worship is Allah the Exalted, it necessarily follows that it be said: He deserved to worship Himself, and that is unreasonable. Ahl al-Sunna said: Your speech is based on the claim that the word 'Allah' means 'the One deserving of worship.' And this is false. Two proofs indicate this: First: We have explained in the interpretation of the names of Allah that 'Allah' is a proper noun, not derived. Second: If we grant that it is derived, we do not grant that its meaning is that He is the One who deserves worship. Two proofs indicate this: First: If it were so, it would necessitate that He would not be an ilah for inanimate objects and animals, because He the Exalted deserves worship from them, and it would necessitate that He would not be an ilah in eternity when He had not created anyone and had not bestowed any favor upon anyone, so He would not have been deserving of worship. Second: We have explained that reason does not indicate the occurrence of deservingness. Because the state of the worshipped does not change due to this worship – and it is burdensome upon the worshipper – so reason must not judge it obligatory. Once this is established, we say: Al-Ilah is the One able to create. The proof for it: That the Exalted only manifested distinction between Himself and other essences by His attribute of creatorship. He said: 'Is He who creates like one who does not create?' And since this attribute is the one mentioned in the context of distinction, we know that the meaning of divinity is nothing but creatorship. So if the servant were the originator of his own actions, he would be a creator of them, and then the distinction between al-Ilah and the servant would be nullified in the specific attributes of divinity. Thus our saying 'Bismillah' indicates that the servant is not an originator from this aspect.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. It begins with the Mu'tazila objection: if God alone is capable of worship, then He must worship Himself, which is absurd. Ahl al-Sunna respond by pointing out that this objection is based on a false premise – that 'Allah' means 'the one deserving of worship.' They refute this in two stages. First, they argue that 'Allah' is a proper noun, not derived from any root. Second, even if it were derived, it does not mean 'deserving of worship.' They give two proofs for this: (1) If it meant that, then God would have to be deserving of worship from inanimate objects and animals, and He would not have been an ilah in eternity before creating anything. (2) Reason cannot determine that worship is deserved, since worship does not benefit the worshipped. Then the passage moves to a positive definition: 'al-Ilah' is the one able to create (al-qadir 'ala al-ikhtira'). The proof is from the Quranic verse 'Is He who creates like one who does not create?' showing that the distinguishing attribute is creatorship. Thus, divinity is essentially creatorship. The conclusion follows that if the servant were the creator of his own actions, the distinction between God and servant would vanish. Therefore, saying 'Bismillah' implies that the servant is not the originator of his acts.

Key Terms
الإله
المستحق للعبادة
اسم علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'Allah' is derived from 'ilāh' meaning 'object of worship' and thus means 'the one deserving of worship'.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna argue that 'Allah' is a proper noun not derived, and even if derived, it does not mean 'deserving of worship' because that leads to absurdities and reason cannot determine such deservingness.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna in this passage, what is the first proof against the Mu'tazila claim about the meaning of 'Allah'?
A) 'Allah' means 'the One able to create'
B) 'Allah' is a proper noun not derived
C) 'Allah' means 'the One deserving of worship'
D) 'Allah' is derived from 'ilāh'

Key Takeaway
The name 'Allah' is a proper noun, not derived from a meaning like 'deserving of worship', thus the Mu'tazila objection based on that meaning is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes feel that Allah owes you something because you worship Him? How does the truth that worship does not benefit Allah change that feeling?
?
If the name 'Allah' is simply His proper noun and not derived from what He does for you, does that affect how you approach Him in prayer? Is your worship still genuine when you know He gets nothing from it?
?
When you think of Allah as 'the One deserving of worship', do you subconsciously measure His worthiness by what He has given you? How can you train your heart to worship Him purely for who He is, not for what you receive?

The True Meaning of 'Ilah' and Proof from Creatorship
Definition of 'al-Ilah' as the Creator and proof from Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'al-Ilah' means the One able to create, and how the Quranic proof establishes creatorship as the distinguishing attribute of divinity.

Core Idea
Divinity is essentially creatorship; only Allah creates.

After refuting the Mu'tazila misinterpretation of the name 'Allah', the author now provides the positive definition of 'al-Ilah' and its proof. This passage is crucial for understanding the Sunni concept of tawhid al-uluhiyya, tying it directly to the attribute of creatorship. Let's see how the Quran and reason point to this.

Guided Path
1
Read the positive definition
The author states: 'al-Ilah is the One able to create'.
This is the core claim.
2
Understand the Quranic proof
The verse 'Is He who creates like one who does not create?' shows that creatorship is the distinguishing attribute.
3
Consider the implication
If humans created their own actions, the distinction between God and servant would vanish.
This refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine of human agency.
4
Connect to 'Bismillah'
Saying 'Bismillah' implies that the servant is not the originator of his actions.
This shows practical application.

First Sunni Refutation: The Same Dilemma
Sunni response: if power and motive necessitate action, Mu'tazila are also forced.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunnis turn the Mu'tazila's own argument against them using a logical dilemma about power and motive.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila cannot escape: power and motive either necessitate action or not—both options backfire.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God creates all actions, He would be compelling sin. The Sunnis respond by showing that the Mu'tazila's own framework leads to the same conclusion. This passage presents the first of several responses, a dilemma that forces the Mu'tazila to choose between two unacceptable consequences. Let's see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully. Notice the initial claim: 'This which you presented against us applies to you.' Then identify the two branches.
The passage is a classic kalam dilemma.
2
Identify the two branches
The argument splits into two cases: either the act is necessitated when power and motive are present, or it is not necessitated. Understand each branch.
3
Trace the implications of each branch
If necessitated, then God compels the servant to sin. If not necessitated, the act occurs by chance and is not under the servant's power, making punishment unjust.
Both outcomes are problematic for the Mu'tazila.
4
Connect to the Mu'tazila position
Realize that the Mu'tazila wanted to affirm free will and deny divine coercion, but this dilemma shows their own view leads to either coercion or unjust punishment.
Thus, their objection against Sunnis applies to themselves.
5
Appreciate the dialectical strength
This is a powerful refutation because it uses the opponent's own assumptions (power and motive as causes) against them. It does not deny human responsibility but exposes inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل السنة والجماعة : هذا الذي أوردتم علينا ، وارد عليكم وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : عند حصول القدرة والداعية . إما أن يجب الفعل ، أو لا يجب . فإن وجب ، لزمكم ما ألزمتموه علينا . لأنه تعالى هو الخالق لتلك القدرة ولتلك الداعية . وهما يوجبان الكفر والفسق . فكان ذلك إجبار للعبد على القبح . وأما إن قلتم : إن عند حصول القدرة والداعية لا يجب الفعل فحينئذ يكون صدور الفعل اتفاقياً ، والاتفاقي لا يكون مقدوراً للعبد . فيترتب أن العقاب عليه ظلم . فثبت : أن الذي أوردوه علينا وارد عليهم لا محالة
Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a said: This which you have presented against us is also present against you. Its clarification is from several aspects: The first: When power (al-qudra) and motive (al-dā‘iya) are present, either the act is necessitated (wājib) or not. If it is necessitated, then what you have imposed on us applies to you, because He Almighty is the creator of that power and that motive, and they necessitate disbelief and wickedness. So that would be compelling the servant to evil. And if you say that when power and motive are present the act is not necessitated, then in that case the occurrence of the act is contingent (ittifāqī), and the contingent is not within the servant's power. So punishment for it would follow as injustice. So it is established that what they presented against us necessarily applies to them.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is the first of several Sunni responses to the Mu'tazila. The argument is a dilemma. The Sunni scholars point out that the Mu'tazila believe that human acts proceed from power (qudra) and motive (dā‘iya) given by God. They then pose two possibilities: either when power and motive are present, the act is necessitated (wājib) or not. If it is necessitated, then God's creation of that power and motive forces the servant to perform the sinful act, so God becomes the coercer, which is exactly what the Mu'tazila criticize. If it is not necessitated, then the act occurs by chance (ittifāqī), and what is by chance is not under the servant's power—so punishing for it is unjust. Either way, the Mu'tazila face the same objection they raised against the Sunnis. Thus, the dilemma shows that their own position is inconsistent.

Key Terms
أهل السنة والجماعة
القدرة
الداعية
الوجوب
الاتفاقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni argument denies human free will and proves determinism.
Correction: The argument does not assert determinism; it reveals a logical inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position by showing that their own assumptions lead to unacceptable consequences.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni dilemma, if the act is necessitated when power and motive are present, what follows?
A) The act is voluntary.
B) God compels the servant to sin.
C) The act is by chance.
D) The servant is free.

Key Takeaway
The Sunnis show that the Mu'tazila's objection against predestination applies equally to their own view, using a dilemma that forces them to choose between divine coercion and unjust punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'God made me do it' or 'It was out of my control'? This lesson shows that both extremes—necessitation and coincidence—lead to injustice. Will you today take full ownership of your actions, acknowledging that your choices are real even though God is the ultimate creator? Write a one-sentence pledge.
?
Imagine you are a Mu'tazili debating with a Sunni. The Sunni forces you to choose: either God compels you to sin, or your sins are random accidents. Neither feels right. But what if your own heart already struggles with this tension? Reflect: Do you truly believe you are free, or do you sense a mystery here that no system fully solves? How will you humble yourself before that mystery without abandoning responsibility?
?
This passage uses pure logic to corner an opponent. But logic alone cannot save you. If you rely on your own reasoning to understand fate and free will, you might end up like the Mu'tazila—trapped. Do you trust your intellect more than the revealed wisdom of the Salaf? What would it mean to submit your questions about qadar to the Quran and Sunnah, even when they seem paradoxical?
