Second Proof: Proportionality of Punishment Refutes Mu'tazila
Sanusi presents a reductio ad absurdum against Mu'tazila on divine justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the Mu'tazila's own principle of proportional justice to argue that God's judgments are not bound by human rationality.

Core Idea
Finite sins and eternal punishment: God's justice transcends human measure.

This proof turns the Mu'tazila’s own ethical rationalism against them. If punishment must fit the crime, then eternal punishment for finite sin would be unjust by human standards. But God decrees eternal punishment, so His judgments cannot be compared to ours. This paragraph shows why Mu'tazila theology collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the flow from premise to conclusion.
The bracketed part is a footnote explaining the actual divine decree.
2
Identify the logical premises
Find the two key premises: (1) human disbelief and sin are finite, (2) reason requires punishment to be proportionate to the crime. See how they lead to an expected finite punishment.
3
Observe the contradiction
Notice that God has actually decreed eternal (infinite) punishment for some sins. This contradicts the expected finite punishment under human reasoning. Why does Sanusi use this contradiction?
4
Understand the conclusion
The contradiction shows that we cannot judge God's actions by our standards of good and evil. Therefore, the Mu'tazila doctrine that actions have inherent moral value that binds God is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن كفر العبد ومعصيته متناهية . والعقل يقضي أن يكون العقاب بقدر الجرم . لا زائداً عليه ، فوجب أن يكون عقاب الكفر والمعصية متناهياً [ فلما جعل عقاباته غير متناهية (۱) ] علمنا أنه لا يمكن أن يقال : أحكام أفعاله على أحكام أفعالنا في الحسن والقبح . وذلك يبطل قول المعتزلة .
The second: that the disbelief and disobedience of the servant are finite. And reason judges that punishment is proportionate to the offense, not exceeding it. So it is necessary that the punishment for disbelief and disobedience be finite. [Since He made their punishments infinite (1)], we know that it cannot be said: His judgments about our actions are like our judgments about good and evil. And that invalidates the statement of the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Mu'tazila's own principle of justice against them. Sanusi argues that since disbelief and sin are finite acts, reason dictates that punishment should be proportional and finite. However, God has decreed eternal punishment for such acts. If God's judgments were analogous to human ones, that would be unjust. Therefore, we cannot compare God's judgments to human ones, which refutes the Mu'tazila position that actions have inherent good and evil that God must follow.

Key Terms
كفر
معصية
متناهية
العقاب
الحسن والقبح
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that eternal punishment is unjust because it violates proportionality.
Correction: The argument does not claim eternal punishment is unjust; instead, it shows that God's judgments are not bound by human rational standards, so the Mu'tazila cannot use proportionality to criticize divine actions.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the second proof against the Mu'tazila?
A) Human actions are infinite in duration.
B) Punishment must be proportional to the crime, and since sins are finite, eternal punishment violates human justice standards, showing God's judgments are incomparable.
C) God cannot punish eternally because that would be unjust.
D) The Mu'tazila believe in eternal punishment.

Key Takeaway
The eternal punishment for finite sins proves that God's judgments are not constrained by human notions of proportionality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your finite sins are met with infinite mercy from God—or, in this argument, the possibility of infinite punishment—how does that reshape your sense of urgency about repentance? Are you presuming too much on His mercy or neglecting His justice?
?
Sanusi says that God's judgments are not bound by our sense of proportionality. Does that thought make you feel small, or does it liberate you from measuring your worth by your own moral scorecard? What would you have to let go of to fully submit to a God whose justice does not fit your calculations?
?
When you sin, do you secretly believe God owes you a chance because your sin was 'not that bad'? How does this passage expose that assumption as a subtle form of Mu'tazila thinking, and what will you do to root it out of your heart?

Opponent's Argument from Commands and Prohibitions
Opponent cites Quranic commands and uses rational analogy to argue for human capacity; author starts his response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that Quranic commands and prohibitions prove human capacity, and the beginning of the author's response referencing his earlier rational rebuttal.

Core Idea
Commands imply ability; otherwise they are absurd.

The opponents of the Ash'ari position on qadar often cite the numerous commands in the Quran as evidence that humans must have free will and capacity. In this passage, they list many such verses and argue that commanding someone incapable would be as foolish as commanding inanimate objects. The author then begins his rebuttal by referencing his earlier rational arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the cited Quranic verses
Notice the many Quranic commands cited, from 'hasten to forgiveness' to 'obey Allah and the Messenger'. These are all direct commands to humans.
The opponent uses these as evidence that humans must be capable of obeying.
2
Understand the logical argument
The opponent argues that commands and prohibitions would not be accepted if the servant had no power over action and inaction. It is rationally absurd to say 'Do!' to one who cannot do, just as it would be absurd to send prophets to inanimate objects.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if commands didn't require ability, one could command rocks.
3
Observe the author's initial response
The author begins his answer by referencing his earlier refutation of rational doubts, mentioning 'that burdening what does not...' The sentence is cut off, but he is pointing to the concept of 'taklif ma la yutaq' (burdening what is not possible).
The full response continues in the following pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني من وجوه استدلالتهم من كتاب الله تعالى: الأمر والنهي: وسارعوا إلى مغفرة من ربكم، سابقوا إلى مغفرة من ربكم، أجيبوا داعي الله وآمنوا به، استجيبوا لله يا أيها الذين آمنوا اركعوا واسجدوا واعبدوا ربكم، آمنوا خيراً لكم، واتبعوا أحسن ما أنزل إليكم من ربكم، وأنيبوا إلى ربكم، وما آتاكم الرسول فخذوه وما نهاكم عنه فانتهوا، أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول. وبالجملة: فالأوامر والنواهي الواردة في كتاب الله، لا حد لها ولا حصر، ولو لم يكن العبد قادراً على الفعل والترك، لم يقبل أمره ولا نهيه، وكيف يجوز في العقل أن يقال: افعل يا من لا يفعل؟ ولو جاز ذلك، فلم لا يجوز أن يبعث الأنبياء إلى الجمادات، وينزل عليهم الكتب ويملأ تلك الكتب من الوعد والوعيد للجمادات؟ وكما أن صريح لفظ القرآن يشهد بأن كل ذلك سفه وعبث، فكذلك صريح العقل يشهد بأن أمر العبد ونهيه - مع لا قدرة له على الفعل والترك - عبث وسفه. والجواب: إنا بينا في باب الجواب عن شبههم العقلية: إن تكليف ما لا...
The second aspect of their proofs from the Book of Allah: command and prohibition: 'And hasten to forgiveness from your Lord,' 'Race to forgiveness from your Lord,' 'Respond to Allah’s caller and believe in Him,' 'Respond to Allah, O you who have believed, bow and prostrate and worship your Lord,' 'Believe, it is better for you,' 'And follow the best of what was revealed to you from your Lord,' 'And turn in repentance to your Lord,' 'And whatever the Messenger gives you, take it, and whatever he forbids you, abstain from it,' 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger.' In summary: the commands and prohibitions in the Book of Allah are without limit or count. If the servant were not capable of action and inaction, his command and prohibition would not be accepted. And how is it rational to say: 'Do, O you who does not do?' And if that were permissible, why would it not be permissible to send prophets to inanimate objects and reveal books to them and fill those books with promise and threat for inanimate objects? Just as the explicit wording of the Quran bears witness that all that is foolishness and frivolity, so likewise explicit reason bears witness that commanding and prohibiting the servant while he has no power over action and inaction is foolishness and frivolity. The answer: We have explained in the section on answering their rational doubts: that burdening what does not...
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument of the opponents from scripture based on commands and prohibitions (al-amr wa al-nahy). The opponent lists numerous Quranic verses that command or prohibit specific actions directed at humans, such as racing to forgiveness, responding to Allah, bowing, obeying the messenger, etc. The argument is that these commands would be meaningless if the human had no ability to perform or refrain from the commanded/prohibited acts. If the servant were not capable of action and inaction, the command would not be accepted. It is rationally absurd to say 'Do!' to one who cannot do. If that were allowed, then sending prophets to inanimate objects and giving them books of promise and threat would also be allowed, which is clearly foolishness. Therefore, both Quranic expression and rational necessity prove that humans have power over their actions. The author begins his answer by referring to his earlier refutation of their rational arguments, stating that he has already explained 'that burdening what does not...' (the sentence is cut off at the page break). This indicates that the full answer will continue on the following pages.

Key Terms
الأمر
النهي
القدرة
الفعل
الترك
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commands prove humans have independent power to act without Allah's creation.
Correction: The author agrees commands imply ability, but that ability is created by Allah at the moment of action, not independently possessed by the human.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the opponent's argument from commands and prohibitions?
A) Commands are meaningless if the person lacks the ability to obey or disobey.
B) The Quran contains too many commands to count.
C) Allah does not command impossible things.
D) Prophets were sent to inanimate objects.

Key Takeaway
The opponent uses Quranic commands to argue for human capacity, but the author will counter by explaining that Allah creates the capacity at the time of action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent's argument makes sense to our minds: commands seem useless without ability. Yet the author begins his response by referencing 'burdening what is not possible.' Have you ever quietly resented a divine command as too difficult? How does this passage expose the limits of your own reasoning about Allah's justice?
?
Imagine you are one of the 'inanimate objects' in the reductio – completely incapable of responding. Would Allah still be just in commanding you? What does your answer reveal about your understanding of His sovereignty?
?
If you truly believe that every moment of obedience is a gift of ability from Allah, how would you approach the next verse of command in your daily reading? Will you let this lesson transform your prayer from a checklist into a plea for capability?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
12 min
Objections Against Divine Creation of All Acts and the Response
Author argues against divine creation of all actions and responds with a positive account.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections against the doctrine that God creates all human actions, and the author's response grounding human responsibility in the combination of ability and motive.

Core Idea
Human acts are not directly created by God; the combination of ability and motive produces them.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a crucial debate in Islamic theology. The author first raises two rational objections against the view that God creates all human acts: if God creates both condition and consequence, the servant is excluded, and reward/punishment become meaningless. Then a scriptural argument from Quran 19:88-91 is given to reinforce the point. The author then provides a response, grounding human responsibility in the combination of ability (qudra) and motive (dāʿī).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in before analyzing.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the first objection
The first objection argues that if God creates both the condition (e.g., obedience) and the consequence (reward), the servant is excluded from the equation, making conditional promises absurd.
Compare the divine speech 'If you obey, I reward' with a self-referential 'If I do X, I do Y'.
3
Understand the second objection
The second objection states that reward and punishment only encourage if the servant has genuine ability to choose. If God creates all actions, that ability vanishes, making the threat/ promise useless.
This is a common theological argument for human free will.
4
Analyze the scriptural argument and the author's response
The author cites Quran 19:88-91 to show that if God created the disbelievers' statement, the rebuke would be self-condemning. Then the response: actions arise from the combination of ability and motive, not direct divine creation.
The response also appeals to the earlier twelve aspects about burdening beyond capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان المقدمة الثانية. وهي قولنا: إن ذلك يقتضي أن لا يكون فعل العبد فعلا الله تعالى. فتقديره من وجهين: الأول: إنه لو كان الشرط فعلا الله تعالى، كما أن الجزاء فعله، لصار العبد من هذا الشرط والجزاء خارجاً من البين، ويصير التقدير كأنه تعالى يقول: إني متى فعلت الفعل الفلاني فإني أفعل بعده فعلاً آخر. إلا أن التغاير بين هذا الكلام وبين قوله أيها العبد إن أطعتني أثبتك، وإن عصيتني عاقبتك: معلوم بالضرورة. الثاني: إنه تعالى إنما رتب هذه الأجزية على أفعال العباد ترغيباً لهم في الطاعات ومنعاً لهم عن المحظورات. وهذا إنما يفيد إذا كانوا قادرين على الفعل والترك، متمكنين من كل واحد منهما على البذل. وقد بينا: أنه لو كان موجد أفعال العباد هو الله تعالى لما بقيت هذه القدرة وحينئذ يصير هذا الترغيب والترهيب عبئاً بالكلية. ومما يؤكد ذلك قوله تعالى: (وقالوا: اتخذ الرحمن ولداً. لقد جئتم شيئاً إداً. تكاد السموات يتفطرن منه وتنشق الأرض وتخر الجبال هداً: أن دعوا للرحمن ولداً) والاستدلال به من وجهين: الأول: إن ذلك القول لو كان مخلوقاً لله تعالى لصار الكلام ركيكا لأنه يصير تقدير الكلام: تكاد السموات أجعلها منفطرة والأرضين أجعلها منشقة بما خلقت في العبد هذا الكلام، وأردته وقضيت به. ومعلوم: أن ذلك في غاية الركاكة. وأيضاً: فقوله: (لقد جئتم شيئاً إداً) نص في أن العبد هو الذي جاء بذلك الفعل. الثاني: إن الواحد منا إذا أنكر على غيره فعلاً، ثم أتي بمثله عد سفيها. قال تعالى: (أتأمرون الناس بالبر، وتنسون أنفسكم)؟ فلما عاب عليهم قولهم إن للرحمن ولداً فالذي خلق ذلك القول فيهم يكون العبث عليه الزم. لو أن قائلاً قال: تأملوا قبح قولهم إن للرحمن ولداً، ومن قبحه انفطرت السموات وانشقت الأرض فانهدت الجبال ثم مع ذلك فإني أنا الذي فعلت ذلك، وخلقته فيهم وقضيته عليهم وأردته منهم. لقال الناس: ما أشد حماقة هذا القائل، وما أقبح صنعه. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فكيف يليق هذا برب العالمين؟ والجواب: إن عندنا مجموع القدرة والداعي، موجب للفعل، وعلى هذا التقدير فهذه الإلزامات ساقطة عنا. وأيضا: فالوجوه الاثني عشر المذكورة دالة على وقوع تكليف ما لا يطاق، وكل ذلك يبطل هذه الكلمات. والله أعلم
And the explanation of the second premise, which is our statement: that this necessitates that the act of the servant not be an act of God Almighty. So its exposition is from two aspects: First: if the condition were an act of God Almighty, just as the consequence is His act, then the servant would be excluded from both this condition and consequence, and the estimation would become as if He Almighty says: 'Whenever I do such-and-such an act, then I will do another act after it.' But the difference between this speech and His saying 'O servant, if you obey Me I will reward you, and if you disobey Me I will punish you' is known necessarily. Second: He Almighty only arranged these rewards upon the acts of the servants as an encouragement for them in acts of obedience and a deterrence for them from prohibited things. And this only benefits if they are capable of doing and refraining, able to exert each one. And we have shown: that if the creator of the servants' acts were God Almighty, this ability would not remain, and then this encouragement and deterrence would become completely useless. And what confirms that is His Almighty saying: (And they said: The Most Merciful has taken a son. Indeed, you have come with an egregious thing. The heavens almost break apart from it, and the earth splits, and the mountains fall in ruin: that they attributed to the Most Merciful a son). And the inference from it is from two aspects: First: if that statement were created by God Almighty, the speech would become awkward because the estimation of the speech would be: 'The heavens – I make them break apart, and the earths – I make them split, by what I created in the servant of this speech, and I willed it and decreed it.' And it is known that this is extremely awkward. Also: His statement (Indeed, you have come with an egregious thing) is a clear text that the servant is the one who committed that act. Second: if one of us disapproves of another's action, then does the like of it, he is considered foolish. He Almighty said: (Do you enjoin righteousness upon people while you forget yourselves)? So when He criticized them for their statement that the Most Merciful has a son, then the one who created that statement in them would be more deserving of frivolity. If a speaker said: 'Consider the ugliness of their statement that the Most Merciful has a son, and from its ugliness the heavens break apart, the earth splits, and the mountains collapse, yet despite that, I am the one who did that, created it in them, decreed it upon them, and willed it from them' – people would say: 'How severe is the foolishness of this speaker, and how ugly his deed.' And if the matter is thus, how is this befitting the Lord of the worlds? The answer: Among us, the combination of ability and motive necessitates the act, and upon this estimation these implications are repelled from us. Also: the twelve mentioned aspects indicate the occurrence of burdening beyond capacity, and all that invalidates these words. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding whether God creates all human actions. The author first presents an objection (the second premise) that if God creates both the condition and the consequence in conditional sentences, the servant is excluded. Then a second objection: rewards and punishments would be useless if God creates all acts. Then a scriptural argument from Quran 19:88-91: if God created the speech of the disbelievers, the Quranic rebuke would be awkward. The objection is developed with two aspects: (1) the verse would be self-condemning if God created the speech, and (2) God's criticism of others would apply to Himself if He created their speech. The author then provides an answer: the combination of ability (qudra) and motive (dāʿī) necessitates the act, so the implications are avoided. Additionally, the twelve aspects (mentioned earlier) showing burdening beyond capacity invalidate these objections. The passage ends with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
القدرة
الداعي
تكليف ما لا يطاق
الشرط
الجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates all actions, then human responsibility for reward and punishment is meaningless.
Correction: The author argues that this leads to absurdities (self-referential divine speech, useless rewards) and instead proposes that human acts arise from the combination of ability and motive, preserving responsibility.

Checkpoint
According to the response in this passage, what is the cause of an action?
A) Divine creation alone
B) Human power alone
C) The combination of ability and motive
D) Random chance

Key Takeaway
The combination of ability and motive is the true cause of human actions, preserving accountability while acknowledging divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'God created this in me' or 'It was destined for me'? How does this lesson's insistence that your ability and motive cause your actions confront that excuse? Will you today take full ownership of your sin and turn to God in repentance, without blaming Him?
?
In your daily life, are you aware of times when your motives are selfish? This lesson teaches that your actions stem from your ability and motive. If you know your motive is corrupt, what concrete step will you take today to purify it, knowing that God holds you accountable?
?
The objection in the lesson shows that if God directly created all acts, His criticism of evil would be foolish. How does this deepen your conviction that God is just and that you cannot blame Him for your faults? Will you now stop justifying your sins by divine decree and instead seek His forgiveness with humility?

Rational Judgments

8 min
First rational proof: the disbeliever's power and the need for a preponderator
Author presents the first rational proof for God's creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first rational proof that God creates human actions by necessitating a preponderator.

Core Idea
A disbeliever's equal power over opposites proves an external preponderator must come from God.

This lesson presents the first of several rational proofs for the Ash'ari position that human actions are created by God. The author argues that a disbeliever capable of both faith and disbelief cannot choose one without a preponderating cause, and that cause must ultimately be from God. Let's walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice how the author uses conditional logic.
2
Identify the key premise
The core premise is that the disbeliever's power is equally related to both opposites. Understand why equality necessitates a preponderator.
3
Trace the dichotomy
The argument uses a dichotomy: either the disbeliever is not capable of faith (conclusion obtained) or he is capable. Follow the second branch to see where it leads.
4
Understand the regress argument
If the preponderator comes from the servant, the same question reapplies, leading to infinite regress. See why only God can be the ultimate preponderator.
5
Appreciate the conclusion
The author declares it a conclusive proof that God creates the motive in the disbeliever's heart.
The editor's critique will be examined in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الأول : وهو أن الكافر المقدم على الكفر إن لم يكن متمكناً من الإيمان فقد حصل المطلوب ، وإن كان متمكناً منه كانت قدرته بالنسبة إلى الضدين على السوية . فإقدامه على الكفر دون الإيمان - مع أن ضده كان ممكنات . يستغني عن المرجح أو يفتقر إليه . فإن كان الأول لزم استغناء الممكن عن الموجح . وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني فذلك المرجح ، إن كان من العبد عــاد التقسيم فيه ، وإن كان من الله تعالى ، فهو المطلوب. لأنه ظهر أن الكافر ما أقدم على الكفر إلا لأجل أن الله تعالى قوى تلك الداعية في قلبه ، وقد دللنـا على أنه متى حصلت الداعية المرجحة ، فإنه لا بد من [ الوجوب (1) ) وهذا . برهان قاطع لا محيص عنه (۲) (۱) من ( ط ، ل ) . (۲) برهان ليس قاطعاً . لأنه افترض المرجح بين الله والإنسان. ومن الممكن افتراض المرجح من الإنسان نفسه . أي أن الإنسان إذا ظهر له خير وظهر له شر . فإن الإنسان يمكنه بنفسه ترجيح جانب الخير ، أو ترجيح جانب الشر ، ومن الممكن افتراض المرجح في ذات الشيء نفسه . على =
The first proof: That the disbeliever proceeding to disbelief, if he is not capable of faith, then the required (conclusion) is obtained. And if he is capable of it, then his power (qudra) with respect to the two opposites is equal. So his proceeding to disbelief rather than faith – while its opposite was possible – either dispenses with a preponderator (murajjih) or depends on it. If the first, then the possible would be independent of the necessitator, which is impossible. And if the second, then that preponderator – if it is from the servant, the division returns upon it; and if it is from God the Exalted, then it is the required (conclusion). Because it has become apparent that the disbeliever only proceeded to disbelief because God the Exalted strengthened that motive in his heart, and we have proven that whenever the preponderating motive occurs, then [obligation] necessarily follows. And this is a conclusive proof from which there is no escape. (1) From (T, L). (2) It is not a conclusive proof, because it assumed the preponderator is between God and man. And it is possible to assume the preponderator from man himself…
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the Ash'ari position that God creates human actions. The author presents the first argument: The disbeliever who commits disbelief either is capable of faith or not. If not, then the point is conceded (God prevents him). If he is capable, then his power is equally related to both opposites (faith and disbelief). His choice of disbelief over faith therefore requires a preponderator (murajjih) to explain why one possibility occurs rather than the other. This preponderator cannot come from the servant because that would lead to an infinite regress (the same question reapplies to the servant's act of preponderating). Therefore, the preponderator must be from God, meaning God creates the motive that determines the action. The author declares it a conclusive proof. The text includes an editor's marginal note criticizing the proof, but the author's original argument ends before that note.

Key Terms
الكافر
متمكناً
الضدين
المرجح
الداعية المرجحة
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof is circular because it assumes the need for a preponderator without justification.
Correction: The proof does not assume the need; it derives it from the equality of power. Equality logically necessitates a preponderator, as per the principle of sufficient reason.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why can't the preponderator come from the servant?
A) Because the servant does not have free will
B) Because that would lead to an infinite regress
C) Because God has already decreed the action
D) Because the servant is not capable of choice

Key Takeaway
The first rational proof shows that a disbeliever's equal power over opposites requires an external preponderator, which must ultimately come from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the disbeliever in this proof: He had equal power to choose faith, yet he chose disbelief because Allah strengthened a motive in his heart. Think of a time when you chose something wrong despite knowing better. Does this proof suggest that Allah played a role in that choice? If so, how does that affect your view of your own responsibility? What does it mean for how you ask Allah to guide your heart before decisions?
?
If every action ultimately requires a preponderator from Allah, what does that imply about the state of your heart right now? Are you actively seeking Allah to tip the scales toward obedience, or are you relying on your own equal power? How will you change your morning du'a and your preparation for the day in light of this?
?
This proof shows that even the disbeliever's act is under Allah's control. How does that shape your attitude toward those who oppose the truth? Does it lead you to despair or to hope? What one concrete step can you take today to trust more in Allah's power to change hearts, including your own?

A critique of the first proof
An editor's note critiques the first proof's assumption about the preponderator's source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a common objection to the first proof: that the preponderator could come from the human agent himself without infinite regress.

Core Idea
The proof's assumption that the preponderator cannot be from man is challenged by the possibility of self-determination.

In this lesson, we examine a marginal note attached to the first proof. The note criticizes the argument for assuming that the preponderator must be either from God or the servant, ignoring the possibility that the human agent himself can be the preponderator through his own will. Let's analyse this critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique
Read the short passage that criticizes the proof. It claims the proof is not conclusive because it assumed the preponderator is between God and man, but it could be from man himself.
This critique is from an editor, not the original author.
2
Understand the alternative
The critique suggests that man himself can preponderate between good and evil without needing an external cause.
This challenges the need for infinite regress.
3
Evaluate the objection
Consider whether this critique effectively refutes the proof or if the proof can be defended.
The original author is not present to respond.

Source Text

beginner

The Third Proof: Knowledge Requires Prior Knowledge
Al-Matalib al-'Aliyah vol.9 p.383 - Third rational proof on impossibility of acquiring knowledge without prior knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational proof that the acquisition of knowledge by the servant requires prior knowledge, leading to an infinite regress, thereby proving that all knowledge is directly created by God.

Core Idea
True knowledge of a thing requires prior knowledge, making self-acquisition impossible.

This lesson examines a powerful rational proof that challenges the notion that humans can acquire knowledge purely through their own effort. The author argues that before we can intentionally seek knowledge, we must already know that our belief corresponds to reality, which would require prior knowledge of the object itself. This leads to an infinite regress, demonstrating that initial knowledge must be granted directly by God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Third Proof Passage
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice the logical progression: the servant needs to know that a belief is knowledge, which requires knowing correspondence, which requires knowing the object.
2
Unpack the Conditional Chain
Work through the chain: If knowledge acquisition depends on prior knowledge, then the first knowledge would need prior knowledge ad infinitum.
3
Recognize the Impossibility
Understand why this infinite regress is impossible (it would mean no knowledge can ever be acquired).
4
Connect to Divine Creation
See how this proof supports the Ash'ari position that all knowledge is created by God, not acquired by human effort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث : العبد ما لم يعرف أن هذا الاعتقاد علم لا جهل ، يمكنه أن يقصد إلى إيجاد العلم بدلاً عن الجهل، وإنما يعلم كون هذا الاعتقاد علماً ، إذا علم أنه مطابق للمعلوم. وإنما يعلم ذلك إذا علم حال المعلوم . وإلا فيلزم أن تكون قدرته على تحصيل العلم بالشيء مشروط بحصول العلم بذلك الشيء . وإنه محال . وهذه الدلائل قد بيناها في الباب الأول من هذا الكتاب . فلا فائدة في الإعادة
The third proof: The servant, as long as he does not know that this belief is knowledge and not ignorance, he can intend to produce knowledge instead of ignorance. He only knows that this belief is knowledge when he knows that it corresponds to the known. He only knows that when he knows the state of the known. Otherwise, it would follow that his power to acquire knowledge of a thing is conditional upon having knowledge of that thing, and that is impossible. And we have explained these proofs in the first chapter of this book, so there is no benefit in repetition.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This is a third rational proof focusing on the acquisition of knowledge. The author argues that for the servant to intentionally acquire knowledge of something, he must first know that his belief corresponds to reality. To know that correspondence, he needs prior knowledge of the object. This leads to an infinite regress, making the acquisition of knowledge impossible through the servant's own power. Therefore, knowledge must be created directly by God. The author notes that these arguments were already detailed earlier in the book, so he does not repeat them at length.

Key Terms
الاعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that this proof denies the possibility of learning altogether, implying that humans can never acquire new knowledge.
Correction: The proof only denies that the very first knowledge can be acquired by the servant's own power. It does not deny subsequent learning, which builds on prior knowledge granted by God.

Checkpoint
What does the third proof argue is impossible?
A) The servant can never acquire knowledge
B) The servant can acquire knowledge through effort
C) The servant's power to acquire knowledge is conditional on prior knowledge, leading to infinite regress
D) Knowledge is always innate

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that the very act of intending to acquire knowledge already assumes prior knowledge, leading to an infinite regress which is impossible, so knowledge must be directly created by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn something about your faith, do you first humbly ask Allah to grant you understanding? Or do you rely solely on your own intellect? This proof shows that without prior knowledge from Allah, you cannot even recognize true knowledge. How does this challenge your approach to seeking religious knowledge? Will you start each study with a sincere dua for guidance?
?
Have you ever felt proud of your own understanding, thinking you arrived at it through your own effort? The third proof demonstrates that even the ability to know that your belief is true is a gift from Allah. Does this humble you? Write down one area where you need to acknowledge Allah's direct gift of knowledge in your life.
?
Consider the infinite regress argument: if you had to acquire knowledge by your own power, you would need to know something before you know it. This is impossible. So, how do you explain the fact that you do have true knowledge? Does this lead you to see Allah's direct creation in every moment of understanding? Pause and thank Him for the gift of knowledge you have right now.
